   - A. Current Form ADV in encoded format.

   - B. Current Form ADV in standardized format.

   - C. Current and historical Form ADV in encoded format.

   - D. Current and historical Form ADV in standardized format.

##### **KEY CONCEPTS**

###### **LO 93.a**

In addition to past fraudulent activity, items on Form ADV of which potential investors should be aware include: conflicts of interest the firm may have with investors, soft dollar arrangements, associations with broker/dealers, firms serving as custodians, registration under the ICA of 1940, the existence of a CCO, firms that are majority employee owned, investor size, firms with clients who are agents, and firms that manage hedge funds.

###### **LO 93.b**

In the past, cost considerations pertaining to fraud likely kept the emphasis on disclosure, but in the post-Bernie Madoff environment, more attention has been given to securities law enforcement. Strong fraud prediction methods would prevent some investors from earning a fraud risk premium. Fraud detection methods also suffer from a free-rider problem. The benefits of identifying investments with fraud risk are limited because of short sale restrictions.

###### **LO 93.c**

Providing historical and current Form ADVs in a standardized format has improved investors’ ability to use disclosed data to predict fraud.

##### **ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES**

##### **Module Quiz 93.1**

1. **B** In the U.S., investment advisors who manage more than $25 million are required to file Form ADV annually or whenever there is a material change. (LO 93.a)

2. **A** Firms that have more clients who are agents (e.g., pension fund manager who is the client but not a direct beneficiary of the funds) are more likely to engage in fraud. (LO 93.a)

3. **A** The smaller (larger) the firm’s investors, the more (less) likely fraud will occur. (LO 93.a)

4. **D** The costs of obtaining relevant data and estimating fraud risk may be lower than the benefits for investors as a whole. However, the costs may be too high for a single investor to bear, and even if the single investor were to bear it, the other investors who benefit would not likely contribute to the costs (i.e., free-rider problem). (LO 93.b)

5. **D** To improve transparency, the SEC has now made the current and historical Form ADVs available to investors in a user-friendly (standardized) format. (LO 93.c)

The following is a review of the Current Issues in Financial Markets principles designed to address the learning objectives set forth by GARP<sup>®</sup> . Cross-reference to GARP assigned reading—Board of Governors of the Federal Reserve System.

### **READING 94**

**REVIEW OF THE FEDERAL RESERVE’S SUPERVISION AND REGULATION OF SILICON VALLEY BANK**

###### **Study Session 15**

#### **EXAM FOCUS**

This topic focuses on the events leading up to the failure of Silicon Valley Bank (SVB) and the regulatory shortcomings observed. For the exam, understand the history behind how SVB rose from its founding in 1983 to disaster in 2023. From the perspective of supervision, focus on what happened between 2018 and 2021 that created a poor transition in regulatory burdens for SVB. There were many events that led up to the ultimate collapse of SVB. Be familiar with the bank’s deposit concentration, the type of deposits, the realities with their held-to-maturity (HTM) and available-for-sale (AFS) portfolios, their contingent funding efforts, and attempts to raise capital. With respect to governance and risk management, understand the three listed areas of deficiency, what happened with the firm’s chief risk officer (CRO), and why supervisors were too lax in their dealings with SVB. For their liquidity position, focus on the bank’s noted deficiencies, their 30-day liquidity buffer, the ways in which management began to address liquidity concerns, and the lack of timely regulatory response. Finally, be aware of management’s use of the net interest income (NII) model and the economic value of equity (EVE) model.

#### **MODULE 94.1: SVB BACKGROUND AND RISK EXPOSURES**

#### **Evolution of Silicon Valley Bank**

##### **LO 94.a: Describe the events leading up to the failure of Silicon Valley Bank.**

Headquartered in Santa Clara, CA, Silicon Valley Bank Financial Group (SVBFG) was founded in 1983. They were a financial services firm with primary operations in the United States. Their largest subsidiary was **Silicon Valley Bank (SVB)** , which was a California state-chartered bank under the supervision of the Federal Reserve. Their clients were primarily U.S.-based emerging growth or mature firms in the technology and life sciences sectors. SVB’s strategy involved attracting early-stage (or start-up) firms as clients and then retaining them as they grew through the industry lifecycle. Nearly half of all venture capitalists in the technology and life sciences sectors were clients of SVB.

From 1983 to 2019, SVB grew deposits gradually from $18 million to $58 billion. These deposits tripled to $209 billion by 2021. This rapid growth was fueled by clients who enjoyed liquidity events like IPOs, secondary offerings, SPACs, acquisitions, and other fundraising activities. From year-end 2018 to year-end 2021, SVB grew assets by 271% as compared to the banking sector’s average of 29%. This outsized deposit growth was due to them targeting start-up firms and venture capitalists as clients. These depositors put so much of their money at SVB that, as of 2022, roughly 94% of all SVB deposits were not insured by the Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation (FDIC).

SVB’s positive net deposit growth lulled them into a mindset that this trend would continue. However, in the second half of 2022, venture capital activity fell sharply due to declining investor risk appetites resulting from interest rate increases. This decline in VC activity caused SVB’s once stellar deposit growth rate to become negative (i.e., a net outflow of deposits). SVB customers were making withdrawal requests to fund their own business operations.

This sparked a significant issue because SVB had been investing deposits in long-term, HTM government securities or agency-issued mortgage-backed securities (MBSs). They were counting on their deposit growth from 2019 to 2021 to continue, and they were trying to boost short-term returns. It is important to understand that these long-dated MBSs were classified as HTM because SVB fully intended on not selling them until the fixed income securities matured. As of December 31, 2022, most of their HTM portfolio had a maturity of 10 years or longer and a weighted average duration of 6.2 years. HTM assets can be carried on a bank’s books at their historical cost, not at the current market value (i.e., mark-to-market). According to accounting conventions, if a portion of the HTM portfolio were sold to cover customer withdrawal requests, then the entire HTM portfolio would be reclassified as available-for-sale (AFS) and marked-to-market at a loss. Management did not want to do this. It should also be noted that SVB’s ratio of securities owned (i.e., AFS and HTM portfolios) to total assets was more than double that of the average large banking organization.

In 2023, SVB’s deposit outflows accelerated as their customer’s need for cash increased. Depositor patience was further reduced when the _Financial Times_ ran an article discussing SVB’s exceptionally high securities-to-assets ratio. On March 8, SVB announced a sale of $21 billion from its AFS portfolio, which realized an after-tax loss of $1.8 billion. On the same day, they disclosed plans for a $2.25 billion equity offering to buy them some time for their HTM portfolio to recover, and both Moody’s and S&P revealed a potential rating downgrade for SVB. Between social media and the reality that most of SVB’s client base were personally connected because they were in the same industry, depositor withdrawal requests jumped to $40 billion on March 9 alone. Management knew that they could not keep up with this pace of deposit outflows. In the evening of March 9, they communicated to banking supervisors that they expected outflow requests of $100 billion on March 10 and they did not have the capital to meet that level of demand. On the morning of March 10, the California Department of Financial Protection and Innovation (CDFPI) closed SVB and delegated the FDIC as the receiver.

#### **Supervisory Oversight**

**LO 94.b: Describe shortfalls and deficiencies in the Federal Reserve’s supervisory oversight of Silicon Valley Bank during the period that the bank transitioned from the Fed’s Regional Banking Organization (RBO) portfolio to its Large and Foreign Banking Organization (LFBO) portfolio.**

Banks are classified under one of two categories based on the size of their assets. A **regional banking organization (RBO)** has assets between $10 billion and $100 billion, while a **large and foreign banking organization (LFBO)** either has assets exceeding $100 billion or it is a foreign bank operating in the United States. Both RBOs and LFBOs are supervised by regional Reserve Banks with some level of oversight by the Board of Governors of the Federal Reserve System.

Supervision of RBOs involves a plan to address any identified concerns. The plan should specifically include financial and managerial strengths along with emerging risks. Supervisors then use the _Bank Exams Tailored to Risk (BETR) program_ to shift examination hours from low-risk banks to high-risk banks. Risk reviews are periodically scheduled to consider unique risks associated with a given bank. Continuous monitoring involves regular meetings with senior bank leadership, review of key internal management reports, leveraging control functions (e.g., internal loan reviews, internal audits), and coordination with other regulators.

Supervision of LFBOs involves more depth and breadth. Horizontal exams are used to compare one bank against its peers relative to capital, liquidity, and cybersecurity. On an annual basis, the Federal Reserve rates these banks according to the _large financial institution (LFI) rating system_ , which considers capital planning, liquidity and risk management, and governance controls. Continuous monitoring for LFBOs requires a current understanding of the bank’s risk management practices. It is more in depth than the continuous monitoring used for RBOs because it is intended to function as an

early warning system before risk factors escalate. Through this more stringent process, larger banks are subject to _enhanced prudential standards (EPS)_ .

The Division of Supervision and Regulation (DSR) reports to the Board of Governors and performs a pivotal role in banking sector risk detection. The DSR is required to provide an annual assessment letter to Reserve Bank presidents detailing the status of all banks, in aggregate, within the regional Reserve Bank’s purview. The available ratings associated with this letter are “strong,” “effective,” “marginally effective,” and “requires improvement.” SVB’s relevant regulator was the Federal Reserve Bank of San Francisco (FRBSF). Figure 94.1 shows the ratings for the aggregate portfolio of banks under the watch of FRBSF between 2019 and 2022.

**Figure 94.1: Ratings Issued to FRBSF by the DSR**

*[image omitted]*

In July 2018, the Board of Governors raised the threshold for classification as LFBO from $50 billion in assets to $100 billion. Had the threshold been left at $50 billion, then SVB would have shifted into LFBO status in 2018. This shift delayed the supervision change for SVB to February 2021. The transition was not managed well. Complicating matters was an examination pause during the COVID-19 pandemic. SVB staff described the shift to LFBO as a cliff event. One day they were treated as RBO and the next day it was LFBO without a gradual upshift. It would have been far better to have begun the transition as the $100 billion threshold was approaching. Regulators were hesitant to pull forward more stringent EPS requirements until they were certain that SVB would achieve LFBO status. In February 2021, the RBO supervision cycle was well underway. It did not conclude until July 2021. Regulators agreed to allow the RBO supervision cycle to conclude and then the LFBO team would begin their work. SVB had to very quickly acquire the skills, processes, and teams to meet the EPS demands of the LFBO supervisors.

Some changes were also occurring with the regulatory system. From 2016 to 2022, banking sector assets grew by 37% while the headcount of regulatory supervisors declined by 3%. In 2021, a culture change began at the DSR. The vice chair for supervision looked to reduce regulatory burdens on RBO banks, meet a higher burden of proof on supervisory conclusions, and demonstrate due process related to recommended actions. The result was a delay in actions from supervisory staff and a reluctance to escalate potential concerns. When SVB was labeled as RBO, regulators allocated few resources to the bank, but this changed rapidly when they shifted to LFBO status.

When SVB was moved into the LFBO portfolio, regulatory examinations covered key EPS areas like risk management, liquidity, and governance. Before 2021, risk management was rated as “Satisfactory-2” but after the shift to the LFBO standards,

they were downgrade to “Less than Satisfactory-3.” Liquidity followed a similar story. Before 2021, it was rated as “Strong-1” but after the switch it was downgraded to “Satisfactory-2.” Governance was first checked in the August 2022 review (which began in late 2021), and it was rated as “Deficient-1.” When a deficiency is noted, a _memorandum of understanding (MOU)_ is issued to denote an informal enforcement action. This process began in August 2022, but there are too many stakeholders that need to review the MOU before it becomes official. It needed to go through the FRBSF, the DSR, The Board of Governor’s legal department, and the California Department of Financial Protection and Innovation. By the time the MOU made it through this process, SVB had already failed.

#### **SVB Risk Exposures**

**LO 94.c: Identify Silicon Valley Bank’s specific risk issues which led to and accelerated its failure including deposit concentration, type of deposits, held-tomaturity securities, available-for-sale securities, the bank’s contingent funding plan and capacity, and its capital raising efforts.**

There were three critical weaknesses noted in SVB’s failure. They involved governance and risk management, interest rate risk as it related to their portfolio, and liquidity risk management. The multiplier was the banks’ rapid growth. SVB’s board of directors did not have the expertise to lead a large bank with over $200 billion in assets. They were focused on short-term measures of profitability without fully understanding their longer-term risk exposures. They were also slow to address the rapid changes in risk management. Supervision from the Federal Reserve failed to keep pace with the rate of change SVB was experiencing. Specific risks for SVB could be grouped into the following categories:

- _Deposit concentration_ . SVB’s depositor base was highly concentrated in start-ups, venture capitalists, and private equity firms in the technology and life science sectors. Due to industry concentration, they all experienced business issues at the same time and demand for withdrawals (i.e., deposit outflows at SVB) accelerated all at once.

- _Type of deposits_ . Deposits were large and 94% of deposits were uninsured (i.e., above the FDIC coverage limit). Initially, the deposits were noninterest bearing, but in 2022 they switched and became interest bearing. This changed the duration of the bank’s liabilities and caused liquidity stress testing results to worsen.

- _Held-to-maturity (HTM) securities_ . The HTM portfolio was largely invested in longdated MBSs. Most of these securities had a maturity of 10 years or longer and a weighted average duration of 6.2 years. Management removed hedges against rising rates in order to focus on short-term profitability. When rates continued to rise, the losses in the HTM portfolio became substantial. HTM assets are carried on the books at historical cost, which kept the true losses out of the public eye. At one point, accounting rules dictated that selling a portion of HTM assets to cover capital needs would require the entire HTM portfolio to be reclassified as AFS. This would mean an instant shift from historical cost accounting to mark-to-market, which would

recognize substantial losses and spook investors and depositors. By the end of 2022, this rule was relaxed, and management was considering a one-time transfer of funds out of the HTM portfolio, which now would not have required a complete reclassification as AFS.

- _Available-for-sale (AFS) securities_ . Assets classified as AFS are marked-to-market, which means that the balance sheet will reflect a timelier perspective of the value of the bank’s liabilities. In late 2022, management considered selling some AFS assets to cover deposit demands. The losses would have been sizable because the bank had invested in long-dated fixed income securities before the interest rate increase cycle.

- _Contingent funding and capacity plans_ . SVB management did not have sufficient plans in place to access capital from contingent sources. They needed to expand repo funding and bilateral relationships. They began to address this in the fourth quarter of 2022, but by that time it was too late.

- _Capital raising_ . SVB management attempted to offer promotions to retain deposits, but this did not work. They considered selling HTM assets, but the losses were substantial and could not fully support the pace of withdrawal requests.

###### **MODULE QUIZ 94.1**

1. Silicon Valley Bank (SVB) was shut down in 2023 because:

   - A. the Federal Reserve raised interest rates too high.

   - B. foreign depositors withdrew all of their money at the same time.

   - C. they did not align their assets with potential customer withdrawal needs.

   - D. accounting conventions did not allow SVB to properly adjust to meet depositor withdrawal requests.

2. From the perspective of regulation, what was the issue that caused a delay in SVB receiving elevated regulatory scrutiny?

   - A. SVB hid assets from regulators.

   - B. The regulatory culture became more intense.

   - C. The asset threshold change for being considered LFBO.

   - D. Held-to-maturity assets were no longer involved in regulatory reviews.

3. Which of the following items was a factor that contributed to the failure of SVB?

   - A. Short duration on HTM securities.

   - B. Liquidity downgrades from regulators.

   - C. Concentration of depositors in two industries.

   - D. Realized losses from transferring assets from HTM to AFS.

#### **MODULE 94.2: SVB MANAGEMENT DEFICIENCIES AND FAILURES**

#### **SVB Governance and Risk Management**

**LO 94.d: Identify and describe the failures and shortfalls of Silicon Valley Bank in the areas of governance and risk management including those related to the CRO**

**position and the bank’s internal audit function.**

Corporate governance is the system of rules, processes, and procedures that enables a firm to navigate economic and operational uncertainty. The end goal of effective governance is strong risk management. SVB’s governance and risk management functions were lacking when they reached $50 billion in assets, and they were deficient at the end of an unexpected growth cycle resulting in over $200 billion in assets.

When SVB was below the $100 billion asset mark (i.e., still in RBO status), their CAMELS (capital adequacy, asset quality, management, earnings, liquidity, and sensitivity to market risk) rating was listed as “Satisfactory-2.” This happened in 2018, 2019, and 2020. In the review for the 2020 supervisory cycle (dated May 3, 2021), they were listed as “Satisfactory-2” despite several noted issues. Supervisors saw issues with credit risk management, internal loan reviews, and technology weaknesses. They also noted that management was reactive to issues identified by supervisors rather than proactively looking for risk factors independently. Yet, the supervisors still maintained a rating of effective (i.e., “Satisfactory-2”) because they were focused on the bank’s strong financial performance up to this point.

In late 2021, the LFBO supervisory team had their first look at SVB’s governance and risk management structure. Meetings with management confirmed that they were not equipped to handle the rapid growth that SVB was enjoying. In conjunction with the DSR office, the LFBO supervisors waived a governance rating for 2021 to allow them time to gather more information (i.e., due diligence) to support their rating.

In a report issued on May 31, 2022, the supervisors itemized the following three _matters requiring immediate attention (MRIAs)_ :

1. _Board effectiveness_ . The board, management, and the CRO did not adequately shift from RBO standards to the more rigorous EPS requirements associated with larger banks (i.e., LFBOs). They hired consultants to help in 2020, but that still did not accomplish the goal. For these reasons, the supervisors found that the board (and management) did not have the necessary experience to run a large financial institution.

2. _Risk management programs_ . The risk management system was found to be ineffective. Some specific deficiencies included a lack of a formal risk appetite standard, no mechanism for tracking or reporting root causes of risks, a lack of enterprise-wide policies and procedures, no enterprise-wide internal control framework, an incentive structure whose rewards were not linked to risk management outcomes, and an underdeveloped talent acquisition and performance review process.

3. _Internal audit effectiveness_ . The internal audit (IA) function did not have a methodology that was sufficient to challenge management. They did not provide the audit committee with timely and sufficient reporting, and they did not ensure timely analysis of critical risk management functions.

The CRO recognized that she did not have the skillset needed to navigate SVB through the growing pains of becoming a large bank. This individual left the firm in April 2022.

Supervisors did not raise any concerns that the bank did not have a CRO because they were actively searching for one. This follows the pattern in which the supervisors were not citing issues either because the bank was highly profitable or because they were working toward a solution. While the CRO search was ongoing, risk management was supervised by a team of internal senior risk managers who were all very new to their roles. A new CRO was finally hired in December 2022.

#### **SVB Liquidity Risk Management Deficiencies**

**LO 94.e: Identify the scope of Silicon Valley Bank’s liquidity risk management deficiencies and shortfalls, including its modeling and stress testing of its 30-day liquidity buffer, as well as the actions that management and regulators considered to address these specific liquidity issues.**

Liquidity is a financial institution’s ability to meet its cash and collateral obligations at a reasonable cost. Liquidity risk is a natural part of the banking relationship because they use depositor funds, which are short-term in nature, to lend over longer periods of time. This so-called _maturity transformation_ is an expected part of the business.

Regulation focuses on strong liquidity management practices including board oversight, liquidity risk tolerance parameters, internal liquidity stress testing, and contingency funding plans. SVB’s liquidity risk management practices were fundamentally flawed and not suited for a firm of their size. This was partly due to their rapid growth from 2019 to 2021. Regulatory supervisors did not adequately identify the changes in SVB’s risk profile.

When SVB was still in the RBO portfolio (i.e., assets less than $100 billion), supervisors issued consistently positive reviews of their liquidity risk management practices. Their balance sheet was growing and heavily skewed toward large and uninsured deposits from VC-backed and private equity clients. Liquidity risk appeared to be minimal given the high growth in depositor assets and a large portfolio of low-credit risk securities in the HTM and AFS portfolios. The low perception of risk led the supervisors to use the RBO “risk-focused guidelines” to conduct very light reviews of SVB’s liquidity risk, which was clearly underestimated. In low-risk scenarios, a single supervisory staff member would often cover multiple risk centers (e.g., liquidity risk, interest rate risk, etc.).

A much more thorough review occurred when SVB was transitioned to the LFBO portfolio. Supervisors found weaknesses across all areas reviewed. They cited the following six specific issues:

1. SVB needed to develop a plan to address shortfalls in liquidity stress testing and contingency funding.

2. The internal audit function needed a redesign to provide sufficient risk management oversight.

3. Liquidity stress testing needed to use more realistic assumptions and reflect immediate stress testing rather than the expected evolution over time.

4. Stress testing with respect to deposits did not incorporate realistic risks.

5. Liquidity risk limits and the associated processes were not keeping pace with the increase in size that SVB was enjoying.

6. SVB did not adequately assess potential funding sources.

The result was a liquidity rating of “Conditionally Meets Expectations.” Supervisors were again relying on the cash reserves and securities held on SVB’s balance sheet to create a buffer for weak risk controls. The cited six weaknesses were likely more in line with a deficient rating rather than the one provided.

A timeline of the awareness of the deterioration in SVB’s liquidity was as follows:

- _2022 Q2_ : SVB’s concentrated client base began withdrawing deposits to meet their business needs. The bank began offering promotional deals to retain deposits with limited effectiveness.

- _2022 Q3_ : In response to the LFBO review’s six identified issues, SVB began to update their liquidity stress testing process. As of August 21, 2022, they reviewed their 30day liquidity buffer and modeled it to be a shortfall of $18 billion. Additionally, they modeled a 90-day liquidity shortfall of $23 billion. They incorrectly treated these as an “operational shortfall” when they should have been taken more seriously. Combining their internal findings of deficiency in their 30-day liquidity buffer and their 90-day liquidity buffer, these modeled shortfalls represented a material safetyand-soundness concern. Despite the negative modeled 30-day liquidity buffer, supervisors did not materially change their risk ratings.

- _2022 Q4_ : Management began to recognize the problems. The board of directors was informed of the details in their November 2022 strategy meeting. They still continued to consider the modeled 30-day liquidity shortfall as “operational” rather than as a violation of EPS standards, which it was. Management made several attempts to address the liquidity issues including increasing Federal Home Loan Bank advances, increasing their repurchase agreement capacity, and changing the assumptions used in the 30-day liquidity shortfall modeling. This last option decreased the shortfall by $5 billion, but assumptions should never be relaxed to produce a more favorable outcome. This does not change reality. Supervisors were aware of the change in liquidity stress testing assumptions, but they planned to delay their comments until the 2023 review cycle.

- _2023_ : Supervisors conducted a horizontal review of SVB relative to peer banks. Their preliminary finding was that SVB was not meeting expectations. One serious concern was SVB’s insufficiently supported deposit outflow speed assumption. Management shifted their assumption to outflows not occurring until Days 31–90. SVB’s management made key assumptions (e.g., lending commitments and intraday assumptions) less conservative. It was also noted that management needed to make serious improvements to expand capacity through repo funding and bilateral relationships.

Supervisors did not adequately rate SVB’s risks or keep pace with the rate of deterioration in their processes and models. They primarily focused comments on SVB’s liquidity risk management practices rather than on the liquidity positions

themselves. Supervisors had limited interaction with SVB during the beginning of 2023. This changed on March 8 and March 9 when the music stopped for the bank.

#### **SVB Interest Rate Risk Management Deficiencies**

**LO 94.f: Describe the deficiencies in Silicon Valley Bank’s interest rate risk management process, including its modelling process, and explain how proper use of metrics such as net interest income (NII) at risk and economic value of equity (EVE) could have improved its management of interest rate risk.**

Interest rate risk is inherent in the banking industry, but banks need to have adequate risk management practices to control for associated risks. There are two key metrics used to model interest rate risk and they both use deposit assumptions to model expected cash flow needs of the bank. These metrics are as follows:

- _Net interest income at risk_ . **Net interest income (NII)** measures potential volatility over short periods of time. It tests the effect of large basis point moves in the yield curve and potentially incorporates yield curve twists. The goal is to quantify potential short-term cash flow impacts. This involves _deposit betas_ , which are the assumed pass-through of market movement to deposit accounts.

- _Economic value of equity_ . The **economic value of equity (EVE)** is the present value of assets minus the present value of liabilities. It measures the potential structural mismatch on the bank’s balance sheet with the goal of estimating the longer-term impact of interest rate risk on the bank’s securities.

SVB’s risk appetite statement (RAS) only included NII and ignored EVE. They estimated that NII would increase during a rising rate environment because they failed to consider the impact of rising rates on their customer base (i.e., they assumed low deposit betas). Only a limited number of interest rate changes were tested. They did not conduct backtesting and only had limited sensitivity testing.

Management focused almost exclusive on the NII measurement. When they saw the yield curve invert, they anticipated a recession and falling, not rising, rates. They began to position their balance sheet for a falling rate cycle, which never arrived. Beginning in March 2022 and finalizing in July 2022, management removed their hedges against rising rates because they would have hurt profits if rates fell. This action lengthened the duration of the bank’s securities, which harmed the EVE metric. At the same time, the bank’s deposits were shifting from noninterest bearing to interest bearing. This altered the duration of the bank’s liabilities (i.e., the deposits) and made the EVE even worse.

While EVE was excluded from the RAS, it was included in the review of the Risk Committee on the board of directors. Still, the full board was not aware of the deterioration the EVE would have shown. The Asset/Liability Committee (ALCO) only considered parallel shifts in the yield curve while ignoring nonparallel shifts that would have indicated trouble brewing. Additionally, management altered the assumed withdrawal rate for deposits and implemented other unsubstantiated assumption changes, which made the EVE outcomes look more favorable. This did not alter economic reality. It only made things appear better than they really were.

From 2018 to 2022, the supervisor’s CAMELS rating for market risk was “Satisfactory2.” The 2022 CAMELS review cycle was vetted in November 2022. The planned new market risk rating was downgraded to “Less than Satisfactory-3.” This review was not officially finalized until after SVB had already failed.

###### **MODULE QUIZ 94.2**

1. The governance and risk management function at SVB was:

   - A. supervised by a very qualified board of directors.

   - B. not regulated properly due to political connections.

   - C. supervised by a very qualified chief risk officer (CRO).

   - D. not able to keep up with the rate of growth the bank was enjoying.

2. SVB management attempted to solve their modeled 30-day liquidity buffer shortfall by:

   - A. selling AFS holdings at a loss.

   - B. selling HTM holdings at a loss.

   - C. borrowing money through the discount window.

   - D. changing assumptions to make them more favorable.

3. What action did SVB’s management take that made their economic value of equity (EVE) model results much worse?

   - A. They shortened the duration of deposits.

   - B. They removed hedges against falling rates.

   - C. They removed hedges against rising rates.

   - D. They brought EVE deterioration to the board’s attention.

##### **KEY CONCEPTS**

###### **LO 94.a**

Silicon Valley Bank (SVB) was founded in 1983. They targeted start-up firms in the tech and life sciences sectors. The bank grew gradually until 2019. However, deposits tripled from 2019 to 2021. The bank expected this rate of depositor growth to continue (or at least stay positive). They chased short-term returns and invested a substantial portion of investor deposits in long-dated mortgage-backed securities. When the Fed began its interest rate increase cycle in 2022, these assets lost considerable value. This would not have been a huge problem had SVB’s customers not also begun to demand withdrawals to fund cash needs in their businesses. The withdrawal demands increased to a point where SVB could not meet the requests and they were shut down by regulators and turned over to the FDIC.

###### **LO 94.b**

Banks with assets between $10 billion and $100 billion are classified as regional banking organizations (RBOs). Banks with assets over $100 billion (or a foreign bank operating in the United States) are classified as large and foreign banking organizations (LFBOs). Due to SVB’s rapid deposit growth, they crossed from RBO to LFBO. However, a regulatory change altered the threshold to $100 billion from the previous $50 billion in 2021. This change delayed the update in status by three years. RBO banks are still supervised, but there is a much lower burden of analysis. LFBOs are monitored much

more heavily. The regulatory transition from RBO to LFBO was not managed well by the Federal Reserve. In 2021, there was also a culture change in the Division of Supervision and Regulation that lessened the regulatory burden for RBO banks. This widened the gap between what was being reviewed when SVB had less than $100 billion in assets to when it was reclassified as LFBO.

###### **LO 94.c**

Several factors contributed to the failure of SVB. They include the high concentration of depositors (i.e., primarily two industries and the same overall subset of clients), the type of deposits (i.e., large and mostly uninsured deposits), the heavy losses in their HTM and AFS portfolios due to poor interest rate risk management, their lack of contingency funding plans, and their difficulty raising capital.

###### **LO 94.d**

SVB struggled with their governance and risk management policies. Many of the policies were lacking or nonexistent. The board, management, and the chief risk officer (CRO) were not equipped to navigate the bank through the rapid growth they were experiencing. LFBO status had much more stringent standards, and the board was simply not prepared. The CRO recognized her experience shortfall and stepped down in April 2022. The bank did not find a replacement until December 2022. While they were searching, risk management was led by a group of senior risk managers who were also underqualified for the role. Supervisors cut the bank some slack because they were very profitable, and they were working on some of the needed changes. In hindsight, this lax supervision was an incorrect decision.

###### **LO 94.e**

SVB had several notable liquidity concerns. When they were still in the RBO portfolio, regulators did not address these issues using the “risk-focused guidelines” to allow their deposit growth and the size of their security portfolio to outweigh the observed risks in processes and positions. Shifting to the LFBO portfolio brought a more in-depth review, with six noted issues. One of which was a 30-day liquidity buffer with an $18 billion shortfall. Once management accepted the reality of the issues, they began to expand capacity and contingency planning. They also changed the assumptions used in their 30-day liquidity buffer models to make things appear more favorable. Supervisors chose to wait for the 2023 review cycle to comment on these new developments.

###### **LO 94.f**

It is commonly understood that as interest rates rise, bond prices fall, and longer-dated bonds fall more than shorter-term ones. SVB had a few issues with respect to interest rate risk management. First, management chose to focus on the short-term-focused net interest income (NII) model rather than the longer-term economic value of equity (EVE) model. In other words, they prioritized short-term profitability over long-term solvency. Second, they assumed, as many did, that the inverted yield curve was a harbinger of a recession. This led them to anticipate falling not rising rates. They removed hedges to protect against rising rates to boost expected short-term profits. They also changed some assumptions related to deposits in order to make the interest

rate risk stress testing look more favorable. Supervisors delayed their rating downgrades until it was too late.

##### **ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES**

##### **Module Quiz 94.1**

1. **C** SVB was shut down in 2023 because they invested depositor money into longdated mortgage-backed securities and failed to adequately anticipate customer withdrawal requests. The Federal Reserve’s interest rate increase cycle compounded the problem, but it did not cause it. There are accounting conventions that cause management to not want to sell portions of their held-tomaturity portfolio, but that was management’s choice in conjunction with their previous bad decisions to focus on high duration assets. Withdrawal requests came from a consortium of depositors and most of SVB’s clients were U.S. based. (LO 94.a)

2. **C** The core issue that caused a delay in SVB receiving a higher level of regulatory scrutiny was the change in 2018 that altered the threshold from $50 billion to $100 billion in assets for shifting from RBO to LFBO. This shift happened in 2021 for SVB. At the same time, the vice chair for supervision changed the regulatory culture to lessen the burden on RBO banks. This meant that SVB was shifting from a more laxed RBO process to the more stringent LFBO process all at once rather than gradually over a multiyear transition period. (LO 94.b)

3. **C** Silicon Valley Bank failed for many reasons. These reasons included deposit contraction, long durations on HTM securities, massive losses on HTM assets, which were not sold or transferred to AFS, and delays in regulatory rating downgrades. (LO 94.c)

##### **Module Quiz 94.2**

1. **D** The governance and risk management function at SVB was not able to keep up with the rate of growth that the bank was enjoying. Broadly speaking, neither the board nor the CRO had the requisite skillset to adequately manage risk for a large financial institution. Regulators cut them slack because the bank was profitable (i.e., no risks were materializing yet) and they were working toward some solutions. (LO 94.d)

2. **D** SVB’s modeled 30-day liquidity buffer issues were very real. They amounted to a modeled $18 billion shortfall in the fourth quarter of 2022. Rather than raise capital or sell securities at a substantial loss, management chose to relax the assumptions used. This reduced the modeled shortfall by $5 billion but did nothing to alter reality. (LO 94.e)

3. **C** Management made the decision to remove hedges against rising rates. This protected short-term profitability measures, but it made the losses in the HTM and AFS portfolios more pronounced. Their EVE metric was harmed by removing

the hedges against rising rates. At the same time, they lengthened the assumption on the duration of deposits to make EVE look better than it really was. (LO 94.f)

### **READING 95**

## **THE CREDIT SUISSE COCO WIPEOUT: FACTS, MISPERCEPTIONS, AND LESSONS FOR FINANCIAL REGULATION**

###### **Study Session 15**

#### **EXAM FOCUS**

In this topic, we examine contingent convertible bonds (CoCos), which have been popular instruments to help recapitalize and deleverage banks in distress. CoCos have grown in popularity and are favored by large institutional players. For the exam, understand CoCo terminology and key design features, including the different triggers and the loss absorption mechanism, and why CoCos provide important protections to struggling financial institutions. Also, be able to contrast the rescue of Credit Suisse by Swiss regulators in 2023 with the rescue of Bear Stearns by U.S. regulators in 2008, and be able to identify the rationales for the write-down of the Credit Suisse CoCos. Finally, be able to explain why the write-down of the Credit Suisse CoCos has created significant market challenges and negative investor reaction, and be familiar with the characteristics proposed to reform the CoCo structure.

#### **MODULE 95.1: CONTINGENT CONVERTIBLE BONDS**

**LO 95.a: Describe the features and mechanics of contingent convertible bonds (CoCos) and explain the rationale for banks to issue them.**

**Contingent convertible bonds (CoCos)** are a type of fixed income security that helps stabilize the distress of an issuer by offering immediate recapitalization. CoCos were borne out of the distresses of the global financial crisis of 2007–2009 in order to limit systemic risk. They are a form of convertible bonds where the conversion can happen automatically if a trigger event occurs. For example, if a bank’s core Tier 1 regulatory capital ratio falls below the minimum regulatory requirement. The conversion infuses the issuer with equity and reduces the need for capital injection by a government during a crisis. CoCos are often used to recapitalize _going concern_ banks, that is, banks

that expect to remain viable following the CoCo write-down (by contrast, a _gone concern_ bank is one in liquidation or bankruptcy).

CoCos are similar in some respect to _total loss absorbing capacity (TLAC) bonds_ because they both protect equity and minimize losses. However, TLAC bonds are designed to ensure that the holding companies of global systemically important banks (GSIBs) have sufficient equity to absorb losses and allow their operating affiliates to continue operations during a resolution process and not allow their liabilities to be at risk.

#### **CoCo Mechanisms**

CoCos have two key features: (1) the trigger that activates them, and (2) the loss absorption mechanism. These design features are illustrated in Figure 95.1. There can be multiple triggers that can be either _mechanical_ , which are predefined based on the capital ratio, or _discretionary_ (also called point of nonviability, or PONV), which is based on the judgment of a supervisory body or regulator if the bank’s capital has weakened sufficiently to the point that it requires recapitalization.

The mechanical trigger is activated when the bank’s capital (either based on book or market value) drops below a certain percentage of risk-weighted assets (RWAs). The discretionary trigger is activated based on the supervisor’s view of the bank’s insolvency. Basel III rules require that all regulatory capital CoCos have a discretionary (PONV) trigger.

When any of the triggers is breached, the loss absorption mechanism is activated. CoCos absorb losses either through a _conversion to equity_ at a predefined conversion rate, or through a _write-down of principal_ . The equity conversion can be specified based on the market price of the stock, or based on a predetermined price, or a combination of the two where the predetermined price acts as the floor. If the mechanism is a conversion to equity, the existing shareholders’ position is diluted, who therefore have an incentive to avoid conversion. The principal write-down can be partial or full.

**Figure 95.1: CoCo Features**

*[image omitted]*

Source: Avdjiev, Stefan, Bilyana Bogdanova, and Anastasia Kartasheva. 2013. “CoCos: A Primer.” _BIS Quarterly Review_ , September 2013, 43–56.

For CoCos to qualify as regulatory capital under Basel III, they must meet two criteria: (1) include a discretionary (PONV) trigger, and (2) have a minimum regulatory capitalto-risk-weighted assets ratio (i.e., the going concern rule). This is meant to protect capital levels and therefore improve a bank’s resilience to external shocks. Overall, CoCos are designed to allow banks to easily recapitalize during financial stress without requiring external intervention (e.g., a bailout).

#### **The CoCo Market**

CoCos offer investors higher yields than traditional bonds. They have a lower priority of claim and will typically be attractive to investors with a long time horizon who are looking for a yield premium. For example, a 2013 study assessed CoCos to have average yield-to-maturities that were 2.8% above subordinated bonds and 4.7% above senior unsecured bonds of the same bank.

The CoCo market has grown significantly over the last two decades. Based on a 2020 analysis, there have been over 700 CoCo issuances raising around $500 billion between 2009 and 2015. Over half of CoCos include a mechanical trigger with a principal writedown. The majority of CoCos up until 2015 were Additional Tier 1 (AT1) capital instruments issued by developed market banks.

CoCos are typically favored by well-capitalized, larger banks, for three reasons. First, because these banks are healthy, the risk of conversion is low, providing a yield benefit to the issuing bank. Second, the senior bondholders of the bank benefit from CoCo issuances given the risk-reduction benefits they offer. Third, the issuance of CoCos has historically not impacted the issuing bank’s share price negatively; in fact, share prices often rose. However, this gives rise to a moral hazard problem because it can lead shareholders to take excessive risks favoring write-down CoCos because losses would be absorbed by the investors in the write-down CoCos, and not by shareholders.

###### **MODULE QUIZ 95.1**

1. A bank’s contingent convertible bond (CoCo) has a point of nonviability (PONV) trigger. The PONV trigger would be activated:

   - A. at the discretion of the issuing bank if its capital weakened significantly.

   - B. automatically if the bank’s liquidity balances fell below a specified value.

   - C. automatically if the bank’s Tier 1 capital ratio fell below a specified value.

   - D. at the discretion of a regulator if the conversion would avoid the bank’s nonviability.

2. Which of the following contingent convertible instruments would most likely qualify as regulatory capital under Basel III rules?

   - A. Bond 1 has a mechanical trigger based on book value, and a maximum regulatory capital-to-risk-weighted assets ratio.

   - B. Bond 2 has a mechanical trigger based on market value, and a minimum regulatory capital-to-risk-weighted assets ratio.

   - C. Bond 3 has a discretionary (point of nonviability, or PONV) trigger, and a minimum regulatory capital-to-risk-weighted assets ratio.

- D. Bond 4 has a discretionary (PONV) trigger, and a maximum regulatory capital-torisk-weighted assets ratio.

#### **MODULE 95.2: CREDIT SUISSE COCOS**

**LO 95.b: Explain the rescue of Credit Suisse by Swiss regulators in 2023 and compare it to the rescue of Bear Stearns by U.S. regulators during the financial crisis in 2008.**

#### **2008 Bear Stearns Rescue**

The March 2008 collapse of U.S. investment bank Bear Stearns was one of the key events of the global financial crisis of 2007–2009. The bank suffered heavy losses mainly due to its exposure to securitized investments that were backed by subprime mortgages. The bank’s troubles forced the U.S. Federal Reserve and the U.S. Treasury to rescue the bank by facilitating a merger with JPMorgan. Given that both the Federal Reserve and the Treasury had limited powers to force a merger, the Federal Reserve needed to invoke a rarely used section of the Federal Reserve Act (section 13(3)), which allowed it to provide liquidity support to an institution but only under unusual and urgent circumstances.

As part of its bailout arrangement, the Federal Reserve persuaded JPMorgan to buy all Bear Stearns shares at an initially proposed price of $2/share, subject to shareholder approval, and assume all Bear Stearns liabilities. The Federal Reserve then set up a collateralized special purpose vehicle (SPV) jointly with JPMorgan, financed with a $1 billion junior tranche by JPMorgan and a $29 billion senior tranche by the Federal Reserve, and used the SPV to buy up to $30 billion of troubled Bear Stearns assets. The Federal Reserve subsequently had to make concessions to Bear Stearns shareholders by raising the share price to $10/share. The deal was finalized in May 2008.

#### **2023 Credit Suisse Rescue**

Fifteen years following the events of Bear Stearns, U.S.-based Silicon Valley Bank (SVB) collapsed in March 2023, which created systemic risk and contagion in the global banking system. The collapse destabilized banks with spillover into Europe, creating significant liquidity problems for the Swiss banking giant Credit Suisse. Credit Suisse’s liquidity problem was so large that it was forced to request up to CHF50 billion in liquidity support from the Swiss National Bank, Switzerland’s central bank. The Swiss central bank and the Swiss Financial Market Supervisory Authority (FINMA) then helped organize a takeover of Credit Suisse by the Swiss investment bank UBS. As part of the arrangement, nearly CHF16 billion in Credit Suisse CoCos had been fully written down, helping to recapitalize the bank’s balance sheet.

**LO 95.c: Explain the rationale for the write-down of Credit Suisse CoCos that was engineered by regulators during the rescue of Credit Suisse and its takeover by**

##### **UBS.**

In contrast to the more limited authority of the U.S. government, the Swiss federal government had significantly more authority under Swiss emergency law. Specifically, the Swiss federal government had the authority to trigger the write-down of the CoCos, thereby avoiding the more complex route of setting up an SPV. The CoCo bond documents gave FINMA the authority to trigger a conversion to equity if Credit Suisse reached the PONV. As a result, when FINMA triggered the conversion and helped recapitalize Credit Suisse, the CoCos functioned exactly as they were intended.

The write-down of the CoCos (AT1 instruments) in March 2023 helped to quickly deleverage the Credit Suisse balance sheet. Further, the intervention of the Swiss government did not involve a complete bailout of creditors. This helped alleviate market concern that credit bailouts create a moral hazard problem by encouraging more risk taking by large financial institutions that reasonably expect they would be bailed out when facing significant distress.

**LO 95.d: Describe the reactions by market participants to the write-down of the CoCos, and explain and evaluate different arguments and lessons learned related to the decision to write down the CoCos.**

#### **Market Reaction**

The complete write-down of the Credit Suisse CoCo bonds, without any material adverse impact on shareholders, caught many investors by surprise and caused significant negative market reaction. Based on an analysis of Credit Suisse’s credit default swap (CDS) bond spreads, the market did not anticipate a write-down of the AT1 until the day before the takeover. Bondholders and many market participants viewed the events as a subversion of the traditional creditor hierarchy, under which bondholders have a more senior claim than shareholders in a distress scenario. While investors understood that CoCos were riskier than traditional bonds—as evidenced by their higher yields—they likely did not fully understand the risks, including that writedown CoCos are, in essence, junior in claims to equity.

The adverse reaction was significant enough that it caused several non-Swiss European regulatory bodies to publicly distance themselves from the action of the Swiss government, in part to avoid contagion to the $250 billion European AT1 market. For example, shortly following the FINMA decision, the EU’s Single Resolution Board, the European Banking Authority (EBA), and the European Central Bank (ECB) issued a statement explicitly stating that in the event of a banking crisis, the regulatory intervention would lead to common shareholders absorbing losses first, and only then followed by a write-down of AT1 capital.

Further, both the adverse market reaction and the statements by the non-Swiss regulatory bodies incentivized several lawsuits against Credit Suisse, in large part arguing that the write-down of CoCos contradicted generally accepted creditor hierarchy, and that FINMA did not act in good faith in its intervention.

#### **Lessons Learned**

The key lesson from the Credit Suisse CoCo write-down was that despite a strong argument that the CoCo write-down worked exactly as it was intended, it created significant uncertainty and confusion, even among experienced investors. Many investors were unaware of the difference between going concern and gone concern CoCos, and were also unaware that these CoCos included multiple triggers: an automatic trigger when the common equity Tier 1 (CET1) ratio was breached, and a discretionary trigger based on the financial regulator’s assessment that the PONV had been reached. This confusion was further exacerbated by the lack of clear criteria to determine what is the PONV.

This ambiguity also created pricing uncertainty, which increased costs to investors. The statement put out by the EBA and the ECB stating that they would not deviate from the traditional creditor priority further fueled confusion and created uncertainty in the CoCo market.

Reforming and simplifying the CoCo structure would help reduce these concerns. A proposed model for CoCos calls for its design to be simple, have off-the-shelf pricing, and allow for an easy recapitalization process. These CoCos would not include an automatic or discretionary trigger, but instead would give the issuer an option to convert the CoCo’s fixed income component into equity. Because CoCos provide equity capital exactly at a time when a struggling bank requires capital infusion, CoCos also act as a countercyclical instrument. Their countercyclical nature is a key advantage to the issuing banks.

Under the redesigned structure, the conversion would be based on a specified strike price, which would provide the issuing bank with the ability to raise equity capital at predefined terms. Because the right to convert is an option for the issuer, pricing the CoCos would be based on a predictable option pricing model. This simpler model would also remove regulatory involvement in determining whether a trigger is met.

###### **MODULE QUIZ 95.2**

1. Which of the following features did not help trigger the Credit Suisse contingent convertible bonds (CoCos) write-down in March 2023?

   - A. Requiring the Swiss federal government to act under Swiss emergency law.

   - B. Involving the Swiss federal government to trigger the write-down of the CoCos.

   - C. Setting up a collateralized special purpose vehicle to facilitate the recapitalization.

   - D. Giving the Swiss Financial Market Supervisory Authority (FINMA) the authority to trigger a conversion to equity.

2. Following the Credit Suisse CoCo write-down in March 2023, there has been a push to reform the CoCo structure. Which of the following features is not one of the elements proposed to reform the CoCo structure?

   - A. Simplicity.

   - B. Off-the-shelf pricing.

   - C. Easy recapitalization process.

D. Automatic, rather than discretionary, triggers.

##### **KEY CONCEPTS**

###### **LO 95.a**

Contingent convertible bonds (CoCos) were introduced following the global financial crisis of 2007–2009 to limit systemic risk. CoCos help recapitalize a distressed bank and reduce the need for a bank bailout by a government during a crisis.

CoCo features include a trigger that activates them, and a loss absorption mechanism. The trigger can be mechanical (based on a capital ratio trigger), or discretionary (also called point of nonviability, or PONV) based on the judgment of a supervisory body. The loss absorption mechanism converts the bonds to equity (based on a predetermined price, market price, or a combination of both) at a predefined conversion rate or improves capital through a write-down of principal.

For CoCos to qualify as regulatory capital under Basel III, they must meet two criteria: (1) include a discretionary (PONV) trigger, and (2) have a minimum regulatory capitalto-risk-weighted assets ratio (going concern rule).

CoCos have higher yields than traditional bonds, because they have a lower priority of claim. They are generally attractive to well-capitalized, larger banks.

###### **LO 95.b**

Bear Stearns suffered heavy losses due to its exposure to subprime mortgages. The bank was bailed out by the U.S. Federal Reserve and the U.S. Treasury in 2008 through a merger with JPMorgan. The bailout was facilitated by setting up a collateralized special purpose vehicle (SPV), with a $1 billion infusion by JPMorgan and a $29 billion infusion by the Federal Reserve. The Federal Reserve then used the SPV to buy up to $30 billion of troubled Bear Stearns assets.

Following systemic risk and contagion from the global banking system in 2023, the Swiss bank Credit Suisse faced significant liquidity problems that required it to request liquidity support from the Swiss National Bank. Credit Suisse was then taken over by the Swiss investment bank UBS. As part of the takeover arrangement, the Swiss financial regulator FINMA triggered a nearly CHF16 billion write-down of Credit Suisse CoCos, which helped to recapitalize the bank’s balance sheet.

###### **LO 95.c**

The Swiss federal government had significant authority under Swiss emergency law to trigger the write-down of the Credit Suisse CoCos. Specifically, the regulator FINMA had the authority to trigger a conversion to equity when it determined that Credit Suisse reached the PONV. A key argument of this arrangement was that the CoCos functioned exactly as they were designed, by infusing equity and helping to deleverage Credit Suisse’s balance sheet.

###### **LO 95.d**

The write-down of the Credit Suisse CoCos created several problems as follows:

1. It caught many investors by surprise who did not anticipate a write-down of the CoCos.

2. It caused significant negative market reaction because market participants viewed the events as disregarding the accepted creditor hierarchy that bondholders have a more senior claim than shareholders in a distress scenario.

3. Several non-Swiss European regulatory bodies distanced themselves from FINMA’s action, and publicly stated that in the event of a banking crisis, common shareholders would absorb losses first, rather than bondholders.

4. Several lawsuits were brought against Credit Suisse.

5. It increased the costs to investors by creating pricing uncertainty.

To remedy these problems in the future, there is a push to reform the CoCo structure. A proposed structure would require CoCos to be: (1) simple, (2) have off-the-shelf pricing, and (3) allow for an easy recapitalization process. Under this structure, the issuer would be given the option to convert the CoCos based on a specified strike price, rather than based on automatic or discretionary triggers.

##### **ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES**

##### **Module Quiz 95.1**

1. **D** CoCos typically have multiple triggers, including a mechanical trigger based on a predefined capital ratio, and a discretionary trigger called PONV trigger. A PONV trigger is based on the judgment of a supervisory body or regulator that the bank’s capital has weakened and that without the CoCo conversion the bank could become insolvent or nonviable. (LO 95.a)

2. **C** For CoCos to qualify as regulatory capital under Basel III rules, they must include a discretionary (PONV) trigger, and have a minimum regulatory capital-to-riskweighted assets ratio (going concern rule) to protect capital levels. (LO 95.a)

##### **Module Quiz 95.2**

1. **C** FINMA, the Swiss federal financial regulator, under Swiss emergency law, had the authority to trigger the write-down of the Credit Suisse CoCos and trigger a conversion to equity. This helped avoid a more complex scenario of setting up a special purpose vehicle to bail out the bank. (LO 95.c)

2. **D** Reforming the CoCo structure recommends their design to be (1) simple, (2) have off-the-shelf pricing, and (3) allow for an easy recapitalization process. CoCos would not have triggers (either automatic or discretionary), but instead give the issuer an option to convert the CoCo’s fixed income component into equity. (LO 95.d)

### **READING 96**

## **ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE AND BANK SUPERVISION**

###### **Study Session 15**

#### **EXAM FOCUS**

This topic focuses on the adoption, advantages, disadvantages, and regulation of artificial intelligence (AI) in the financial sector. For the exam, understand the five advantages and disadvantages offered by AI for banks and their customers. Also, pay attention to how these factors might inform potential regulatory responses, including the potential for bias and its connection with alternative data.

#### **MODULE 96.1: ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE AND BANK SUPERVISION**

#### **AI Applications in the Financial Sector**

**LO 96.a: Describe historical evolution and common types of AI-based applications used in the financial sector.**

**Artificial intelligence (AI)** is a form of intelligence that is generated directly by computers rather than humans. The concepts underlying AI were first developed by Alan Turing in a paper from 1950. In 1997, almost 50 years later, IBM’s Deep Blue supercomputer used these ideas to win a chess match against world champion Garry Kasparov. These advances have harnessed the power of **machine learning** , where computers write their own code, and **neural networks** , which classify text, images, and other data. Some familiar applications of these innovations include Goggle searches, the movie recommendation engine for Netflix, and the advances in human–computer interaction via ChatGPT.

AI innovations have not escaped the notice of financial institutions. A survey conducted by McKinsey and Co. in 2019 found that 36% of firms in the financial sector are automating back-office processes using AI, 32% deploy AI chatbots for customer service, and 25% use AI for fraud detection and the evaluation of creditworthiness.

Cornerstone Advisors produced another survey in 2022 showing that 25% of financial sector firms were using AI for process automation and 18% were using AI chatbots. These two surveys present somewhat different numbers, but they both point to two realities. First, the financial sector is adopting AI technologies. Second, the adoption rate may be slow right now, but it is expected to accelerate considerably in the near future.

Regulators are anticipating this potential growth in AI adoption by financial institutions. In March 2021, the Federal Reserve Board of Governors, the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency (OCC), the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau, the Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation (FDIC), and the National Credit Union Administration jointly issued a request for information (RFI) to gather perspectives on the potential implications of AI on the financial sector.

#### **AI Advantages**

##### **LO 96.b: Explain the advantages of implementing AI-based applications to the banking services companies and their customers.**

Banks are increasingly considering how to use AI technologies as a value-add in their customer experience. The key advantages that AI introduces to the banking sector are as follows:

1. _Cost cutting_ . Deloitte estimates that the top 2,000 U.S.-based banks spend $250 billion annually on customer support inquiries. Using AI chatbots could cut this cost considerably.

2. _On-demand customer service_ . Banking customers can have on-demand access to answer questions using a chatbot. They can also use text recognition to deposit checks and digital services to transfer money or pay bills. No longer is a trip to a brick-and-mortar location, during their operating hours, a necessity.

3. _Fraud detection and prevention_ . Customers can be notified when an algorithm flags a transaction or a request for new service may be fraudulent. One example is when a credit card issuer alerts their customers regarding out-of-state or online transactions that could be the result of fraud. There is also an identity theft component to this advantage. Fraudsters are constantly evolving their path of attack, which makes machine learning and AI a potentially solid defense in that battle.

4. _Anti-money laundering (AML)_ . The Patriot Act, from 2001, places significant AML responsibilities on financial institutions. Since complexity is very high, machine learning and AI are very useful tools for banks to deploy. Companies like Symphony AyasdiAI have developed software that banks can use to help flag potential AML alerts that require human review.

5. _Know your customer (KYC) rules_ . Banks must also verify the identity of their customers. This data would include address information. New York-based Socure has developed AI tools to help banks streamline these verification requirements.

**AI Disadvantages**

**LO 96.c: Discuss the disadvantages and difficulties for financial companies using AI.**

The use of AI technologies will also present challenges when applied to financial institutions. The key disadvantages and difficulties are as follows:

1. _Loss of personal touch._ Some customers prefer a high-touch and in-person experience. They like talking with a human on the phone to answer very specific questions and connecting with a bank teller to handle transactional needs. Shifting to an AI-based interaction has the potential to commoditize the customer experience and reduce differentiation between banks.

2. _Lack of control over potential biases_ . Artificial intelligence gathers data points and evaluates them. All data is essentially reduced to an algorithm. Some of these algorithms may contain unintended biases based on the data inputs (e.g., when using credit scores).

3. _Opaque models_ . Many AI-managed processes operate in a manner that is opaque to users. This makes it difficult to determine how results are achieved, which may pose barriers to acceptance and use. This also presents a challenge when individual credit applicants look for an explanation for credit decisions. An opaque model may not provide enough feedback to help consumers evaluate their next steps.

4. _Evolving sophistication of fraudsters_ . People and entities who pursue fraud are finding some traditional paths blocked. This means that they will invest even more energy in finding alternative ways to conduct their fraudulent schemes. AI will need to constantly evolve to match the sophisticated rate of change in fraudulent creativity.

5. _Uncertain regulatory landscape_ . Regulators are still struggling with the potential impacts of AI in the financial sector. Some factors present risks due to their complexity. Regulation will be an evolving and uncertain backdrop until AI innovations are better understood.

#### **Regulatory Issues**

**LO 96.d: Clarify the specific issues faced by banks and regulators arising from utilizing AI in modeling and valuation.**

Regulation covering the use of AI in modeling and valuation within the financial sector is a developing process. Broadly speaking, regulators view risk management as the desired outcome. They are treating traditional modeling and AI-infused models as existing along a spectrum rather than as two binary options. The end goal of regulation is for risk to be appropriately monitored, controlled, and documented.

Federal regulators are considering several potential issues that are marginally connected with the disadvantages of using AI in the financial sector. Specific issues faced by banks and regulators are as follows:

- _Potential for bias_ . Several regulations already exist to protect consumers. One such law is the Equal Credit Opportunity Act. There is the potential for **algorithmic bias** (a.k.a. algorithmic discrimination), which occurs when the computer code running the AI process is not explicitly designed with fairness in mind. In other words, the AI process could unintentionally perpetuate a human bias like racism. The algorithms are designed to crunch numbers, not to ensure equal access to credit.

- _Evolution in credit scores_ . Traditional models consider a relatively small number of inputs when deriving a credit score. With AI as a copilot, credit scores can adapt to consider a much wider dataset. This could potentially expand access to credit, which is a good thing, but it all hinges on what data is considered.

- _Use of alternative datasets_ . AI models need data in order to analyze it and make better decisions. Some nontraditional data sources include the timeliness of rent and utility payments as well as cash flow patterns in a credit applicant’s bank accounts. The use of these alternative data sources has the potential to expand access to credit. This sounds harmless, but there is also the potential for privacy concerns. The need for alternative data could push into social media and web browsing activity. This would raise concerns of consumer privacy rules and potentially stifling free expression. Certain mortgage regulators (e.g., the Federal Housing Agency, the Department of Housing and Urban Development, Fannie Mae, and Freddie Mac) expressly exclude alternative data sources from mortgage decisions. There are many other credit applications available that do not currently have such limitations.

- _Model opacity_ . The algorithms that propel AI processes operate in a black box. The outcome may be known but the way in which the outcome was derived is not. This presents a natural challenge for regulators who have been focused on the process as well as the outcome.

- _The urge to overregulate_ . The potential exists for regulators to impose excessive constraints on a system they do not fully understand yet. Overregulation could stifle innovation. To their credit, many regulators are currently seeking understanding, but there is the potential to impose a higher level of scrutiny on AI-driven processes than on traditional ones.

###### **MODULE QUIZ 96.1**

1. Artificial intelligence (AI) is:

   - A. a new idea.

   - B. not yet a focus of regulators.

   - C. expanding its reach into the financial sector.

   - D. highly adopted for use in customer service at financial institutions.

2. Which of the following statements is not an advantage for banks to deploy AI technologies as a part of their customer experience?

   - A. Use algorithms to stop all fraudulent transactions.

   - B. Save money while speeding up the customer service experience.

   - C. Use algorithms to verify customer information and identification.

   - D. Use algorithms to flag suspicious transactions that could have anti-money laundering implications.

3. Customers might be hesitant to embrace AI in the banking sector because:

- A. fraud prevention methods are too restrictive.

   - B. regulation creates too many checkpoints for customers.

   - C. they know exactly how the algorithm makes credit decisions.

   - D. they prefer human interaction to solve their unique challenges.

4. Which of the following items is an underlying concern related to the potential for biases when using AI in the banking sector?

   - A. The use of transparent models.

   - B. Algorithmic bias through dataset selection.

   - C. Algorithmic bias through intentional model design.

   - D. Traditional credit scores being more favorable for those with low access to credit.

##### **KEY CONCEPTS**

###### **LO 96.a**

The genesis of artificial intelligence (AI) was a paper by Alan Turing in 1950. In 1997, IBM used a supercomputer (and AI) to beat the world chess champion. Machine learning, neural networks, and generative AI are more modern applications. AI adoption by the financial sector has started slowly but it has been accelerating. Regulators are beginning to take notice and consider the potential implications of AI on the financial sector.

###### **LO 96.b**

There are five key advantages for a bank to use AI as a part of their customer experience. They include cost cutting, providing on-demand customer service, fraud detection and prevention, anti-money laundering compliance, and know your customer compliance.

###### **LO 96.c**

There are five key disadvantages for a bank to use AI. They include a loss of personal touch, lack of control over potential biases, model opacity, the evolving sophistication of fraudulent actors, and an uncertain regulatory landscape.

###### **LO 96.d**

The end goal of financial regulation is to protect consumers and for risk to be appropriately monitored, controlled, and documented. With a rapidly evolving new technology, the risks are sometimes less certain. Some of the known regulatory concerns include the potential for bias, evolution in credit score calculations, the use of alternative datasets, model opacity, and the potential to overregulate.

##### **ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES**

##### **Module Quiz 96.1**

1. **C** Artificial intelligence adoption is building within the financial sector but less than one-third of firms are currently using this technology for customer service. Since

this area is growing so rapidly, regulators are beginning to take notice and trying to understand the potential pitfalls. (LO 96.a)

2. **A** Banks are using AI to cut costs and enhance customer service. They are also using it to detect fraud, but this is a constantly evolving area. They cannot catch all fraud, because fraudsters are perpetually probing new attacks. Banks also use AI for identify verification and to flag AML concerns that need human review. (LO 96.b)

3. **D** One reason that customers may be hesitant to embrace AI in their banking experience is a preference for personal touch. Fraud prevention is a key enhancement that AI brings to banking customers. AI models are opaque, so customers do not have a good understanding of why credit decisions are being made. That is a potential regulatory concern, but regulation over the use of AI in the financial sector is in its infancy. (LO 96.c)

4. **B** The potential for bias when using AI in the financial sector is a serious concern. The algorithms are designed to crunch numbers, not promote fairness. There is the potential that those algorithms will unintentionally promote bias through the data inputs. AI algorithms draw upon alternative datasets that may disadvantage certain credit applicants and have the potential to include inputs that could violate freedom of expression laws. While AI algorithms do not intentionally try to promote discrimination, regulators are not able to make a determination on this important element because AI models are opaque. (LO 96.d)
