### **READING 97**

## **FINANCIAL RISK MANAGEMENT AND EXPLAINABLE, TRUSTWORTHY, RESPONSIBLE AI**

###### **Study Session 15**

#### **EXAM FOCUS**

This topic focuses on the responsible use of artificial intelligence (AI) in the context of financial risk management. For the exam, be familiar with the areas of interest surrounding self-learning models and the discussed ethical considerations. Also, understand how the concept of fairness could be influenced through AI model design. In addition, be aware of how model explainability and archiving model elements are both needed for understanding the fairness outcomes of a model. From the perspective of trustworthy AI, be aware of the elements related to privacy concerns, explainable AI (XAI), and the process of optimization. Finally, understand how clustering and documentation can enhance trust in the explainability of AI model decisions.

#### **MODULE 97.1: AI IMPLEMENTATION IN FINANCIAL RISK MANAGEMENT**

#### **Model Bias and Ethical Considerations**

**LO 97.a: Describe the challenge posed by potential model bias and the ethical and responsible considerations surrounding the implementation of AI-driven solutions in financial risk management.**

The European Union (EU) has proposed the first major regulation targeting artificial intelligence, known as the **Artificial Intelligence Act (AIA)** . The goal of this proposal is to encourage ethical and unbiased behavior in the application of AI models, which are often highly complex. This is connected to Pillar 1 (capital requirements) of the Basel regulatory framework. The proposed oversight applies traditional regulatory model approval techniques because the areas of interest are model development, periodic validation processes, and model change methods. This review includes both static and

self-learning models, and it separates model changes into material and immaterial actions.

Three areas related to self-learning models that have drawn interest are as follows:

1. _Fraud detection_ . Anti-money laundering (AML) is a subset of fraud detection. It is known that fraudulent actors are constantly evolving their methods. Using AI, and particularly self-learning AI, can help keep pace with the rate of change in AML detection and risk mitigation. Another benefit is an expected reduction in false alert flags.

2. _Trading models_ . In a trading context, AI is involved with algorithmic trading and robo advising. These models are making capital allocation decisions, with low levels of explainability to associated stakeholders. Without the ability to review the process, the outcome is less trustworthy.

3. _Credit risk_ . This area has major ethical components. AI is used to extend or deny credit to applicants. The opaqueness of this process has explainability issues that raise ethical and fairness concerns. It is simply not known to what extent biases may be involved in this process.

Ethical considerations are also important with the recent innovations of AI in financial risk management. One concern is called **wanted bias** , which relates to bias in most risk prediction models. There is also the possibility for unwanted **algorithmic bias** to sneak into the methodology underlying the AI models. In this context, discriminatory or other unfair limitations may exist based on inputs to the models or the models themselves. A root cause for bias could be errors in the training data. For example, there could be outliers or noise affecting the learned outcomes. Also, if populations are underrepresented or misrepresented in the training data, then that could introduce unethical biases to AI models.

#### **Maintaining Fairness**

**LO 97.b: Analyze the potential benefits and challenges of utilizing AI while maintaining fairness and preventing biases in risk assessment and decisionmaking.**

Most people agree that “fairness” in AI models is an admirable goal, but quantifying this abstract concept in the context of AI model development can be challenging. Considering this aspect of model design can minimize reputational risk for firms deploying AI in financial risk management. One way to define **fairness** is ensuring an equitable and just allocation of benefits (and costs) while also minimizing the impact of bias, discrimination, and stigmas. An effective fairness process will also provide the opportunity to contest or seek restitution for any unfair decisions made. In other words, AI models must have a layer of accountability built in.

At a high level, two considerations for fairness outcomes are as follows:

1. _Fairness outcomes can be influenced_ . One common example is when there are different health insurance premiums for men versus women. This may be based on

actual cost data, but there is the potential for bias based on the data used for analysis. This fairness outcome could be influenced through AI model design.

2. _Fairness outcomes cannot be influenced_ . Some fairness issues originate from a problem outside the control of the AI model. For example, there could be spillover gender bias that exists in the credit approval process. There is the possibility for women to receive higher credit denial rates than men. This may not be a fairness outcome that can be influenced through model design if it originates from genderbased differences. This is a bigger issue to address that is outside of AI model design.

#### **Validation of Decision-Making Algorithms**

**LO 97.c: Explain the proposed considerations for the technical validation of decision-making algorithms to check for potential unfairness.**

The technical validation of models needs to expand beyond conceptual soundness and model plausibility to include checks for fairness. Factors in this process include the following:

- Training data needs to be reviewed for potential outliers, noise, and bias.

- Model testing should focus on the problems that need to be solved rather than just the process. For example, testing of anti-money laundering alert models should be less focused on calibration and more focused on false positive ratios.

- Areas that are difficult to test (e.g., historically unprecedented financial market scenarios) should be identified and explicitly considered.

- The financial institution’s risk appetite needs to be considered relative to allowable residual risk.

- Explainability becomes increasingly important for decisions with a critical impact on end users. The main drivers of model results should be understood. The level of explainability relates to fairness where low levels of explainability (i.e., transparency) may point to unfairness issues that need to be addressed.

To test both validity and fairness, models must have the ability to be audited.

The ability to audit a model can be very challenging when it is self-learning. By its very nature, self-learning models are constantly evolving, which makes explaining how a specific outcome was historically derived problematic. This makes it vitally important to retain the full data history, all parameters, and all metadata for self-learning models. Jumps in model parameters need to be examined across versions of the model. This is the only way to rewind the model to understand why a certain outcome was achieved. Understanding what the model did is a critical step in considering any issues of fairness in model design.

The following six steps will likely need to be used when self-learning models are deployed:

1. Create an incident management system to determine what happens when the model is not working properly.

2. Develop an infrastructure to monitor model or data drift.

3. Rigidly stress test the model.

4. Develop an infrastructure to archive all relevant historical information (i.e., data, parameters, and versions) to promote explainability.

5. Evaluate the model using the Pareto optimum (i.e., the efficiency line showing tradeoffs between competing goals).

6. Incorporate identified risk and side effects into existing processes and requirements.

#### **Trustworthy AI**

**LO 97.d: Describe the approaches and technologies that should be considered in the implementation and assessment of Trustworthy AI.**

Adopting ethical AI practices involves harnessing the innovative potential of AI in a way that promotes trust, fairness, and explainability. There are various high-level concerns, which include privacy, transparency, and optimization.

Compliance with domestic privacy laws and regulations is a very important consideration for **trustworthy AI** . Three areas of note are as follows:

1. _Differential privacy_ . When looking at the dataset used by an AI model, users should not be able to identify the inclusion or exclusion of any individual data point. This is both an antidiscrimination checkpoint and a roadblock to ensuring that fairness is being implemented appropriately.

2. _Federated learning_ . This feature enables multiple entities to share and combine training data used in the testing of machine learning models without physically sharing the source information.

3. _Synthetic data_ . This data is generated by AI and used to support collaboration between unaffiliated parties. Using a _generative adversarial network (GAN)_ , synthetic datasets can be created that replicate the statistical properties of the real data they are representing. This enables privacy to be preserved, but it does not explicitly address fairness concerns.

**Explainable AI (XAI)** attempts to resolve fairness concerns related to the low transparency inherent in AI models. One XAI approach is to make models easy to interpret by design. A flaw in this approach is that it requires tremendous simplification, which is not sufficient for addressing complex relationships in the underlying data. Another option is to use post-hoc approaches (e.g., local interpretable model-agnostic explanations [LIME] or SHapley Additive exPlanations [SHAPs]) that apply game theory to interpolate what the model may have been doing. These methods are more about making the model less opaque than making it transparent. Using either a LIME or SHAP approach to address explainability requires a significant amount of computing power and it introduces a second model into the workflow. An innovative new approach in an investment context is to generate forecasted market data that is highly correlated to actual market data. Using this approach allows users to explore simulated outcomes to search for explainability with synthetic data that closely resembles real-world data.

The pursuit of explainable and trustworthy AI can be extended through **optimization** . One approach is to optimize four variables: explainability, fairness, model accuracy, and model speed (i.e., efficiency). Despite the usual conflict between explainability and accuracy, this method can mathematically produce an efficient Pareto frontier of choices. This frontier will be nonsmooth and nonconvex. Each point on the frontier can be displayed as a single line in a parallel-coordinate plot. Users can then select the line with the most desirable set of tradeoffs. In other words, this optimization method can be used to find a compromise solution that achieves the most desirable combination of the four variables for the user’s unique situation.

Many companies are now incorporating formal ethics reviews in their use of AI. This includes internal and external algorithmic audits. The purpose is to ensure that models are producing verified results that are sufficiently trustworthy and explainable. The Frankfurt Institute for Risk Management and Regulation is working on solutions in trustworthy AI through a project called Financial AI Cluster (FAIC).

#### **AI Case Study in Credit Risk Management**

**LO 97.e: Examine the application of Explainable AI (XAI) in the field of credit risk management as presented in the use case of a European insurance group.**

The EU Horizon 2020 project presented a use case scenario to begin addressing concerns related to explainable AI in the context of a European insurance group. It drew upon resources from the FIN-TECH (FINancial supervision and TECHnology compliance training) program, and it applied SHAP-based XAI to the credit risk management process. The goal was to consider ways to make black-box AI models more explainable to reduce any inhibitors to innovation.

An interesting approach is to utilize SHAP clustering in which portfolio constituents with similar outcomes or explanatory variables are grouped (i.e., clustered) together. The post-hoc explanatory information is clustered together based on observed similarities. Data points at the intersection of two clusters deserve further investigation because they represent “fuzzy” decision-making. Any clusters with nearly equal “yes” and “no” outcomes could indicate an error in the self-learning AI model. The data can also be correlated with input features and predictions. This process is associated with Shapley value decomposition to breakdown the contribution of each input variable to the ultimate outcome of a self-learning AI model. It is important to note that this approach is model agnostic.

This entire process can be performed using only GPU memory, which means that it is very efficient to generate explanations quickly. The FIN-TECH program used RAPIDS, which is a suite of open-source Python libraries to speed up calculation times. Visual dashboards can be used by customer service representatives to answer customer inquiries about financial decisions (e.g., a decision to underwrite or not underwrite a policy).

The goal is to reduce opacity and increase accountability such that AI model users can consider a specific data point and understand the corresponding inputs that

contributed to it. Part of this accountability process is traceability through documentation of datasets, data labeling, and decisions derived from the AI model. The explanation of the outcome must be understandable to all users (i.e., model developers, model validators, risk managers, senior managers, supervisors, and customers). In this context, _explainability_ means that further information would not increase model understanding, the user can sufficiently explain AI decisions to a third party, documentation exists for traceability, the process fosters trust, and supervisors have what they need to ensure compliance with laws and regulations.

1. An ethical concern related to the use of artificial intelligence (AI) in the context of financial risk management is the:

   - A. enacted Artificial Intelligence Act (AIA).

   - B. potential bias in credit decision-making.

   - C. increase in false anti-money laundering flags.

   - D. inability to keep pace with anti-money laundering violations.

2. Fairness is a key consideration in AI model design. Which of the following actions is not something that model developers can control?

   - A. Ensuring that models limit unfair biases.

   - B. Creating an option for model accountability.

   - C. Ensuring that no bias is in the final outcome of a model.

   - D. Providing an equitable distribution of costs through the model.

3. From the perspective of technical validation, it is important to:

   - A. discard model metadata periodically.

   - B. ignore potential outliers in the training data.

   - C. measure only the model outcomes for fairness.

   - D. archive historical data, parameters, and versions of self-learning models.

4. Regarding the ethical implications of artificial intelligence, which of the following statements is most accurate?

   - A. Synthetic data can be used to mask actual user information.

   - B. Synthetic data is not advisable when two unaffiliated entities are collaborating on an AI model.

   - C. Federated learning ensures that no individual user can be identified in the dataset used in a model.

   - D. Optimization methods are not helpful because the variables of interest are often at opposite ends of the risk spectrum.

5. From the EU Horizon 2020’s use case example, it can be seen that:

   - A. clustering by influential input variables can increase model explainability.

   - B. there are no adequate options available to increase trust in model explainability.

   - C. methods to increase trust for risk managers do not address trust issues for supervisors.

   - D. clusters with an equal assortment of “yes” and “no” decisions inspire trust in model accuracy.

**KEY CONCEPTS**

###### **LO 97.a**

Regulation is being considered to address model bias and ethical issues related to the use of artificial intelligence in financial risk management. Many of the concerns relate to self-learning models and the approval and vetting of models. AI models are not transparent (i.e., they have low explainability). This concern leaves the potential for unwanted biases. There are also some specific ethical concerns related to model training data.

###### **LO 97.b**

Broadly defined, fairness means ensuring an equitable and just distribution of benefits and costs while minimizing biases and discrimination. There is the potential for AI model design to address some issues of fairness.

###### **LO 97.c**

Technical validation is important for any model. In this context, fairness also needs to be considered. There can be issues in the training data or in the lack of explainability in the model. It is important to archive historical data, parameters, and model versions so that results can be reverse engineered to better understand if there are any fairness issues in the outcome of the model.

###### **LO 97.d**

It is very important for users to trust that AI models are producing outcomes that consider ethical issues. Matters related to privacy concerns include differential privacy, federated learning, and synthetic data. The explainability of an AI model can be monitored using AI auditing, but there is a tradeoff between some variables of interest (e.g., explainability and model accuracy). Optimization using a Pareto frontier is one way to address this concern.

###### **LO 97.e**

The EU Horizon 2020 project presented a use case in explainable AI using an EU insurance group. They attempted to enhance explainability by clustering outcomes and the associated input variables that led to those outcomes. The process allowed them to use AI to deal with complex situations while better understanding the elements that derived the AI decisions. Documentation is essential to increase trust and to provide developers, risk managers, supervisors, and customers with a level of trust that they understand how AI decisions were made.

##### **ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES**

##### **Module Quiz 97.1**

1. **B** The potential for bias is a material ethical concern relative to AI models. These models are opaque, and stakeholders are often not adequately informed about the reasons for credit decisions. Regulation (e.g., the AIA) has been proposed, but not yet enacted. Two benefits of using AI in financial risk management are a reduction in false AML flags and the ability to keep pace with the evolution in

criminal activity. Still, the lack of explainability poses unknown ethical concerns. (LO 97.a)

2. **C** Model developers can create an accountability system, promote equitable and just distribution of benefits and costs, minimize bias and discrimination, and focus on model outcomes that can be controlled. However, some model outcomes cannot be controlled because biases exist in such a way that model design cannot address. (LO 97.b)

3. **D** A vital element in technical validation is to check for fairness as well as model accuracy. To check for fairness, explainability needs to be a factor and historical archives of all data, parameters, and model versions are necessary. (LO 97.c)

4. **A** There are three areas of note connecting privacy laws to trustworthy AI. Differential privacy relates to not identifying any single user in a larger dataset. Federated learning relates to sharing data between unaffiliated parties, and synthetic data creates an artificial dataset with the same statistical parameters as the original dataset. There is a tradeoff between too much model simplification and the accuracy of the output. Optimization using a Pareto frontier is one way to choose a combination of competing priorities. (LO 97.d)

5. **A** The EU Horizon 2020’s use case identified clusters of influential input variables and outcomes to enhance explainability. This method uses low computing power and can produce fast results. Enhancing trust through the use of clusters and documentation can provide model developers, model validators, risk managers, supervisors, and customers with detailed information that increases their trust in the decisions made by an AI model. (LO 97.e)

### **READING 98**

## **ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE RISK MANAGEMENT FRAMEWORK**

###### **Study Session 15**

#### **EXAM FOCUS**

A key objective for artificial intelligence (AI) risk management is to reduce the negative aspects of AI systems while benefiting from the positive ones. For the exam, understand the challenges for AI risk management, especially risk measurement. Also, an understanding of the AI lifecycle and the key dimensions of an AI system is beneficial for understanding the specific tasks of AI actors. In addition, be able to identify and describe the characteristics of trustworthy AI systems and understand how there are inevitable tradeoffs between those characteristics. Finally, be familiar with the four key functions for AI risk management: govern, map, measure, and manage.

#### **MODULE 98.1: AI RISKS AND TRUSTWORTHINESS**

**LO 98.a: Describe how organizations can frame the risks related to AI and explain the challenges that should be considered in AI risk management.**

#### **Risk Management**

The **AI risk management framework (AI RMF)** views AI systems as having both positive and negative impacts. In the context of risk management, the goal is to minimize negative impacts and maximize positive impacts. This contrasts with traditional risk management approaches, which focus almost exclusively on minimizing the negative impacts. Proper AI risk management in the form of understanding the weaknesses in the current AI systems and wanting to correct them should result in the development of more reliable AI systems that are beneficial to society.

The AI RMF seeks to manage and mitigate the risks of potential harms from implementing AI technology. These potential harms include compromising individuals’ rights and safety, discrimination of specific groups, reputational damage, financial losses, and environmental damage.

#### **Risk Measurement**

Risk measurement challenges to consider for AI in the context of risk management include the following:

- _Using third-party data and computer systems_ is often necessary, but it adds complexity to risk measurement because additional risks are introduced for the organization using the AI system. For example, the organization developing the AI system may not be forthcoming about the risk metrics it employs, or it employs different risk metrics than the organization that will be using the AI system. Additionally, customers (of the organization using the AI system) may not always have the necessary controls in place when interacting with third-party data and systems, which adds further complexity to risk measurement.

- _Identifying and measuring new/incremental risks_ is a challenge for the obvious reason that little or nothing is known about such emergent risks.

- Currently, there are _no universally accepted processes or metrics_ specifically tailored for AI risk measurement. Using generic metrics or forcing the use of metrics (that are ultimately unsuitable) may provide misleading conclusions or results that are useful only in specific contexts. Some measurement processes lack sophistication or are potentially biased.

- Risk measurement may yield _completely different results depending on which stage of the AI lifecycle_ is being measured, because some risks develop (or even decline) over time. An AI system could be thought of as having three stages: design, development, and deployment, and the approach to risk measurement would likely differ in each of those stages.

- The actual level of AI risk in a _real-world setting may differ substantially from what is measured in a theoretical (e.g., pre-deployment) setting_ .

- Some AI systems are complicated and difficult to understand; the _nontransparent nature_ of such systems makes risk measurement a greater challenge.

- The purpose of AI systems is to _act as a substitute for human decision-making_ , so proper measurement and comparison requires benchmark (i.e., baseline) metrics. However, what AI systems do and the way they do it is not the same as it is for humans; therefore, such metrics would be difficult or impossible to determine.

#### **Risk Tolerance**

The level of risk tolerance for an organization or AI actor will depend on the context and the specific use case, which limits comparisons. As policies and conventions evolve in the AI world, risk tolerances will continually change. Among organizations, risk tolerances will also differ due to differing business objectives. For these reasons, attempting to specify risk tolerances will complicate the AI risk management process.

#### **Risk Prioritization**

It is not optimal to reduce all negative risks to zero simply because some negative events are too costly or impossible to eliminate. The challenge for AI risk management is to determine which risks are the greatest for the AI systems. These risks will initially

be given the highest priority in terms of risk mitigation tactics. Excessive risk that comes with potentially significant losses or harm may require temporary cessation of development and deployment activities. If a risk is determined to be low, then it will be given less priority, but the pitfall here is that the risk may actually be greater than expected.

Another facet of prioritization comes in the form of AI systems dealing with sensitive and confidential information, or those that clearly impact people—both types of AI systems are given higher risk prioritization. In contrast, if the AI system does not deal with private information or it impacts only nonhumans, then it might initially be given a lower risk prioritization. Regardless, the challenge remains to engage in ongoing assessment and risk prioritization because such lower-risk AI systems may still possess peripheral impacts that negatively affect humans and the environment.

Additionally, there will likely be _residual risk_ remaining after attempting to mitigate risks. The system provider will need to fully document these residual risks. This documentation will help advise end users of the risks from using the AI system.

#### **Organizational Integration**

An additional challenge with AI risks is that they need to be thought of in an integrated manner within the organization. For example, AI risks could be considered with other key risks, such as cybersecurity, to result in a more unified organizational risk management strategy. In that regard, efficiencies may occur because there are similar risks (e.g., privacy, data and systems security, environmental effects) that impact AI systems and those of non-AI systems.

To achieve this integration, organizations face the initial challenge of setting up and monitoring the necessary controls, duties, and incentives. However, the greater challenge comes in the form of potential changes required in the organizational culture, which comes from the senior levels of management (i.e., “tone from the top”).

As a general note, small- and medium-sized organizations are likely to face different challenges than large organizations when it comes to mitigating AI risks and implementing the AI RMF.

#### **AI Actors**

**LO 98.b: Identify AI actors across the AI lifecycle dimensions and describe how these actors work together to manage risks and achieve the goals of trustworthy and responsible AI.**

**AI lifecycle stages** have been segregated into five key categories by the Organization for Economic Co-operation and Development (OECD). These categories are as follows: (1) People and Planet, (2) Application Context, (3) Data and Input, (4) AI Model, and (5) Task and Output. Figure 98.1 displays these categories in the two inner circles as well as the corresponding AI lifecycle stages in the outer circle. In the best-case scenario, the risk management process begins at the _Plan and Design_ lifecycle stage and proceeds clockwise around the outer circle.

###### **Figure 98.1: AI System Categories and Lifecycle Stages**<sup>**1**</sup>

*[image omitted]*

Notably, the _People and Planet_ category is focused on human rights and overall societal and environmental well-being. AI actors involved in this category include end users, advocacy and environmental groups, and researchers. These actors could help analyze AI impacts, advise suggested limits for employing AI, provide guidance on AI risk management, and discuss the cost-benefit analysis of the AI system in terms of the economy, the environment, and society.

Key **AI actors** within the AI lifecycle are shown in Figure 98.2. The big picture here is that these AI actors cooperate with each other in a risk management context, with the ultimate objective to create a trustworthy and responsible AI. Note that _TEVV_ refers to the test, evaluation, verification, and validation processes that occur on an ongoing basis during the corresponding AI lifecycle stage. TEVV could be useful for determining and monitoring potential or new risks. The ongoing use of TEVV provides opportunities to fix problems early and to prevent or reduce them in the future.

**Figure 98.2: AI Actors Across Lifecycle Stages**

*[image omitted]*

Proper risk management will be achieved with the cooperation of all AI actors. The greater the level of diversity among the actors (e.g., professions, experiences), the greater the potential for more productive brainstorming sessions—including the ability to find creative solutions for existing and new risks.

#### **Trustworthy AI**

**LO 98.c: Describe the characteristics of trustworthy AI and analyze the proposed guidance to address them.**

There are seven specific characteristics of **trustworthy AI** that will be described in detail in this section. These characteristics are as follows:

1. Valid and reliable

2. Safe

3. Secure and resilient

4. Accountable and transparent

5. Explainable and interpretable

6. Privacy enhanced

7. Fair (by managing harmful bias)

A trustworthy AI system has an appropriate mix of these characteristics depending on the purpose of the AI system. _Accountable and transparent_ is a fundamental characteristic, as it is pervasive to all other characteristics. _Valid and reliable_ is also fundamental as it relates to all characteristics (except _accountable and transparent_ ).

The characteristics of trustworthy AI systems need to be measured using appropriate metrics and there needs to be appropriate thresholds for success and failure. This

measurement has some subjective elements—and, therefore, requires human judgment. It is also important to recognize that the approach to the characteristics must be holistic in nature and not done merely on an individual basis. In practice, some characteristics will have higher priority than others in certain scenarios, and the overall concept of trustworthiness follows the principle of “it is only as good as its weakest link.”

Furthermore, the concept of tradeoffs between trustworthy characteristics is always a challenge when managing AI risks. There are many tradeoff scenarios to consider, including: higher interpretability will often come at the cost of lower privacy, and higher accuracy may come at the cost of lower interpretability. The ultimate consideration with tradeoffs is highly dependent on what decision needs to be made, and because managing the tradeoff is largely subjective, it should be adequately justified.

##### **_Valid and Reliable_**

A _valid AI system_ has gone through an unbiased testing process to confirm that the system works properly for its planned use.

A _reliable AI system_ takes the elements of validation as a starting point. It then goes further by considering the time frame (e.g., life of the AI system) and performance without failure.

An _accurate AI system_ will compute amounts that are close to the true values (subject to a stated acceptable margin of error). Additionally, accuracy must consider other factors such as Type I and Type II errors and the ability to generalize to the “real world” as opposed to merely the testing scenarios. Accuracy may be enhanced when disaggregating and disclosing the results for numerous datasets, but this may come at the price of privacy because there is greater transparency with disaggregation.

A _robust (or generalizable) AI system_ can function at the acceptable performance level in different scenarios. Not only must the system perform properly for its planned use (i.e., validity), but it may also need to perform well for other unplanned (but reasonable) uses. It should have low potential harm in the unplanned cases. There is a natural tradeoff between accuracy and robustness—so in simplified terms, a more accurate system may only be usable in a narrow range of circumstances, but a less (but acceptably) accurate system may be usable in a wider range of circumstances.

##### **_Safe_**

A _safe AI system_ will not harm and will not place individuals, property, or the environment in danger. AI systems that are safe will have been designed, developed, and deployed with safety in mind during the earliest stages as well as provide guidelines on how to use the systems responsibly to avoid misuse or dangerous situations. Any incidents involving safety need to be clearly documented, along with the resulting risks to attempt to prioritize and rigorously mitigate the most critical ones (e.g., major threats to health or risk of death).

AI safety can be further enhanced through extensive testing and simulation that is done on an ongoing basis as well as more intense oversight. A “kill switch” to automatically shut down or modify an AI system may be desirable if there is suspected misuse of the system.

##### **_Secure and Resilient_**

A _resilient AI system_ can survive or continue to operate normally in the face of unplanned negative events or changes.

A _secure AI system_ is one that is sufficiently guarded to only allow authorized use and access. It can, therefore, provide service continuity, confidentiality, and with integrity despite threats (internal or external) to access.

Security addresses resilience, but security also encompasses controls to prevent, deal with, or recover from cyberattacks. Resilience addresses robustness (described previously), but resilience also encompasses misuse or abuse of the system or data.

##### **_Accountable and Transparent_**

For AI to be accountable, it must first be transparent. _Transparent AI_ provides all system participants with relevant information regarding the system and the output. The information may be targeted and personalized to specific AI actors or users. The idea is to increase understanding by individuals, which will enhance their confidence in the system. Furthermore, transparency is very useful when investigating inaccurate output or negative impacts in an effort to determine cause(s) and to prevent future occurrences.

_Accountability_ refers to the outcomes of the AI systems, and in that regard, the focus is on the AI actors. There is a positive correlation between risks taken and the associated level of accountability. Thus, when there are potentially serious and damaging outcomes from AI use (i.e., increased risk), then accountability (and by implication, transparency) must be increased. By having risk management processes in place, accountability within the organization can be strengthened.

Attempts to improve accountability and transparency need to consider key issues such as the availability of resources and the need to protect sensitive and confidential data. In that regard, there is an obvious tradeoff between transparency and privacy. Documentation that clearly ties the training data to the decisions made by the AI system enhances accountability and transparency when such documentation is needed when rectifying any AI system failures.

##### **_Explainable and Interpretable_**

AI is _explainable_ if the operational details behind the AI system can be easily conveyed. It is _interpretable_ if the output can be linked to the planned purpose and the output may be assessed and conveyed to interested parties.

By combining explainability and interpretability, developers and users are able to clearly understand the steps involved with how the AI system works—and, therefore, increase their trust in the system and its output. Different levels of explanations should

be provided, as the various users will likely have a wide range of roles and levels of understanding. For interpretability, providing clear justifications to support the AI system’s conclusions will help users interpret results.

In short, in the context of AI systems and their output, transparency can be thought of as _what happened_ , explainability as _how it happened_ , and interpretability as _why it happened_ .

##### **_Privacy Enhanced_**

_Privacy_ covers concepts including anonymity and confidentiality, which should be accounted for during all stages of the AI lifecycle. A more sophisticated AI system may allow for inferencing by using multiple data sources, which could ultimately unmask the identity of individuals or remove the privacy from what was formerly private information.

Privacy and security go together—an increase in one will result in an increase in the other. The use of privacy-enhancing technologies (PETs) comes with tradeoffs, however, as increased aggregation of data is privacy enhancing, but it reduces transparency. In addition, when data is scarce, increasing privacy could reduce accuracy and fairness.

##### **_Fair_**

In an AI context, _fairness_ deals with avoiding harmful bias and discrimination. The concept of fairness is problematic, as what is considered fair is not the same over a large population, and it may change depending on the situation or context. The removal of harmful bias does not always make for a fair system. For example, a geographic bias may be removed, but that action could leave an element of unfairness if there is still a lack of access to AI systems by those who are economically disadvantaged.

Biases can be subdivided into the following types: (1) systemic bias, (2) computational and statistical bias, and (3) human-cognitive bias. _Systemic bias_ could be embedded in data and in the AI lifecycle in general. _Computational and statistical bias_ likely occurs from poor sampling processes that contain too many unrepresentative items. _Humancognitive bias_ is the most pervasive of the biases and refers to how humans may (incorrectly) think that AI uses information to make conclusions or (incorrectly) think what the objective is of the AI system.

Because of the prevalence of bias, it may be embedded in an automated AI system. The power of AI systems could inadvertently spread such biases, potentially increasing harm to certain individuals or groups of society.

###### **MODULE QUIZ 98.1**

1. Regarding risk management challenges, the AI risk management framework (AI RMF) deals directly with which of the following risk features?

   - A. Risk prioritization and risk tolerance.

   - B. Risk measurement and risk tolerance.

   - C. Risk measurement and risk prioritization.

- D. Risk measurement, risk prioritization, and risk tolerance.

2. Which of the following AI lifecycle stages is most likely covered within the key dimension of application context?

   - A. Deploy and Use.

   - B. Plan and Design.

   - C. Verify and Validate.

   - D. Build and Use Model.

3. Within the context of AI systems and their output, which of the following characteristics of trustworthy AI systems is best thought of as “what happened in an AI system?”

   - A. Fairness.

   - B. Explainability.

   - C. Transparency.

   - D. Interpretability.

#### **MODULE 98.2: FUNCTIONS OF AI RISK MANAGEMENT**

#### **AI RMF Effectiveness**

##### **LO 98.d: Explain the potential benefits of periodically evaluating AI risk management effectiveness.**

A periodic evaluation process of AI risk management effectiveness considers an organization’s policies, processes, actions, implementation plans, metrics, expected outcomes, and other factors. Benefits of this periodic evaluation include the following:

- More robust approach regarding overseeing, mapping, measuring, and mitigating AI risks, together with more thorough outcome documentation practices.

- Greater understanding of how trustworthiness characteristics, sociotechnical approaches, and AI risks are integrated, together with the inevitable tradeoffs that need to be made.

- Clearer decision-making framework regarding designing and deploying AI systems.

- Developed set of rules and best practices regarding accountability within the organization for AI risks.

- Stronger AI risk culture within the organization that places importance on determining and mitigating AI risks to reduce the negative impacts to individuals and society.

- Enhanced system of communicating relevant information (internally and externally) regarding AI risks, risk management roles, and best practices.

- Better understanding of downstream risks.

- Enhanced TEVV processes for AI systems.

**AI RMF Core Functions**

**LO 98.e: Describe specific functions applied to help organizations address the risks of AI systems in practice.**

There are four key functions for AI risk management: (1) govern, (2) map, (3) measure, and (4) manage. The _govern_ function is designed to be pervasive across the other three functions. AI risk management should be ongoing and carried out during the entire AI system lifecycle. With the establishment of proper governance, it is often the case that _mapping_ is the first step, followed by _measuring_ and then _managing_ ; however, the order of the functions is discretionary and based on what is most beneficial to the user.

##### **_Govern_**

The **govern function** involves the establishment of an internal risk management culture as well as the creation of a process for determining risks, analyzing the implications of those risks, and mitigating the risks from the AI system. Effective governance results in the AI risk management process as well as the technical aspects of the AI system and design being consistent with the organization’s operational and business objectives. In addition, governance deals with the entire product lifecycle and related issues, such as the use of third-party information and computer systems.

As mentioned, the govern function is comprehensive and ongoing. In that regard, certain areas within governance, such as compliance and evaluation, might be seen in the other three functions as well. For example, a robust system of governance must come from the top (e.g., board of directors) in terms of setting the mission, values, goals, and risk tolerance. From there, senior management implements an appropriate risk management culture throughout the organization. Management staff at lower levels should clearly show the connection between the technical details of risk management and organizational policies.

Categories pertaining to the govern function<sup>2</sup> include the following:

- Policies, processes, procedures, and practices across the organization related to the mapping, measuring, and managing of AI risks are in place, transparent, and implemented effectively.

- Accountability structures are in place so that the appropriate teams and individuals are empowered, responsible, and trained for mapping, measuring, and managing AI risks.

- Workforce diversity, equity, inclusion, and accessibility processes are prioritized in the mapping, measuring, and managing of AI risks throughout the lifecycle.

- Organizational teams are committed to a culture that considers and communicates AI risk.

- Processes are in place for robust engagement with relevant AI actors.

- Policies and procedures are in place to address AI risks and benefits arising from third-party software and data and other supply chain issues.

##### **_Map_**

The **map function** sets up the risk framing within the AI system in terms of determining context and then the risks in relation to the context. In the absence of mapping, the various AI actors involved in the different lifecycle stages are not likely to know of or be able to influence what is being done in the other stages. Given that there is significant integration between the AI actors and their tasks, it creates the problem that the work done in an earlier stage of the lifecycle by one AI actor is inadvertently compromised in a later stage by another AI actor. The end result is a disorganized risk management process with which users are unable to place any confidence.

By being able to determine (negative) risks and their causes during the map function, the objective is to use that information to reduce risk and determine whether an AI system is required or not.

The map function requires the input of a wide range of opinions from both sources internal and external to the organization. Gathering as much information as possible results in benefits to the organization, such as (1) knowing the strengths and weaknesses of their AI system, (2) knowing what real-life constraints on AI can lead to undesirable results, (3) predicting potential undesirable results when AI is used as planned, and (4) knowing the risks that may occur when AI is used for purposes that are unintended. Ultimately, these benefits will hopefully allow for better risk prevention and the creation of trustworthy AI systems.

From the conclusions obtained during the map function, the process moves to the measure and manage functions, assuming that the relevant decision-makers provide approval to continue with using the AI system. At the same time, the map function is not a one-time process, and it may continue to be used as AI systems and their features change over time.

Categories pertaining to the map function<sup>3</sup> include the following:

- Context is established and understood.

- Categorization of the AI system is performed.

- AI capabilities, targeted usage, goals, and expected benefits and costs compared with appropriate benchmarks are understood.

- Risks and benefits are mapped for all components of the AI system, including thirdparty software and data.

- Impacts to individuals, groups, communities, organizations, and society are characterized.

##### **_Measure_**

The **measure function** is a mixture of both numerical and nonnumerical methods. It is a bridging step that uses the information on AI risks from the map function and develops conclusions that will subsequently be used during the manage function.

Measuring risk includes documentation of system functionality and trustworthiness. In addition, metrics for trustworthy features and social impact should be used. Within the measure function, tasks performed include thoroughly testing software and developing

performance assessment techniques. Regarding assessment, actual-to-budget comparisons and standardized reporting documentation are necessary.

As noted previously, trustworthy characteristics may require tradeoffs, so the process of measurement provides an objective basis to make such tradeoff decisions. Based on those decisions, the AI system may require a partial or complete overhaul, or it may be taken out of use completely. Alternatively, a range of controls (e.g., detective, recovery) may be put in place.

A full set of metrics (both quantitative and qualitative) should be developed and available for use by the end of the measure function. Consideration must always be given to developing new metrics as the AI environment evolves. The results from the measurement function will be carried over to the manage function for risk oversight purposes. Similar to the earlier discussion on the map function, the measure function is not a one-time process and may continue to be used as AI systems and their features change over time.

Categories pertaining to the measure function<sup>4</sup> include the following:

- Appropriate methods and metrics are identified and applied.

- AI systems are evaluated for trustworthy characteristics.

- Mechanisms for tracking identified AI risks over time are in place.

- Feedback about efficacy of measurement is gathered and assessed.

##### **_Manage_**

Within the **manage function** , things come full circle as risk resources are distributed to the mapped and measured risks within the context of the govern function. At this step, risks are prioritized, and risk mitigation takes place. In addition, action plans are developed for addressing any incidents and errors.

Data from the govern and map functions are used in the manage function to help reduce the risk of system failures. The documentation processes from the govern, map, and measure functions assist with the risk management process and promote greater transparency and accountability. Analyzing new risks would also occur during the manage function. As usual, the manage function is not a one-time process and may continue to be used as AI systems and their features change over time.

Categories pertaining to the manage function<sup>5</sup> include the following:

- AI risks based on assessments and other analytical output from the map and measure functions are prioritized, responded to, and managed.

- Strategies to maximize AI benefits and minimize negative impacts are planned, prepared, implemented, documented, and informed by input from relevant AI actors. AI risks and benefits from third-party entities are managed.

- Risk treatments, including response and recovery, and communication plans for the identified and measured AI risks are documented and monitored regularly.

###### **MODULE QUIZ 98.2**

1. Which of the following reasons is least likely to be considered a direct benefit of frequent periodic evaluations of AI risk management effectiveness?

   - A. Stronger risk culture.

   - B. Reduction in operating costs.

   - C. More thorough documentation processes.

   - D. Better understanding of downstream risks.

2. Which of the following functions within AI risk management specifically focuses on risk prioritization?

   - A. Map.

   - B. Govern.

   - C. Manage.

   - D. Measure.

##### **KEY CONCEPTS**

###### **LO 98.a**

The AI risk management framework (AI RMF) views AI systems as having positive and negative impacts. Within the context of risk management, the goal is to both minimize the negative impacts and maximize the positive impacts.

Risk measurement challenges to consider for AI include the use of third-party data and computer systems, determining and measuring new or incremental risks, the lack of universally accepted processes or metrics specifically tailored for AI risk measurement, different risk measurement results depending on the stage of the AI lifecycle, differing measurement levels in real-world versus theoretical settings, complicated AI systems that are difficult to understand, and the lack of appropriate benchmark metrics.

Additional challenges exist regarding risk tolerance, risk prioritization, and organizational integration.

###### **LO 98.b**

AI lifecycle activities have been segregated into the following five categories:

1. People and Planet (Use or Impacted By)

2. Application Context (Plan and Design; Operate and Monitor)

3. Data and Input (Collect and Process Data)

4. AI Model (Build and Use Model; Verify and Validate)

5. Task and Output (Deploy and Use)

AI actors cooperate with each other in a risk management context with the ultimate objective to create a trustworthy and responsible AI system.

###### **LO 98.c**

The seven specific characteristics of a trustworthy AI are as follows:

1. Valid and reliable

##### 2. Safe

3. Secure and resilient

4. Accountable and transparent

5. Explainable and interpretable

6. Privacy enhanced

7. Fair

###### **LO 98.d**

Benefits of a periodic evaluation of risk management effectiveness include the following:

- More robust approach regarding overseeing, mapping, measuring, and mitigating AI risks.

- Greater understanding of how trustworthiness characteristics, sociotechnical approaches, and AI risks are integrated.

- Clearer decision-making.

- Development of a set of rules and best practices regarding accountability within the organization for AI risks.

Stronger AI risk culture within the organization.

- Enhanced system of communicating relevant information.

- Better understanding of downstream risks.

- Enhanced TEVV processes.

###### **LO 98.e**

There are four key functions for AI risk management:

##### 1. Govern

2. Map

3. Measure

4. Manage

The govern function involves the establishment of an internal risk management culture as well as the creation of a process for determining risks, analyzing the implications of those risks, and mitigating the risks from the AI system. Effective governance results in the AI risk management process as well as the technical aspects of AI system and design being consistent with the organization’s objectives.

The map function sets up the risk framing within the AI system. By being able to determine (negative) risks and their causes during this function, the objective is to reduce risk and determine whether or not an AI system is required. The map function requires the input of a wide range of opinions from both sources internal and external to the organization. Gathering as much information as possible results in benefits to the organization, such as (1) knowing the strengths and weaknesses of their AI systems, (2) knowing what real-life constraints on AI can lead to undesirable results, (3) predicting potential undesirable results when AI is used as planned, and (4) knowing the risks that

may occur when AI is used for purposes that are unintended. Ultimately, these benefits will hopefully allow for better risk prevention and the creation of trustworthy AI systems.

The measure function is a mixture of both numerical and nonnumerical methods. It is a bridging step that uses the information on AI risks from the map function and develops conclusions that will be used during the manage function. Measuring risk includes documentation of system functionality and trustworthiness. In addition, metrics for trustworthy characteristics and social impact should be used. Within the measure function, tasks performed include thoroughly testing software and developing performance assessment techniques.

Within the manage function, things come full circle as risk resources are distributed to the mapped and measured risks within the context of the govern function. At this step, risks are prioritized, and risk mitigation takes place. In addition, action plans are developed for addressing any incidents and errors.

##### **ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES**

##### **Module Quiz 98.1**

1. **C** Although the AI RMF is used for risk prioritization, it does not prescribe risk tolerance. Risk tolerance is determined independently of the AI RMF. Risk measurement is a crucial part of the AI RMF. (LO 98.a)

2. **B** Within the key dimension of application context, the two applicable lifecycle stages are _Plan and Design_ and _Operate and Monitor_ . (LO 98.b)

3. **C** Within the context of AI systems and their output, transparency can be thought of as _what happened_ , explainability as _how it happened_ , and interpretability as _why it happened_ .

Transparent AI provides all system participants with relevant information regarding the system and the output. The idea is to increase understanding by individuals, which will enhance their confidence in the AI system. Furthermore, transparency is very useful when investigating inaccurate output or negative impacts in an effort to determine cause(s) and to prevent future occurrences. (LO 98.c)

##### **Module Quiz 98.2**

1. **B** Although a reduction in operating costs may be an indirect benefit in future periods, it is least likely to be a direct benefit in the current period. In fact, there are incremental costs incurred in performing more frequent evaluations. (LO 98.d)

2. **C** Within the manage function, risks are prioritized, and risk mitigation takes place. In addition, action plans are developed for addressing any incidents and errors. (LO 98.e)
