The following is a review of the Current Issues in Financial Markets principles designed to address the learning objectives set forth by GARP<sup>®</sup> . Cross-reference to GARP assigned reading—Bank for International Settlements.

### **READING 99**

## **CLIMATE-RELATED RISK DRIVERS AND THEIR TRANSMISSION CHANNELS**

###### **Study Session 15**

#### **EXAM FOCUS**

This reading examines the potential impacts of climate risk drivers on banks through both physical and transition risk drivers. For the exam, understand the distinction between physical and transition risk drivers. Also, be familiar with the analysis of various transmission channels, including both microeconomic channels (which link a bank’s financial risks to counterparty risk) and macroeconomic channels (which link a bank’s financial risks to broad market factors). The two main risks facing banks from these exposures are credit risk and market risk, however, other risks like liquidity risk and operational/reputational risks can also be prominent. Finally, understand the impact of geographic location, and the effect of amplifiers (which increase risks) and mitigants (which decrease risks) in the context of a bank’s financial risk exposures.

#### **MODULE 99.1: CLIMATE-RELATED RISK DRIVERS**

This reading discusses how financial risks of banks and the global financial system are impacted by climate-related risks, which is one of many risks that affect the operations of financial institutions. The findings are based on the work of the Basel Committee on Banking Supervision (Basel Committee), including its Task Force on Climate-Related Risks (TFCR). The relationship between climate-related changes and a bank’s financial risks is based on the following components:

1. _Climate-related risk drivers_ are climate risks that could create financial risks for banks and are classified as either physical or transition risks. Physical risks include weather and climate changes, including both acute and chronic physical hazards. Transition risks relate to changes in risks from moving to a low carbon economy.

2. _Transmission channels_ relate to the channels that directly or indirectly impact a bank’s financial risks, including microeconomic and macroeconomic drivers of climate risk.

3. _Causes of variability_ refer to factors that influence the probability and magnitude of the impact of climate risks on banks, including the location of banks or their assets, and the impact of other climate risk drivers.

4. _Financial risks_ include credit, market, liquidity, and operational risks, which are the four key risk categories that can be impacted by climate risks.

5. _The Basel framework_ provides the regulatory backdrop and refers to the core principles of the four financial risks.

##### **LO 99.a: Describe climate-related risk drivers and explain how those drivers give rise to different types of risks for banks.**

**Climate-related risk drivers** refer to climate changes that impact economies but could also create financial risks for banks. They are usually segmented into physical risks and transition risks. Physical risks arise from weather and climate-related changes and include both acute and chronic physical hazards. Transition risks relate to societal changes from a transition from a carbon-reliant to a low carbon economy.

Global temperatures have risen notably over the last century, and will continue to rise given the increase in greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions globally caused by human activity. Rising GHG emissions cause increased environmental risks, including rising sea levels, increasing rainfalls, and resulting mudslides, but also increases in droughts and wildfires. As a result, curbing the increase in GHGs is critical and will require regulatory and technological changes as well as changes in human behavior.

#### **Physical and Transition Risk Drivers**

##### **LO 99.b: Compare physical and transition risk drivers related to climate change.**

**Physical risk drivers** include weather and climate-related changes and can be further classified into acute physical risk drivers related to extreme weather events (e.g., floods, wildfires) and chronic physical risk drivers related to climate shifts (e.g., rising temperatures, rising sea levels). Predicting these risks is challenging due to differences in the frequency and severity of risks and because they may appear with a lag. About 60% of global natural disasters are meteorological or climatological in nature.

_Acute physical risks_ are related to extreme weather events. These include heatwaves, floods, wildfires, and a range of storms (e.g., hurricanes and cyclones). There is significant scientific evidence that rising global temperatures create acute risks generating both higher incidence of heatwaves and wildfires, while also generating more severe rainfalls and flooding since warmer air can hold more moisture. Acute risks often compound, with some economies experiencing wildfires followed by flooding or droughts. These events can, and have, caused significant damages to local economies creating considerable financial losses.

_Chronic physical risks_ are related to climate shifts, and include rising sea levels and temperatures and ocean acidification. Prolonged periods of rising temperatures can cause changes in ecosystems and can contribute to lands becoming deserts. They may

also contribute to migration of large amounts of population within a country from dry to less dry areas. Melting ice and glaciers can also contribute to rising sea levels and the erosion of coastal land and cities.

The impact of physical risks will be different globally and may impact some regions more severely than others.

**Transition risk drivers** relate to societal changes from transitioning from a carbonreliant to a low carbon economy. Several factors contribute to these changes, including: (1) changes in public climate policies, (2) innovation and changes in technology, (3) changes in investor behavior, and (4) changes in consumer attitudes toward green initiatives.

_Climate policies_ relate to public measures and regulations to control pollution and facilitate energy transition toward a low carbon economy. Approaches differ globally, and may include explicit policies to reduce GHG emissions or to provide subsidies for electric vehicle production.

_Technology_ relates to changes toward greener, less polluting and more energy efficient technologies. This could create additional costs for corporations to adopt more expensive technologies or to operate with higher costs (e.g., due to carbon levies). Technology also relates to adopting technologies that result in greater environmental efficiency, for example, improved energy storage or the adoption of new climateresilient infrastructure.

_Investor sentiment_ relates to a greater awareness and changing attitudes by investors to climate risk, and directly incorporating climate considerations into their investment decisions. Large investors like fund managers can also act as activists by pushing for greater change in climate considerations.

_Consumer sentiment_ relates to changes in human behavior toward climate risks and changes in consumer preferences. This could relate to more efficient energy use or more climate-friendly consumption. A bank’s clients or investors may request the bank to adopt more climate-friendly policies or to direct its investments toward greener products.

Assessing climate risks needs to account for a significant amount of uncertainty in the evolution of these risks and should incorporate the four factors just described. Relying on historical experience may be incorrect given the speed at which changes occur. The nonlinearity of risks (frequent changes) and geographical diversification also increases uncertainty.

#### **Microeconomic and Macroeconomic Drivers**

**LO 99.c: Assess the potential impact of different microeconomic and macroeconomic drivers of climate risk.**

**Transmission channels** link climate event impacts to a bank’s financial risk. Two classifications are microeconomic channels, which link climate risk to a bank’s financial risk and exposure to individual counterparties, and macroeconomic channels,

which link climate risk to a bank’s exposure to broad economic factors. Climate risks impact credit, market, liquidity, and operational/reputational risks as shown in Figure 99.1.

**Figure 99.1: Financial Impact of Climate Risk Drivers and the Role of Transmission Channels**

*[image omitted]*

##### **_Microeconomic Transmission Channels_**

Microeconomic transmission channels look at how banks can be impacted both directly and indirectly from climate risk factors through their exposures to counterparties and financial assets.

###### _Credit Risk_

Both physical and transition risk drivers from climate events can create credit risk for banks. They can affect a borrower’s capacity to service or repay debt obligations, or they can result in a bank’s inability to fully recover losses from borrower default if the pledged collateral values are insufficient.

Physical risk drivers primarily impact banks indirectly through counterparty risk. Physical hazards can reduce counterparty capital and asset values, resulting in lower wealth, lower income, and lower cash flows to service debt. Transmission channels of physical risk drivers include households, corporates, and sovereigns and subnational institutions.

- _Households_ : Severe physical climate risk events like floods and hurricanes often lead to property value declines from damaged real estate and land. This is especially true for properties located in coastal areas or in floodplains. Lower property prices lower bank equity ratios and increase nonperforming asset ratios.

- _Corporates_ : Weather events primarily impact corporates through reduced profitability. Natural disasters can lead to lower corporate sales, reduced equity values, and contribute to supply chain issues. Risk varies greatly by reliance on energy use, geography, and sector. For example, the rise in global temperatures will have a greater impact on the agricultural sector given declining crop yields and lower corporate profitability. In turn, this increases credit risk to lenders including banks.

- _Sovereigns and subnational institutions_ : Sovereigns and subnational governments are impacted by severe climate events through lower tax income from weaker corporates and households, and through higher spending to boost the economy. This could lead to higher borrowing costs and weaker access to debt capital markets, resulting in elevated credit risk to banks from their sovereign and regional and municipal exposures.

Transition risk drivers have a more limited impact on a bank’s credit risk since banks historically have not experienced significant losses from a transition to a low-carbon economy. Credit risk is therefore mainly evaluated through scenario analysis of government policy, technological change, and consumer and market sentiment.

- _Government policy_ : Changes in government policy can directly impact corporate profitability and credit quality. For example, new or increased taxes on GHG emissions reduce corporate earnings and profitability, thereby reducing their creditworthiness which could lead to limited funding access or higher funding costs, and reduced ability to repay debt obligations. In turn, this increases the credit risk for banks that lend to these corporates. Corporates may also be faced with stranded assets from reserves that have been rendered uneconomical given the transition to a low carbon economy. If these reserves are used as collateral for bank lending, banks face increased credit risk of collateral value declines.

- _Technological change_ : Adopting new technologies is expensive which reduces profitability; however, the new technologies can give adopters a competitive advantage over other entities. Banks leading to entities that are unable to adopt to new technologies will face higher potential credit losses.

- _Consumer and market sentiment_ : Both consumer and market sentiment (expectations) may change as a result of a transition to low-carbon economies. An increased expectation of future negative climate events can lead to greater awareness of climate change and more action to prevent adverse outcomes. Those sectors that continue to produce in less environmentally friendly ways may see a negative effect on brand recognition, and less favorable funding conditions. These changing expectations can influence investment decisions with fund managers and investors favoring “green” investment alternatives and divesting investments in entities with high carbon emissions. Increased litigation against fossil fuel producers has also resulted in increased costs and reduced wealth for these entities, elevating a bank’s credit risk through higher borrower default risk.

###### _Market Risk_

Market risk captures the change in value of financial assets to changes in economic factors like interest rates, exchange rates, price shocks, and changes in volatility. Climate transition risks can reduce asset market values, lead to a breakdown in correlations and diminish the effectiveness of hedges. All of this can raise the risk to banks.

- Physical risk drivers can manifest in higher volatility given climate events. As a result, the stock options of entities located in regions impacted by climate risks can experience higher implied volatilities, increasing market risk.

- Transition risk drivers relating to transitioning to a low-carbon economy impact the creditworthiness of bank borrowers resulting in higher risk premiums for carbonintensive borrowers and may result in lower valuations of financial assets that are used as collateral.

- Pricing of climate risks can help corporates and banks minimize their sensitivity to future significant weather events. There is some evidence that asset prices, including

municipal and corporate debt and some equities, already reflect transition risk, although pricing is challenged by a lack of consistent methodologies and disclosures. The evidence for pricing-in transition risk for corporations is mixed, although equity prices of corporates in carbon-intensive industries is seen as partly reflecting climate risks. Similarly, evidence is mixed whether residential real estate prices reflect flood risk and sea level rise risk. A banks use of historical data to assess market risk and price assets may not be appropriate given the significant increase in recent risks in the market.

###### _Liquidity Risk_

Climate risks can raise the liquidity risk of banks given their capacity to raise funds, their ability to liquidate assets in order to meet obligations, and given reduced liquidity levels. Following severe climate events—including the 2011 earthquake in Japan— banks often offer significant liquidity support to the market to ensure stability, but this can negatively impact liquidity buffers. The liquidity needs of a bank’s counterparties can also reduce bank liquidity. For example, following a natural disaster, households and corporations may require liquidity from banks to finance their obligations and pay for the recovery, however, this puts a strain on bank liquidity.

###### _Operational and Reputational Risk_

Operational risk arises mainly from inadequate controls within a bank, employee mistakes, and breakdowns in internal processes and systems. These factors can impact a bank’s reputation. There is mixed evidence if climate events result in operational and reputational risks, although there are some indications that corporations and banks could be subject to legal and regulatory compliance risk, especially from climaterelated lawsuits.

##### **_Macroeconomic Transmission Channels_**

Macroeconomic transmission channels look at how banks can be impacted from climate risk factors through their ties to the economy. Macroeconomic factors primarily impact banks through credit risk and market risk (further research is needed for liquidity and operational impacts).

###### _Credit Risk_

A bank’s credit risk can be impacted through multiple ways, including impaired asset values or stranded assets, higher debt costs, challenges in accessing debt markets, or weaker counterparty creditworthiness. Here we look at the impact of both physical and transition risk drivers on banks.

Physical risk drivers:

- Climate changes can lead to lower economic output given higher mortality rates, lower labor productivity, lower supply, and lower agricultural output. In fact, it is estimated that global economic output will decline by $2 trillion by 2030. Research indicates that natural disasters disproportionally affect poorer municipalities since

they lead to higher costs from physical damage, higher borrowing costs, and even higher underwriting fees to reflect the greater credit risk of these municipalities.

- Similarly, major climate risk events impact developing countries to a greater degree than developed countries. Climate risks have already increased borrowing costs in developing countries, which can lead to higher taxes, lower government spending, and lower economic activity. In turn, these weaknesses impact a bank’s country-level credit assessment.

- Climate risks can also impact socioeconomic changes that in turn have an effect on economic growth. Although it is difficult to quantify the impact of extreme weather factors like droughts and flooding, they can lead to significant damages that could create physical conflicts. These impacts could affect the creditworthiness of a bank’s borrowers, raising the bank’s credit risk.

Transition risk drivers:

- Transition risk drivers impact banks primarily through weakened counterparty creditworthiness following climate risk events. The transition away from fossil fuels toward a low-carbon economy could result in impaired sovereign incomes and stranded assets. For example, an estimated 80% of fossil fuel reserves globally will become stranded. In turn, this could impair their ability to service debt and result in weaker credit ratings, impacting bank ratings within that country. Lower country GDP could also negatively impact household income, resulting in lower borrower credit quality.

###### _Market Risk_

While the implications of physical climate events for market risk are less evident, two of the key impacts are a sovereign’s ability to access debt capital markets and rising debt costs. Higher sovereign risk can result in credit downgrades, which could impact the creditworthiness of banks within that country. Banks could also be exposed to higher losses on government debt, reduced collateral values, and increased funding costs.

#### **Factors That Impact Climate-Related Risks**

**LO 99.d: Describe and assess factors that can amplify the impact of climaterelated risks on banks as well as potential mitigants for these risks.**

In addition to the risk drivers we have discussed so far, the probability and magnitude of the impact of climate-related risks on banks is also determined by three other factors: (1) geographical heterogeneity that determine the extent of the impact, (2) amplifiers of the impacts, and (3) mitigants that moderate the impacts.

##### **_Geographical Heterogeneity_**

A bank’s exposure to climate risks varies by geographical location, by sector, by jurisdiction, and by the nature of the bank’s business. Broad geographical factors like

weather patterns and even political systems could influence risks as well as differences in markets, economies, and financial systems.

Both top-down and bottom-up analysis could be used to assess a bank’s climate-related financial risks, which could vary based on the geographical location of the bank and of their assets.

###### _Climate Risk Drivers_

While average global temperatures are rising, there is considerable variance in temperatures across jurisdictions. Banks that operate in higher acute or chronic physical risk locations, including those impacted by frequent droughts or flooding, will experience more adverse impacts on their operations. Research suggests that banks in many jurisdictions including in Asia, Europe, and Latin America have been impacted by extreme weather events over the last few decades.

Differences in transition risk also exist due to differences in government policies, technological innovation, and changes in attitudes. The speed and type of transition also influence exposure. For example, jurisdictions that were early adopters of lowcarbon policies are likely less exposed to sudden changes in risk factors later. However, there needs to be more detailed research on these risk drivers since they are not widely discussed in existing research.

###### _Economic and Market Structure_

Differences in economic and social policies, differences in financial systems, and the availability of insurance can have a significant impact on a country’s—and therefore its financial system’s—exposure to climate risks. Emerging market economies have been shown to be significantly more exposed to physical and transition risks than advanced market economies. In addition, characteristics like low debt-to-GDP factors could provide resilience to risks in the short term, while a flexible exchange rate, strong infrastructure, financial sector stability, and low levels of inequality can provide longterm resilience to climate risks.

Societal and sector differences also matter in the level of risk exposure. Because rising temperatures adversely impact variables like mortality and labor supply, countries that depend more on labor and outside activities (e.g., agriculture-dependent economies) will be more adversely impacted by weather changes. Countries and regions that are more dependent on fossil fuels or on tourism would also be negatively impacted by adverse climate changes. However, insurance can offer an important mitigating role in transferring risks.

###### _Financial Systems_

The structure of financial markets and a country’s banking system and the state of development of its capital markets can impact a bank’s exposure to climate risks. For example, countries which have a large share of small local banks relative to the share of large banks are likely to see increased lending to consumers and businesses following adverse weather events. While this improves local economic development, it could increase concentration risk if lending is to a small number of borrowers. Some banks

may securitize these climate-risk related assets, especially in the U.S., which could help with redistributing credit risk. Additional research would be needed in this area to gain a better picture of recent exposures.

At the same time, developing economies typically have less liquid financial and capital markets, which could increase exposures and risks to climate risks. Capital markets also differ by the level of development of their derivatives markets. Derivatives are critical in hedging risks, including credit exposures to climate events. For example, it has been shown that the U.S. options market is sensitive to transition risks, such as the uncertainty from climate regulations, as well as physical risks, such as forecasts for hurricane activity.

##### **_Amplifiers_**

Amplifiers are risk factors that enhance climate risks’ impacts on banks. They include: (1) interactions between risk drivers, (2) financial amplifiers, and (3) the combined impact of multiple risk drivers.

###### _Interactions Between Risk Drivers_

While it is important to understand the individual physical and transition risk drivers, their combined impact on a bank’s risks is also important. For example, this could include the concurrent adoption of climate-related policies and the introduction of technological developments. When multiple climate events occur at the same time, they can also increase a bank’s climate risk.

###### _Financial Amplifiers_

Financial amplifiers are those factors whose combined interaction amplifies a bank’s financial risk and could create or enhance losses. The behavior of market participants and interaction with the economy are two examples of amplifiers, often through feedback loops. For example, during heightened climate risk periods, banks may reduce lending or reduce providing insurance coverage to participants. Reduced insurance coverage may make insurance more scarce and more expensive in the future, further exposing individuals and businesses to climate-related risks. Government intervention may be necessary to control the rise in risk premiums for climate insurance (e.g., flood, fire). The lack of insurance itself can amplify financial risks. For example, banks may require borrowers to have insurance policies in place, but they would not monitor if these borrowers renew their insurance policies annually, magnifying the banks’ financial risks.

###### _Combined Impact of Risk Drivers_

Amplification of risks could also occur when a risk driver impacts a bank through multiple channels, including through both microeconomic and macroeconomic channels. For example, a climate risk event could impair the bank’s economic environment (macro factor) while at the same time reduce the creditworthiness of the bank’s clients (micro factor). Another example is through feedback loops, for example, when a physical risk event like a hurricane damages homes and therefore reduces household wealth, this will in turn increase a bank’s credit risk to those homeowners.

##### **_Mitigants_**

Mitigants reduce a bank’s exposure to financial risks and can be both _proactive_ , which are done preemptively (e.g., diversification) and _reactive_ , which are put in place after a risk event (e.g., hedging and insurance). Banks cannot take the availability, reliability, and costs of mitigants for granted. Many mitigants, especially derivatives and insurance used in hedging, must be rolled over or renewed in the future, but the availability of these instruments and their future costs (insurance premiums may rise) are not known upfront and therefore create uncertainty.

The availability and level of mitigants vary by country and by the state of development of a country. Climate-related risks are often pushed to less developed segments of the financial markets which increases financial stability risk. The state of a country’s physical and financial infrastructure can significantly help in mitigating a country’s exposure to climate risks. Countries with well-built and interconnected infrastructure can reduce the financial losses from damages from climate events, while strong access to financial markets can reduce borrowing costs and provide greater access to funding when needed.

Financial mitigants can be categorized as: (1) bank behavior and business models, (2) insurance, (3) capital markets and securitization, and (4) hedging.

###### _Bank Behavior and Business Models_

Historically, a bank’s investment strategies looked at climate risk as an idiosyncratic risk affecting individual assets, and therefore banks hedged this risk through diversifying their portfolios. However, diversification proved much less effective in the face of rising temperatures and extreme weather events. The magnitude of a bank’s exposure to both physical and transition risks depends on the bank’s business models and on the type of risk event. For example, borrowers in high-risk exposure sectors including transportation, fuel-based industries, and manufacturing that is energy intensive are much more sensitive to a sudden shift to a low-carbon economy, which could adversely impact their balance sheets (creating stranded assets) and financial positions. Reducing these exposures is therefore critical. This can be done through a steady reduction in climate-sensitive assets or an increase in financial resources.

Banks can engage in risk reduction in several ways:

- Following climate risk events, especially natural disasters, banks have been observed to reduce their exposure to climate-sensitive assets. For example, following Hurricane Sandy in 2012, banks in affected areas tightened their lending requirements to corporations by lending at higher interest rates and requiring greater collateral from borrowers.

- Banks can strengthen their balance sheet by improving their capital positions. For example, many banks in hurricane-impacted areas strengthened their capital buffers by improving their risk-based capital ratios following the hurricanes.

- Banks have also been observed to reduce their exposure to transition risk drivers by lending at shorter loan maturities, reducing access to permanent forms of financing,

and corporations are subject to increasing shadow bank participation in syndicated deals.

Not all banks focus on risk reduction. Following natural disasters, many banks focus on economic support by increasing lending in the most severely impacted areas, often by reallocating credit from less impacted areas.

###### _Insurance_

Insurance is important because it mitigates losses against physical risks and improves a bank’s financial resilience since the insurer would pay for covering financial losses. Insurance can be used directly by a bank against losses, or by a bank’s counterparties.

Insurance can significantly reduce the losses resulting from natural disasters. Evidence suggests that financial and economic costs from natural catastrophes are primarily from uninsured losses. Insured losses in areas impacted by flooding or earthquakes and other natural disasters are typically small, and the economic impact can even be positive if payouts help support reconstitution efforts in an area.

Market participants’ perception of risk and the mandatory nature of risks in many instances can increase insurance coverage. For example, insurance demand in coastal areas in the U.S. and in flood-risk areas is typically price inelastic and more open to bank lending requirements. However, how much insurance individual borrowers will buy depends on their perception of risk, their age, and their level of education.

A significant shortcoming of insurance is that historically it has only covered a portion of losses, including 51% in 2018 but only 38% in 2019 of global economic losses from natural disasters. This protection gap is even more acute in developing nations, covering only 10% of losses on average. To protect against capital losses, several insurance-linked products have been developed, including catastrophe bonds, which act as a risk transference mechanism for climate risk-related losses. Types of insurancelinked securities include parametric catastrophe bonds, which are based on a trigger event like wind speed or rainfall, rather than on loss measures. The advantage of these bonds is that their triggers can be determined quickly and precisely. This market has gained prominence in recent years, growing to $40 billion in 2020.

###### _Capital Markets and Securitization_

Securitization is a form of risk transference, and securitizing riskier loan portfolios can significantly help banks reduce their on-balance sheet risk. This practice is particularly popular in the U.S. where banks often hold geographically concentrated portfolios. These banks often increase lending following natural disasters to help with economic growth, and then securitize these loans. In fact, mortgage and loan lending in disasterimpacted areas may increase specifically as a result of the climate events. Because these loans and mortgages carry significantly higher risk of default and payment delinquencies, these products are often securitized below the conforming loan limit (this is the dollar threshold for mortgage buying by government-sponsored enterprises).

_Hedging_

A special derivatives type used for hedging weather-related risks are **weather derivatives** . These are derivatives whose payout depends on weather events, and are popular among the insurance, energy, tourism, and entertainment sectors. While they provide useful protection against localized risks, they have proven to be less effective against providing protection of global climate risk events. However, recent innovation in climate derivatives like environmental, social, and governance (ESG) futures and carbon derivatives help bridge some of this gap.

###### **MODULE QUIZ 99.1**

1. A bank’s risk officer is evaluating climate-related risk drivers that could create financial risk for the bank. The risk officer has classified rising global temperatures and wildfires as acute physical risks. The risk officer is correct with respect to:

   - A. wildfires only.

   - B. rising global temperatures only.

   - C. both wildfires and rising global temperatures.

   - D. neither wildfires nor rising global temperatures.

2. A Canadian bank is worried that a corporate borrower will be unable to successfully adopt to new technologies to address rising climate risks, resulting in higher losses on its loans to the corporation. This risk is best characterized as:

   - A. credit risk through microeconomic channels.

   - B. operational risk through microeconomic channels.

   - C. credit risk through macroeconomic channels.

   - D. operational risk through macroeconomic channels.

3. A risk manager notes that macroeconomic transmission channels primarily relate to the impact of severe climate events on a bank’s credit and market risks, while microeconomic transmission channels primarily relate to liquidity and operational risks. The manager is correct with respect to:

   - A. microeconomic channels only.

   - B. macroeconomic channels only.

   - C. both microeconomic and macroeconomic channels.

   - D. neither microeconomic nor macroeconomic channels.

4. Following a recent hurricane, a local U.S. bank reduced the overall amount of lending to corporate borrowers impacted by the hurricane, and began charging higher loan interest rates. The bank’s actions are examples of:

   - A. financial amplifiers.

   - B. financial mitigants.

   - C. stranded assets.

   - D. risk transference.

5. Which of the following factors least likely provides long-term resilience to climate risks?

   - A. Low debt-to-GDP.

   - B. Flexible exchange rate.

   - C. Low levels of inequality.

   - D. Well-built infrastructure.

**KEY CONCEPTS**

###### **LO 99.a**

Climate-related risk drivers in the context of the banking sector refer to climate changes that impact economies but could also create financial risks for financial institutions. Two types of climate-related risks include: (1) physical risks and (2) transition risks.

###### **LO 99.b**

Physical risks include weather and climate-related changes, and can be classified into acute physical risks related to extreme weather events and chronic physical risks related to climate shifts.

Acute physical risks relate to extreme weather events like heatwaves, floods, and wildfires. These risks often compound and can cause significant economic damages and financial losses.

Chronic physical risks are related to climate shifts like rising temperatures causing droughts, but also rising sea levels from changes like melting ice and glaciers.

Transition risks relate to societal changes from the transition to a low carbon economy. These changes are influenced by: (1) changes in public climate policies, (2) innovation and changes in technology, (3) changes in investor behavior, and (4) changes in consumer attitudes toward green initiatives.

Evaluating climate risks should factor in the evolution of risks which create uncertainty in outcomes, and should also account for other factors like the nonlinearity of risks which increases uncertainty.

###### **LO 99.c**

Transmission channels link climate event impacts to a bank’s financial risk and can be classified as microeconomic channels (a bank’s financial risk and exposure to individual counterparties) and macroeconomic channels (a bank’s exposure to broad economic factors).

Microeconomic transmission channels look at how banks can be impacted both directly and indirectly from climate risk factors through their exposures to counterparties and financial assets. These mainly result from physical climate risk and can impact a bank’s credit, market, liquidity, and operational/reputational risks.

- Credit risk can arise from a bank’s exposure to weaker counterparties following severe climate events given counterparties’ lower asset (including real estate) values, lower profitability, collateral value declines, and higher borrower default risk.

- Market risk is the risk of a change in value of financial assets given changes in broad economic factors.

- Liquidity risk relates to a bank’s capacity to raise funds, its ability to liquidate assets on time, and the risk of reduced liquidity levels. Following a natural disaster, banks also often supply liquidity to impacted households and corporations which puts a strain on bank liquidity.

- Operational risk can arise from inadequate controls within a bank, employee errors, and breakdowns in internal processes and systems, all of which could adversely

##### impact a bank’s reputation.

Macroeconomic transmission channels mainly impact banks indirectly through their ties to the economy and the sovereign. The two key risks facing banks are credit risk and market risk.

- Credit risk arises from physical and transition risk drivers that affect national and local economies, leading to impaired asset values, higher borrowing costs, and reduced economic output, especially in developing countries. These in turn could impact the creditworthiness of a bank’s borrowers and raise the bank’s credit risk.

- Market risk arises from climate risks that impact a sovereign’s ability to access debt capital markets and could lead to higher debt costs. Banks in turn could be exposed to losses on government debt, reduced collateral values, and increased funding costs.

###### **LO 99.d**

The impact of climate-related risks on banks is determined by: (1) geographical heterogeneity, (2) amplifiers of the impacts, and (3) mitigants that moderate the impacts.

Geographical heterogeneity refers to the variability to a bank’s exposure to climate risks by geographical location, by sector, by jurisdiction, and by the nature of the bank’s business. Geographical heterogeneity depends on:

- Climate risk drivers which include factors like rising global temperatures, droughts, and flooding and their impacts on a bank’s operations. These drivers are also impacted by differences in government policies, technological innovation, and changes in attitudes.

- Economic and market structure which looks at how differences in economic and social policies and financial systems and the availability of insurance impact a financial system’s exposure to climate risks.

- Financial systems which look at how the structure of financial markets and a country’s banking system and the state of development of its capital markets can impact a bank’s exposure to climate risks.

Amplifiers are risk factors that enhance climate risk impacts on banks, and include:

- Interactions between risk drivers which look at the combined impact of risks on a bank’s exposure to climate risks.

- Financial amplifiers which look at the combined interaction of risk drivers specifically on a bank’s financial risk.

- Combined impact of risk drivers which look at the impact on banks from multiple microeconomic and macroeconomic channels.

Financial mitigants are risk offsets that reduce a bank’s exposure to financial risks. Categories of mitigants include:

- Bank behavior and business models, which suggest that exposure to both physical and transition risks depends on a bank’s business models and on the type of risk event, and a bank’s efforts to reduce these exposures (tighter lending conditions, lending with shorter maturities, improved capital positions).

- Insurance which mitigates losses against physical risks. Insurance improves financial resilience by covering financial losses. Market participants’ perception of risk and lender requirements can increase insurance purchases. However, insurance only covers a fraction of the costs in most countries (worse in developing countries). Capital markets and securitization, which looks at the role of securitizing as a form of risk transference, especially for banks which hold geographically concentrated portfolios.

- Hedging which primarily relates to the widening use of weather derivatives which protect against localized risks.

##### **ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES**

##### **Module Quiz 99.1**

1. **A** Wildfires are correctly classified as acute physical risks, which relate to extreme weather events. Rising global temperatures should be classified as chronic physical risks, which relate to climate shifts like rising global temperatures and rising sea levels. (LO 99.b)

2. **A** The risk of climate events impacting counterparties is captured under microeconomic transmission channels. If the corporation is unable to adopt to new technologies to address climate risks, this will result in higher credit risk (the risk of nonpayment/default on an obligation by one of its borrowers) and potential losses for the bank.

Macroeconomic transmission channels involve a bank’s exposures to broad economic factors like interest rates, inflation, commodity prices, and exchange rates. Market risk is the risk of asset value changes in response to changing economic conditions. (LO 99.c)

3. **B** The two main risk impacts from climate events via both microeconomic and macroeconomic channels are credit risk and market risk. Microeconomic channels also include the impact on liquidity risk and operational/reputational risks. (LO 99.c)

4. **B** Financial mitigants are risk offsets that reduce a bank’s exposure to financial risks. Categories of mitigants include bank behavior and business models, insurance, capital markets and securitization, and hedging. A bank’s tightened credit lending is an example of bank behavior within financial mitigants.

Financial amplifiers are amplifiers that look at the combined interaction of risk drivers specifically on a bank’s financial risk. Stranded assets are assets that no longer earn their expected economic return usually as a result of moving to a low-carbon economy. Risk transference refers to offloading risk from one party to another, either in the form of insurance or securitization. (LO 99.d)

5. **A** Low debt-to-GDP could provide short term resilience to climate risks. A flexible exchange rate, strong infrastructure, financial sector stability, and low levels of

inequality can provide long-term resilience to climate risks. (LO 99.d)

The following is a review of the Current Issues in Financial Markets principles designed to address the learning objectives set forth by GARP<sup>®</sup> . Cross-reference to GARP assigned reading—Basel Committee on Banking Supervision.

### **READING 100**

## **CLIMATE-RELATED FINANCIAL RISKS—MEASUREMENT METHODOLOGIES**

###### **Study Session 15**

#### **EXAM FOCUS**

The focus of this reading is on climate-related risks and how banks and supervisors measure and model these risks to understand financial and operational impacts. For the exam, be familiar with the primary issues to date in identifying and measuring financial risks tied to climate change. Understanding these risks requires unique data and the various methodologies used to analyze the data. Also, know the methodologies already in place and those in development, as well as strengths and weaknesses of those approaches. Finally, modeling frameworks present several gaps and challenges that must be addressed going forward.

#### **MODULE 100.1: INTRODUCTION TO CLIMATERELATED FINANCIAL RISKS**

In April 2021, the Basel Committee on Banking Supervision issued the “Climate-Related Financial Risks—Measurement Methodologies” report. This report reflects on five critical findings:

1. Forward-looking and granular measurement methodologies are needed due to the unique features of climate-related financial risks.

2. Banks and supervisors have focused on mapping near-term transition risk drivers into portfolio and counterparty exposures.

3. Banks and supervisors have primarily focused on credit risk assessments as they try to translate climate-related exposures into financial risk categories.

4. The pace of translating climate-related risks into quantifiable financial risks has increased.

5. Future analytical exploration is needed due to measurement gaps in data and risk classification methods. More exploration is also needed for developing

methodologies suitable for assessing nonstandard, long-term climate issues.

There continues to be uncertainty in the following three primary areas:

1. Intrinsic uncertainty related to physical and transition risk driver projections

2. Data gaps, which create measurement uncertainty

3. Model-based uncertainty

Climate change could have profound financial and economic impacts on banking institutions. To effectively manage the risk associated with climate change, these institutions should be identifying significant climate transmission channels and risk drivers, mapping and measuring climate-related exposures, and translating climaterelated risks into financial risk metrics that can be quantified.

A risk assessment framework for banks and supervisors should incorporate the following:

- Risk identification (risk drivers and transmission channels)

- Exposure mapping and measurement (metrics and indicators, as well as methodological considerations)

- Risk quantification (applying various measurement approaches to economic models and forward-looking assessments)

Risk management (determining how to manage and mitigate risk exposures)

#### **Considerations and Issues With Climate-Related Financial Risks**

**LO 100.a: Describe main issues in identifying and measuring climate-related financial risks.**

Banks and supervisors should consider the following concepts when mapping exposures and measuring financial risks.

#### **Transition and Physical Risk Measurement**

**Transition risk** results from the shifting of a high-carbon economy to a low-carbon economy. **Physical risk drivers** or hazards are applied to financial exposures through damage functions. These functions define specific hazard impacts on activities and real assets which generate financial cash flows. The impacts can be incorporated into risk models that apply financial risk parameters. A damage function is driven by time horizon, hazard severity, location uniqueness, and relevant sectors.

While transition and physical risks may be assessed separately, their dependencies may merit assessment in tandem. As physical risks increase, government policymakers may feel pressure to reduce future physical risks—and, therefore, increase the level of transition risk.

#### **Exposure Granularity**

Clients and counterparties for banks include individual households, corporations, government entities, and other financial institutions. Banks must determine the level of exposure granularity needed for each of these parties. The factors that influence these decisions include relevant data availability, specific risk drivers (transition and physical), the higher computational complexity associated with increased granularity, and the risk management decisions that the assessment is supporting.

While higher granularity methodologies are more valuable for valuations and underwriting, they can be highly complex and suffer from input data quality. Lower granular methodologies may be better suited to portfolio allocations and strategic planning, as they are less complex and more tolerant of sporadic data.

#### **Top-Down and Bottom-Up Approaches**

Top-down approaches begin by measuring risk at an aggregated (general) level and then breaking it down into its component parts. Industry or sector averages are determined and then applied to individual exposures. The logic is that any differences between companies within an industry and sector are minimal.

Bottom-up approaches measure risks at a component level and aggregate them up to a consolidated view. Risk exposure assessments at individual loan or counterparty levels may be summed to provide banking sector–views or portfolio level–views of risk exposure. A challenge with this approach is understanding any potential correlations among risk exposures.

#### **Incorporating Risk Mitigation and Risk Reduction**

Potential risk mitigation is a key element of climate-related financial risk measurement. While financial risks may be mitigated through government regulations and measures, banks and supervisors can take proactive measures as well. Gross exposure is a measure of climate risk impacts without the incorporation of mitigation strategies. Net exposure is a measure of climate risk impacts after accounting for proactive offsetting strategies, such as derivatives hedging and insurance. Gross exposure is important to capture not only because seeing the magnitude can help inform decisions, but also because mitigation strategies may change, lapse, or fail to function as designed. Modeling gross exposures and mitigation strategies separately may be helpful.

#### **Heterogeneities**

Because of the uniqueness of each individual bank’s portfolio and the exposures that each client and counterparty faces (which differ due to factors such as geography, technology, political environment, sector, location, etc.), heterogeneities impact the selection of measurement approaches. Geography is significant because climates are different in various locations. Climate risks tend to be similar across a given sector, even if individual clients within that sector are impacted differently. Policies and regulations may also differ by individual client or counterparty.

#### **Sources of Uncertainty**

Though modeling for climate risk has improved considerably, there is still the risk of misestimating risk. In particular, there is the concern of underestimating climate risk. Inevitably, assumptions must be made regarding interactions between the climate, human activity, economic activity, and government activity. Forecasting future emissions, technology, policy actions, and economic consequences is a challenge to the development of a model. In addition, climate change has a long time horizon that requires significant assumptions regarding timing and discounting.

###### **MODULE QUIZ 100.1**

1. A CFO estimates a climate-related financial risk of $35 million. She incorporates insurance with a benefit of $15 million and derivatives contracts that would provide a benefit of $3 million. The gross exposure associated with the financial risk is closest to:

   - A. $17 million.

   - B. $20 million.

   - C. $32 million.

   - D. $35 million.

2. If a bank wants to employ a risk management methodology for strategic planning purposes, it will most likely use a model that has:

   - A. low granularity and low complexity.

   - B. high granularity and low complexity.

   - C. low granularity and high complexity.

   - D. high granularity and high complexity.

#### **MODULE 100.2: DATA NEEDS AND MEASUREMENT METHODOLOGIES**

#### **Unique Data Needs**

**LO 100.b: Identify unique data needs inherent in the climate-related risks and describe candidate methodologies that could be used to analyze these types of data.**

Unlike the data that is used for traditional financial risk analysis, the data needed for climate-related financial analysis is unique and may not be fully available. In addition, historical relationships between climate impact and financial or economic consequences may not be particularly useful in developing forward projections, given the dynamics of climate change.

The following three data categories are useful for assessing climate-related financial risk:

1. _Data related to physical and transition risk drivers translated into economic risk factors._

This category includes information on climate and current/projected hazard events. Driver data can come from commercial third parties, academic organizations, and

government agencies. Survey agencies also provide “off-the-shelf” data useful in identifying geographic areas exposed to physical hazards and areas that are particularly vulnerable to physical hazards. Data can be used to project macroeconomic and microeconomic variables for different climate-related scenarios.

2. _Data linking climate-adjusted economic risk factors to exposures._

Vulnerability to risk factors is critical for banks and supervisors to understand. Exposure vulnerability data can then be used to translate economic risk factors to financial exposures. Geospatial counterparty locations are used to determine bank exposure vulnerabilities to physical risks, while counterparties’ economic activity within specific areas can drive bank exposure vulnerabilities to transition risk.

With physical risks, the concern is damage to inventory and property, value chain disruptions, and declines in region or sector activity. Aligning physical hazards to vulnerable locations will help in estimating economic impact and cash flow disruptions. Understanding how corporate, retail, and municipal borrowers are interconnected can help with determining how weather events can negatively impact a local economy. For transition risk exposures, assessing vulnerability requires data on counterparty sectors and their associated carbon sensitivities.

3. _Financial exposure data used to translate economic risks into financial risks._

Data used to project valuations, prices, and cash flows may be useful in translating exposure vulnerabilities into estimates of financial loss. Exposure at the bank system level requires data on portfolio composition and relevant counterparty information. The impact of climate risk on bank liquidity requires data on funding provider behaviors in response to climate risk drivers.

Climate risk classifications are helpful in ranking exposures based on their vulnerability to and associated financial risk from climate-related economic risk factors. Traditional financial risk measures such as leverage ratios, debt service coverage ratios, and loan-to-value ratios may have unique exposures to climate-related risks. The ability to incorporate dynamic features into a classification scheme that accounts for technological advances and ever-changing climate impacts is critical.

In creating a climate risk classification scheme, geographic location is important, as it is a primary driver of physical risk differentiation. Proximity and vulnerability to physical hazards are obviously significant factors. For transition risk, jurisdiction plays the most important role, as laws, regulations, energy grids, and markets drive an individual or entity’s ability to manage this risk. Another useful differentiating factor is economic sector. While greenhouse gas emissions may be quantified as an average number by sector, individual entities within the sector are likely to have unique exposures and vulnerabilities.

#### **Current and Developing Methodologies**

**LO 100.c: Describe current and developing methodologies for measuring**

**climate-related financial risks employed by banks and supervisors.**

**LO 100.e: Identify strengths and weaknesses of the main types of measurement approaches.**

Because climate-related financial risk estimations are still in the early stages of development, there are various modeling approaches used in practice. Examples include the following:

- **Integrated assessment models (IAMs).** These models combine economic growth, energy, and climate modeling. They have been very popular in linking projections of greenhouse gas emissions and transition risk drivers to impacts on economic growth. Unfortunately, IAMs have not captured extreme weather economic impacts as they are more useful in capturing recurring physical risks. IAMs also tend to understate severity associated with future climate events, which is exacerbated by the lack of historical data and precedent.

- **Sensitivity analysis.** This analysis has been useful in illustrating the volatility of projected losses due to probabilities assigned to tail events, although underestimating tail events remains a big concern because of the potential financial impact it can have on banks.

- **Input-output models.** These models are useful in estimating the economic impact of policy shifts from climate change events on a region or industry.

- **Computable general equilibrium (CGE) models.** These models are useful in testing policy experiments with complex interactions among agents and sectors in the economy, although the results may be somewhat limited by overall complexity.

- **Dynamic stochastic general equilibrium (DSGE) models.** These models are also used to manage complexity, although the computational burden is significant.

- **Overlapping generation (OLG) models.** These models are a more transparent way to model long-term macroeconomic evolutions.

- **Agent-based models (ABMs).** These models are useful in measuring climate-related impacts because they can better manage complexity and uncertainty. The modeler employs decision-making rules and creates simulations where individuals and institutions within the economy interact with one another. Technological change and damage functions are incorporated into the model. Although ABMs are highly flexible, they require a significant amount of computation and data demand, and the underlying mechanisms driving model outputs are somewhat unclear.

- **Stress testing.** This activity has been useful in linking bank portfolio risk to the modeling approaches just listed.

As mentioned, traditional measures of financial risk do not always apply well to climate-related financial risk assessments due to limited historical data, inconsistent time horizons, and the range of impact uncertainties. Banks and supervisors are currently using the following broad risk measurement approaches:

- **Climate risk ratings/scores.** Risk scores are used to rate climate risk exposures of countries, companies, portfolios, and even assets. Grading criteria (qualitative and quantitative) are used to assign a quality score to the various exposures.

- **Scenario analysis.** This analysis is used to identify projected impacts on individual exposures and portfolios through the identification of physical and transition risk scenarios, the linking of the scenarios to financial risks, the assessment of sensitivities to risks, and the extrapolation of sensitivities to determine an aggregate measure of potential losses and exposure. Forms of scenario analysis include the following:

- _Stress testing_ is used to evaluate an entity’s resiliency to economic shocks.

- Macroprudential stress tests measure the impact of financial shocks to financial systems, while microprudential stress tests are used to evaluate an entity’s solvency. Climate-related stress testing assesses the resiliency of an entity and/or its systems to the impacts of severe climate scenarios.

- _Sensitivity analysis_ is used to evaluate the impact of a specific variable on

- economic outcomes. This approach is particularly useful in evaluating transition risk based on individual policy changes.

- **Natural capital analysis** (third-party approach). This analysis is useful in assessing how the degradation of nature and natural resources (which are finite) negatively impacts an institution. This portfolio-level assessment is carried out by identifying relevant sectors, assets, and geographies; relevant natural capital assets like forests and water; potential disruptions to nature; and sectors, assets, and geographies that are the most vulnerable.

- **Climate value at risk (VaR)** (third-party approach). This risk assessment applies the VaR framework to climate change impacts to an entity’s balance sheet.

Risk scenario analyses use forward-looking information in conjunction with historical data to assess the impact of financial and macroeconomic variables from climateeconomy models. Baseline scenarios are used as a comparison to scenarios reflecting different risks associated with climate change. Steps in scenario analysis include choosing the scenario, modeling economic impacts, and translating those impacts into financial risk drivers.

While macroeconomic stress testing focuses more on financial statements across a two-to-five-year time frame and is useful for estimating capital needs, climate-related scenario analysis focuses more on specific exposures over various time frames and can help modelers identify the impact to an entity’s risk profile and strategy.

The two scenario dimensions for climate-related financial risks are (1) the climate outcome (temperature increase) and (2) the type of transition (gradual or abrupt reductions in emissions and the availability of technology). Banks and supervisors can rely either on in-house developed models or externally generated scenarios coming from the Network for Greening the Financial System (NGFS) or the International Energy Agency (IEA). Drawbacks to these scenarios include the lack of detailed economic damages from physical risk drivers and the lack of extreme event impacts in the model.

Time horizon poses a challenge to models, as physical climate risks extend well beyond the typical capital planning and strategic planning horizons (of two to three years and

three to five years, respectively). Modeling uncertainty is a natural result of this longer time horizon and the necessity of making assumptions about a distant future.

Banks and supervisors must make assumptions about their balance sheets over a given time horizon. The balance sheet can either be static or dynamic. While static balance sheets help illustrate climate risk in current exposures, a static balance sheet becomes less reliable as the time horizon extends beyond current asset durations. Dynamic balance sheets are useful in assessing the impacts from possible strategy adjustments needed to reduce climate risk exposures.

###### **MODULE QUIZ 100.2**

1. In gathering the data needed for taking physical and transition risk drivers and translating them into economic risk factors, a bank will likely gather the information from each of the following sources except:

   - A. government agencies.

   - B. retail brokerage firms.

   - C. academic organizations.

   - D. commercial third parties.

2. Which of the following statements regarding broad risk management approaches is most accurate?

   - A. Stress testing is a form of scenario analysis.

   - B. Assigning climate risk ratings and scores is an external third-party approach.

   - C. The VaR framework cannot be applied to a climate-related financial risk model.

   - D. Sensitivity analysis is very useful when assessing the impact of policy changes on physical risk.

#### **MODULE 100.3: CLIMATE-MEASUREMENT STRENGTHS AND WEAKNESSES**

**LO 100.d: Compare and contrast climate-measuring methodologies utilized by banks, regulators, and third-party providers.**

At this point, banks are still in the early stages of developing models and methodologies to quantify and translate exposures into climate-related financial risks. Supervisors have focused more on transition risks and third parties (e.g., academics, rating agencies, think tanks) have also worked on methodologies to assess climate-related financial risks.

#### **Exposure Mapping and Measurement**

Bank-level methodologies can be categorized as follows:

- **Portfolio and sector exposures.** Identifying material risk transmission channels within a bank’s exposure profile is critical. Key indicators include physical vulnerability of collateral based on location, measures of carbon and emission intensity, and energy label or efficiency real estate distributions. Both qualitative and quantitative data is relevant.

- **Transition risks.** Banks must understand the impact to certain sectors of transitioning to a low-carbon economy. Sectors most at risk include utilities, transportation, oil and gas, construction, and metals and mining. Determining the carbon footprint of bank assets is also valuable in estimating transition risks, and this can be done at a full balance sheet level or just for sectors sensitive to transition risk. _Financed emissions_ related to lending and investment activities and _greenness_ of financial assets are also valuable measures of transition risk. _Alignment_ approaches measure the gap between current portfolios and portfolios consistent with a specific climate target. _Green activities_ (like renewable energy) and _brown activities_ (like coal and gas-fired power) are also areas for banks to analyze, with a goal of increasing the former and reducing the latter.

- **Physical risks.** Management of physical risk at the portfolio level will focus on geographic concentration of risk, hazard types, probability, and severity. Physical risk scores and geospatial mapping help banks assess where they are exposed to flood risks.

- **Client or project ratings and scores.** Banks are increasingly looking at transition and physical exposures to climate-related financial risk at the individual counterparty level. A bank will evaluate climate-related risks and opportunities for companies that are currently financed or may be financed in the future by banks. This analysis, which includes both qualitative and quantitative components, helps to inform the bank’s lending decision. Ratings and scoring mechanisms may be done inhouse or by external entities. Qualitative measures include assessing the client’s climate strategy, management capabilities in dealing with risks, and vulnerability areas. An example of a risk assessment is a traffic light approach, where _red_ indicates high exposure to climate risk and _green_ indicates low exposure. Traditional credit scores may be complemented with climate risk scores.

Supervisor-level methodologies use similar methodologies to the bank methodologies just described. High-level categories of transition and physical risk assessments are as follows:

- **Transition risk.** This is assessed and matched to bank exposures using ad hoc surveys or regulatory information. Corporate (non-real estate) portfolios are often assessed by supervisors using indicators related to carbon footprint, emission intensity, and sensitivity to climate policies. A sector-level view is taken to assess which sectors in the economy are most vulnerable to policy change. Real estate tends to have fewer analyses related to transition risk.

- **Physical risk.** Supervisors assess most relevant hazards in their jurisdictions and the specific regions within their jurisdiction that are most vulnerable. Publicly available information and climate risk scores from commercial vendors are helpful in this regard. Key indicators include flood maps, water stress levels from industrial facility locations, populations below specific elevations, agriculture percentage as a component of gross domestic product, and areas most vulnerable to climate change. Exposures to physical risk can be done at a counterparty, activity, sector, or country level depending on the granularity of available data. A supervisor will use the outputs

of these models to assess the percentage of the portfolio in higher risk areas and exposure to specific climate events.

#### **Risk Quantification**

Bank-level methodologies employ stress testing and scenario analysis to both inform strategic planning and to quantify climate-related financial risks. Scenario analysis for climate-related financial risks is used to understand the potential impact on specific portfolios, to assess limitations and benefits, and to refine methodologies. Transition risk analysis looks at impacts to credit parameters for counterparties in specific sectors. Physical risk analysis looks at household (mortgage) and corporate exposures, where a key exposure is for sectors that are highly sensitive to long-term weather pattern changes. Chronic physical risks are converted into productivity changes, while acute physical risks are viewed through the lens of extreme weather impacts on real estate collateral.

A series of outreach workshops in 2020 were conducted to gather data on how banks are managing climate-related financial risks. The following are the key takeaways from the workshops:

- Climate-related financial risks are still not fully understood, and there is only initial evidence that these risks are priced into financial markets.

- For transition risks, banks have focused on specific economic sectors. The most relevant sectors were the ones with high carbon footprints (utilities, energy/oil, transportation, gas, construction, etc.).

- For physical risks, banks have focused on economic sectors and geographic regions. The most relevant sectors here were utilities, agriculture, real estate, and retail mortgages.

- Key data sources include public data sources (company annual reports), external rating agency and commercial third-party data, internal client data, regular data collections, and ad hoc requests.

- Heatmaps have been helpful in monitoring and managing portfolio and sector climate-related financial risks.

- Banks are still in the piloting stages in terms of methodologies to quantify climaterelated risks, and there is still uncertainty regarding incorporating this information into existing risk management processes.

- Scenario analyses used to assess transition risks have focused on emission-intensive sectors, while analyses used to assess physical risks have focused on the impact of hazards like floods and water-related risks on individual portfolios or on groups of clients.

- Insurance coverage is critical to risk mitigation, although these coverages may change over time.

Supervisors can use stress tests and scenario analysis to inform macroprudential policies and for microprudential supervision. For macroprudential policies, the size and distribution of climate risks to the financial system must be assessed. For microprudential, business model vulnerability and the quantification of financial

exposures vulnerable to climate risk drivers are the focal points. Because of the relatively long time horizon and the nature of these risks, stress tests and climate scenario analysis tend to be more exploratory in nature. The two main objectives served by these analytical approaches are to help supervisors understand the impact of climate change on business and risk management strategies and to raise awareness and incentivize the development of appropriate risk models and the filling of data gaps.

Climate scenarios are translated into financial market and macroeconomic variables. Sector-level calibrations can help supervisors enhance granularity and differentiate risk profiles across sectors. Multicountry, multisector frameworks can also be deployed. Borrower-level analyses may be used to complement macro- and sector-level approaches.

The following are key takeaways from supervisors responding to a survey in 2020:

- Transition risk is a higher priority than physical risk.

- Supervisors are focused on banking system (macro) and individual bank (micro) dimensions.

- Mapping and measuring climate risk driver exposures are the primary methodologies deployed, with stress tests and scenario analyses also heavily used.

- Third-party scenarios and methodologies are the most used, with in-house methodologies planned for the future.

- The primary sources for data are public or borrower reports.

- A sectoral approach is the primary tool used to assess transition risk, with emissions or emission intensity as the primary indicator.

- An aggregate climate risk indicator is the most commonly used metric to assess physical risk, with primary drivers being storms, floods, and droughts. Most physical risks are assessed at the country and regional levels.

- Short time horizons are used for transition risk only, while long time horizons are used for both transition and physical risk. Time ranges are from 5 to 30 years.

Third-party approaches continue to be heavily used by banks and supervisors. Features of these methodologies include scenario selection, exposure mapping, transition and physical risk shocks, and impact assessments on financial performance. Sensitivity and vulnerability to climate events is included in these approaches via the identification of resources impacted by climate change and facility exposures. Models may also incorporate adaptive capacities, alternative production sources, and damage functions.
