modeling?

- A. Long time horizons for physical risks.

- B. Long time horizons for transition risks.

- C. Short time horizons for physical risks only.

- D. Short time horizons for transition risks only.

#### **MODULE 100.4: GAPS AND CHALLENGES IN MODEL FRAMEWORK DESIGN**

**LO 100.f: Assess gaps and challenges in designing a modeling framework to capture climate-related financial risk.**

As noted earlier, the methodologies used to measure climate-related financial risks are still in their infancy in terms of development. Future analytical development will focus on three areas: (1) data availability, (2) modeling complexity, and (3) conceptual challenges.

#### **Risk Differentiation**

Transition and physical risk classifications are established to group exposures among identification criteria that approximate and reflect these risks. Aggregate risk classifications, which can be made using publicly available information on vulnerable sectors and countries, are simple to determine and useful to banks and supervisors. However, the identification criteria used may not truly be specific or granular enough for counterparty differentiation. Adding ratings/scores as complements can help, although they may be hard to integrate into day-to-day counterparty risk management. Quality, data availability, and a lack of an established link to financial risk parameters continue to present modeling challenges.

A risk classification system should have “comparability” across banks’ exposures. However, the desire to compare and aggregate bank exposures must be balanced with the desired level of risk differentiation and complexity or granularity needed. The obstacles toward developing comparability include the following:

- **Jurisdiction comparability.** A baseline set of common standards is needed for the methods used to assess risk drivers like physical and transition risks, as well as the metrics used to evaluate the risks. Developing useful, cross-jurisdictional standards is a challenge.

- **Aggregation perspectives.** Determining the risk profile of large banks is challenging, as financial services and bank lending provided to corporations that are active across multiple jurisdictions is difficult to model given the differences unique to each jurisdiction. Consistent bank decisions in line with group-wide policy may conflict with climate risks unique to location.

The biggest obstacle to climate risk measurement process development is data and information availability. Specific limitations include the following:

- Data for transition and physical risk drivers can lie outside the ranges for traditional financial data. The data may be incomplete, lack sufficient granularity, or not be

updated with appropriate frequency.

- While third-party (external) providers try to fill data gaps and assign ratings/scores to transition and physical risks, end users have limited insight into the data scrubbing process or the accuracy of the data itself. Comparability and standardization among providers are also challenges for input reconciliations.

- Some of the data gaps may be filled by counterparties providing proprietary, nonpublic information to banks as part of the lending process. Smaller counterparties may provide more qualitative data than quantitative, which presents challenges. Also, banks may not be able to easily update data after underwriting and onboarding processes have concluded. Public information (both quality and quantity) is often tied to company size, making it difficult to compare counterparties. Common accounting standards and principles related to climate data are nonexistent.

- Supervisors can augment the data gathered by banks via supervisory reports, which provide standardized and recurring data. However, supervisory reporting may not be as granular as needed to truly assess transition and physical risk.

#### **Climate-Related Financial Risk Complexity**

Ideally, a modeling framework will allow banks and supervisors to translate climate risk drivers into financial risk parameters in a prospective manner. Three big challenge areas for developing this framework are (1) fully capturing economic effects from climate scenarios, (2) managing long-term horizon assessments, and (3) operational assessment challenges.

- **Capturing economic effects.** Because climate-related financial risks are complex, banks and supervisors have a difficult time incorporating scenarios into existing risk processes and structures. There is significant uncertainty regarding climate risk drivers and the nonlinearity of the climate system. A robust analysis must consider many alternative scenarios and different modeling approaches using the same scenario. Existing models typically fall short of estimating the full impact of climate change, as chronic physical risks like temperature increases can be modeled but extreme weather events or future disruptive changes are not usually captured. In addition, damage functions from physical risks are typically not granular enough to be fully captured. Historical observations tend to be either missing or not good predictors of the future, which means empirical risk parameters cannot be derived by financial models. There are work-arounds to this challenge, but they require gathering complete data and necessary assumptions regarding the relationship of scenario-related variables and the financial impact to counterparties.

- **Long-term horizon assessments.** Climate-related financial risks require extending time horizons out far longer than typical modeling—in some cases, it is up to 30 years. Reliably assessing risk outcomes is difficult because economic and financial projections become more uncertain the further out the horizon extends. Observed (actual) results are more likely to differ from estimations as time horizons extend. Future efforts should be geared toward addressing increased uncertainty about future portfolio composition.

- **Operational assessment challenges.** Both banks and supervisors must continue to assess climate-related risks by building operational capabilities. The quality of information technology systems and the ability to manage significant quantities of data will drive the bank’s ability to assess its overall exposure to climate risks. Climate-related financial risk measurement is interdisciplinary and will require significant investments in both in-house functional expertise and outsourced climatespecific expertise.

#### **Key Takeaways**

The following is a list of the key overall takeaways from this report on climate-related financial risks:

- Much of the work done by banks and supervisors to map and measure climaterelated risk exposures has focused on transition risk over physical risk.

- Remaining gaps in creating models include climate-relevant risk data from counterparties, disclosure quality, and data consistency across jurisdictions and portfolios.

- As many banks and supervisors have limited in-house expertise, there is a market for third-party provider services and data.

- Areas of focus have been more on credit risk and market risk than on liquidity risk and operational risk.

- Climate-related metrics are used by banks to communicate their strategies on climate change to their stakeholders.

- Translating climate change scenarios into financial risks remains a challenge. Climate and financial risk are often difficult to model together, exacerbated by uncertainty regarding transition and physical risk projections across very lengthy time horizons. Insurance mechanisms may be in place to mitigate climate risks, although protection gaps and potential scope changes may limit future coverage.

- Supervisors for internationally active banks face an even greater challenge to be consistent across jurisdictions.

- For measurement, risk classification is a key issue. Classification approaches, to date, have been geared toward disclosure and stakeholder communication. Classifications embedded within risk metrics have also presented comparison challenges. Globally consistent climate risk classifications and sufficient risk mapping are goals.

- Data gaps are also a key measurement concern. Although data collection is expensive, banks and supervisors need accurate and comprehensive data to understand and model counterparty vulnerability, sector and region vulnerability, and real economic and financial system impacts of climate change to translate climate risk into financial risk.

- For methodologies, investments are needed to develop models that can measure forward-looking climate-related risks. Models that can accommodate longer time horizons are important.

##### **KEY CONCEPTS**

###### **LO 100.a**

Banks and supervisors should consider the following concepts when mapping exposures and measuring financial risks:

- Transition and physical risk measurement: Transition risks result from the shifting of a high-carbon to a low-carbon economy. Physical risk drivers or hazards are the specific hazards that impact future financial cash flows.

- Exposure granularity: Banks and supervisors must find a balance between the granularity needed and the associated complexity and cost.

- Top-down and bottom-up approaches: The former measures risk at an aggregated (general) level and then breaks it down into its component parts. The latter measures risks at a component level and aggregates them up to a consolidated view.

- Incorporating risk mitigation and risk reduction: While financial risks may be mitigated through government regulations and measures, banks and supervisors can take proactive measures as well. Gross exposure is a measure of climate risk impacts without the incorporation of mitigation strategies. Net exposure is climate risk impacts after accounting for proactive offsetting strategies, such as derivatives hedging and insurance.

- Heterogeneities: Because of the uniqueness of each individual bank’s portfolio and the exposures each client and counterparty faces (which differ due to factors such as geography, technology, political environment, sector, location, etc.), heterogeneities impact the selection of measurement approaches.

- Sources of uncertainty: Assumptions must be made regarding interactions between the climate, human activity, economic activity, and government activity, which can lead to underestimating risk.

###### **LO 100.b**

Three data categories useful for assessing climate-related financial risk are as follows:

- Data related to physical and transition risk drivers translated into economic risk factors

- Data linking climate-adjusted economic risk factors to exposures

- Financial exposure data used to translate economic risks into financial risks

Climate risk classifications are helpful in ranking exposures based on their vulnerability to and associated financial risk from climate-related economic risk factors. In creating a climate risk classification scheme, geographic location is important as it is a primary driver of physical risk differentiation. For transition risk, jurisdiction plays the most important role, as laws, regulations, energy grids, and markets drive an individual or entity’s ability to manage this risk.

###### **LO 100.c**

Examples of the types of climate-related financial risk estimation models include the following:

- Integrated assessment models (IAMs)

- Sensitivity analysis

- Input-output models

- Computable general equilibrium (CGE) models

- Dynamic stochastic general equilibrium (DSGE) models

- Overlapping generation (OLG) models

- Agent-based models (ABMs)

- Stress testing

Banks and supervisors are currently using the following broad risk measurement approaches:

- Climate risk ratings/scores

- Scenario analysis

- Stress testing

- Sensitivity analysis

- Natural capital analysis (third-party approach)

- Climate value at risk (third-party approach)

Risk scenario analyses use forward-looking information in conjunction with historical data to assess the impact of financial and macroeconomic variables from climateeconomy models.

The two scenario dimensions for climate-related financial risks are (1) the climate outcome (temperature increase) and (2) the type of transition (gradual or abrupt reductions in emissions and the availability of technology).

Time horizon poses a challenge to models, as physical climate risks extend well beyond the typical capital planning and strategic planning horizons.

Banks and supervisors must make assumptions about their balance sheets (which can either be static or dynamic) over a given time horizon.

###### **LO 100.d**

Bank-level methodologies can be categorized as follows:

- Portfolio and sector exposures

- Transition risks

- Physical risks

- Client or project ratings and scores

Bank-level methodologies employ stress testing and scenario analysis to both inform strategic planning and to quantify climate-related financial risks. Supervisor-level methodologies use similar methodologies to the bank methodologies and are categorized based on managing transition risk versus physical risk.

Key takeaways from the 2020 workshops involving banks include the following:

- Climate-related financial risks are still not fully understood.

- For transition risks, banks have focused on specific economic sectors with high carbon footprints (utilities, energy/oil, transportation, gas, construction, etc.).

- For physical risks, banks have focused on economic sectors and geographic regions. The most relevant sectors here were utilities, agriculture, real estate, and retail mortgages.

- Key data sources include public data sources (company annual reports), external rating agency and commercial third-party data, internal client data, regular data collections, and ad hoc requests.

- Heatmaps have been helpful in monitoring and managing portfolio and sector climate-related financial risks.

- Banks are still in the piloting stages in terms of methodologies to quantify climaterelated risks, and there is still uncertainty regarding incorporating this information into existing risk management processes.

- Scenario analyses used to assess transition risks have focused on emission-intensive sectors, while analyses used to assess physical risks have focused on the impact of hazards like floods and water-related risks on individual portfolios or on groups of clients.

- Insurance coverage is critical to risk mitigation, although these coverages may change over time.

Key takeaways from supervisors responding to a survey in 2020 include the following:

- Transition risk is a higher priority than physical risk.

- Most supervisors are focused on banking system (macro) and individual bank (micro) dimensions.

- Mapping and measuring climate risk driver exposures are the primary methodologies deployed, with stress tests and scenario analyses also heavily used.

- Third-party scenarios and methodologies are the most used, with in-house methodologies planned for the future.

- The primary sources for data are public or borrower reports.

- A sectoral approach is the primary tool used to assess transition risk, with emissions or emission intensity as the primary indicator.

- An aggregate climate risk indicator is the most commonly used metric to assess physical risk, with primary drivers being storms, floods, and droughts. Most physical risks are assessed at the country and regional levels.

- Short time horizons are used for transition risk only, while long time horizons are used for both transition and physical risk. Time ranges are from 5 to 30 years.

Third-party approaches continue to be heavily used by banks and supervisors.

###### **LO 100.e**

Much of the climate-related financial risk modeling is in its infancy in terms of development, and the longer time horizons make estimations more difficult. However, banks and supervisors are learning how to translate these model outputs into financial risks and to incorporate the outputs into overall risk assessment and strategic planning.

###### **LO 100.f**

Future analytical development will focus on three areas: (1) data availability, (2) modeling complexity, and (3) conceptual challenges.

A risk classification system should have “comparability” across banks’ exposures. The desire to compare and aggregate bank exposures must be balanced with the desired level of risk differentiation and complexity or granularity needed. The obstacles toward developing comparability include jurisdiction comparability and aggregation perspectives.

The biggest obstacle to climate risk measurement process development is data and information availability. Specific limitations include incomplete and inconsistent data, the opaqueness of externally provided data, differences in data stemming from counterparty size, and a lack of granularity in supervisory reports.

Three big challenge areas for developing a comprehensive modeling framework are (1) fully capturing economic effects from climate scenarios, (2) managing long-term horizon assessments, and (3) operational assessment challenges.

The following is a review of the Current Issues in Financial Markets principles designed to address the learning objectives set forth by GARP<sup>®</sup> . Cross-reference to GARP assigned reading—Basel Committee on Banking Supervision.

### **READING 101**

**PRINCIPLES FOR THE EFFECTIVE MANAGEMENT AND SUPERVISION OF CLIMATE-RELATED FINANCIAL RISKS**

###### **Study Session 15**

#### **EXAM FOCUS**

The effects of climate change could include physical and transition risks that negatively impact the stability and continuity of all banks (regardless of size) as well as the overall financial system. This reading outlines 12 broad principles on managing climate risks followed by 6 broad principles pertaining to supervisors.<sup>1</sup> This material is general in nature—so, for the exam, focus on understanding the principles described.

#### **MODULE 101.1: GOVERNANCE, CONTROL, CAPITAL, LIQUIDITY, AND RISK MANAGEMENT PRINCIPLES**

**LO 101.a: Describe the principles for managing climate-related financial risks related to corporate governance and internal control framework.**

#### **Corporate Governance**

There are three distinct principles to consider pertaining to corporate governance.

_Principle 1: Banks should develop and implement a sound process for understanding and assessing the potential impacts of climate-related risk drivers on their businesses and on the environments in which they operate. Banks should consider material climate-related financial risks that could materialize over various time horizons and incorporate these risks into their overall business strategies and risk management frameworks._

Key physical and transition risks must be included in formulating business plans, including an assessment of their potential effects on the robustness of the bank’s business model over various time periods as well as effects on meeting the bank’s goals.

This requires a determination of how climate risks will affect a bank’s response to changes in the economy, the financial system, and the competitive environment.

The entire assessment should include the board and senior management in the stages where they are needed; the assessment methodology adopted by the board must be thoroughly articulated to the nonsenior bank management and staff. The impact of climate risks could be extended to account for possible amendments to employee remuneration within the bank.

Internal risk management policies must be congruent with statements made externally by the bank regarding initiatives and objectives pertaining to climate risk.

_Principle 2: The board and senior management should clearly assign climate-related responsibilities to members and/or committees and exercise effective oversight of climate-related financial risks. Further, the board and senior management should identify responsibilities for climate-related risk management throughout the organizational structure._

Clearly assigning management responsibilities allows key climate risks to be accounted for within the bank. Furthermore, it is necessary to ascertain that the various groups within the bank have the needed knowledge and tools to properly perform their duties in managing climate risks.

The board and senior management must possess sufficient knowledge of climate risks, and senior management must be able to handle such risks. On an as-needed basis, consultation with external specialists and technical training for board members and senior management on climate risks may be desirable.

_Principle 3: Banks should adopt appropriate policies, procedures, and controls that are implemented across the entire organization to ensure effective management of climaterelated financial risks._

Policies and procedures throughout all areas of the bank (e.g., new client acceptance, audits of material transactions) should account for climate risk management.

#### **Internal Control Framework**

_Principle 4: Banks should incorporate climate-related financial risks into their internal control frameworks across the three lines of defense to ensure sound, comprehensive, and effective identification, measurement, and mitigation of material climate-related financial risks._

The first line of defense involves risk assessments whereby employees must have the technical knowledge and experience to properly spot climate-related risks when accepting new clients, reviewing credit applications and renewals, and setting up new products.

The second line of defense involves the bank’s risk department, and it should operate its risk analysis and review processes separate from the first line. For example, this may involve questioning the analysis performed by the first line.

The third line of defense involves the bank’s internal audit department, and it takes a high-level view of the internal control process, together with a review of the first and second lines. Additionally, the third line considers data quality.

**LO 101.b: Describe the principles for managing climate-related financial risks related to capital and liquidity adequacy and the risk management process.**

#### **Capital and Liquidity Adequacy**

_Principle 5: Banks should identify and quantify climate-related financial risks and incorporate those assessed as material over relevant time horizons into their internal capital and liquidity adequacy assessment processes, including their stress testing programs where appropriate._

There should be analysis of solvency (e.g., debt-to-equity ratio, interest coverage ratio) in the context of potential and significant negative climate events during the longerterm capital planning period. Specifically, the concern here is the potential reduction in equity.

A parallel analysis should occur for impacts on liquidity in the short term arising from climate risks in both normal and stressed situations. Liquidity risk, here, refers to significant negative net cash outflows and/or significant deterioration in the current or quick ratio, for example.

To clarify the term _incorporate_ , it would mean including such risks in the firm’s business model, business strategy, and stress testing plans. Stress testing accounts for circumstances that are extreme but still possible for the firm to encounter.

Climate risks are likely to be incorporated gradually and, in that regard, firms must be able to determine the key climate risks that could significantly impair their balance sheets. In addition, firms should identify the relevant risk indicators and metrics to help with quantification of exposures. Finally, it is important to be able to determine any important connections between climate risks and regular financial risks.

#### **Risk Management Process**

_Principle 6: Banks should identify, monitor, and manage all climate-related financial risks that could materially impact their financial condition, including their capital resources and liquidity positions. Banks should ensure that their risk appetite and risk management frameworks consider all material climate-related financial risks to which they are exposed and establish a reliable approach to identifying, measuring, monitoring, and managing those risks._

Significant climate risks must be clearly described and accounted for in the risk appetite framework.

A thorough analysis of climate risks should be performed on a periodic basis. Defining materiality in terms of overall risk management within the firm is important, especially to ensure that all material risks are addressed. Concentration risk is the most crucial to consider regarding sectors, industry, geography, and counterparties. Climate

risks, like all key risks, need to be identifiable through risk factors to ensure that they can be subsequently mitigated.

Risk management tools should be employed such as setting exposure limits to market, credit, liquidity, and operational risk.

Climate risks are constantly changing, and there may be new ways for such risks to enter the mainstream financial system. This means that banks must attempt to comprehend such risks and their evolution so as to minimize any negative impacts on other key financial risks.

###### **MODULE QUIZ 101.1**

1. Within the internal control framework, the third line of defense most likely covers which of the following areas?

   - A. Internal audit and data quality only.

   - B. Internal audit and risk assessments only.

   - C. Risk assessments and data quality only.

   - D. Risk assessments, internal audit, and data quality.

2. Which of the following types of material risk is most directly associated with climaterelated financial risk?

   - A. Concentration risk.

   - B. Counterparty risk.

   - C. Default risk.

   - D. Liquidity risk.

#### **MODULE 101.2: COMPREHENSIVE MANAGEMENT AND SUPERVISION PRINCIPLES**

**LO 101.c: Describe the principles for the management of climate-related financial risks related to management, monitoring, and reporting, comprehensive management of credit risk and other risks, and scenario analysis.**

#### **Management Monitoring and Reporting**

_Principle 7: Risk data aggregation capabilities and internal risk reporting practices should account for climate-related financial risks. Banks should seek to ensure that their internal reporting systems are capable of monitoring material climate-related financial risks and producing timely information to ensure effective board and senior management decision-making._

When accumulating data across the firm, the process should have controls in place to account for climate risks to spot the exact risks, to spot any concentration of such risks, and to spot any new risks. Underlying all of this is to have controls in place to produce data that is correct and usable. The bank may need to improve its existing IT systems to allow for the proper accumulation, processing, and centralization of the data to allow for the proper evaluation of key climate risks.

Taking a further step, banks might involve some of their clients or counterparties by gathering data from them regarding their transition strategies. Sometimes, it is not possible to obtain relevant data on climate risk—and, in those instances, it may be necessary to use approximations and best assumptions to derive _temporary_ data.

Reporting of climate risks must occur periodically and be done without delay; therefore, the choice of what constitutes _periodic_ must be carefully considered.

Metrics should be used that are both numerical and nonnumerical in nature to allow climate risks to be properly managed. The bank should fully and publicly disclose any restrictions it may encounter in being able to sufficiently analyze climate risk data.

#### **Comprehensive Management of Credit Risk**

_Principle 8: Banks should understand the impact of climate-related risk drivers on their credit risk profiles and ensure that credit risk management systems and processes consider material climate-related financial risks._

Credit policies and procedures should be thorough and meticulously defined to allow proper assessment and management of significant climate risks and their effects on the bank’s credit portfolio. In the context of lending activities, banks should ensure that there is sufficient assessment of potential climate risks for all borrowers before granting credit as well as continuous review of such risks during the credit term.

When identifying climate concentration risks, tools such as heatmaps could be used to measure and illustrate any geographic pockets of risk.

When managing climate risks, tools such as tightening underwriting standards, shorterterm loans, lower lending amounts, and greater collateral requirements could be used. In addition, banks could explicitly limit their exposures regarding industries, sectors, and geography if exposures in specific areas are not deemed desirable.

#### **Comprehensive Management of Market, Liquidity, Operational, and Other Risks**

There are three principles related to the comprehensive management of market, liquidity, operational, and other risks.

_Principle 9: Banks should understand the impact of climate-related risk drivers on their market risk positions and ensure that market risk management systems and processes consider material climate-related financial risks._

When looking at the securities in their own asset portfolios, banks must determine the impact of climate risks on portfolio value. Also, they must assess the impact of losses and higher standard deviation of returns on portfolio value and devise methods to minimize the impacts.

_Shock scenario analysis_ might be an effective approach to model the effect of climate risks on the bank’s portfolio value. One way to do so could be to shock the liquidity of assets that have climate risk exposure and make differing assumptions on the timeliness and ability to offset those exposures. In addition, hedges may become very

costly or impossible to obtain depending on the nature and severity of any climate events.

_Principle 10: Banks should understand the impact of climate-related risk drivers on their liquidity risk profiles and ensure that liquidity risk management systems and processes consider material climate-related financial risks._

Banks must consider how climate risks will affect their cash outflows in the form of borrowers drawing more from their credit lines or depositors withdrawing their funds suddenly, for example. In addition, banks should consider the effects on the value of the underlying assets in their liquidity buffers.

_Principle 11: Banks should understand the impact of climate-related risk drivers on their operational risk and ensure that risk management systems and processes consider material climate-related risks. Banks should also understand the impact of climaterelated risk drivers on other risks and put in place adequate measures to account for these risks where material. This includes climate-related risk drivers that might lead to increasing strategic, reputational, and regulatory compliance risk, as well as liability costs associated with climate-sensitive investments and businesses._

Climate risks need to be considered in terms of their effect on overall business operations in normal times and on business continuity during potential material climate events. The effects of any material climate events should also be considered in the context of other business risks such as strategic, reputational, compliance, and general liability.

#### **Scenario Analysis**

_Principle 12: Where appropriate, banks should make use of scenario analysis to assess the resilience of their business models and strategies to a range of plausible climaterelated pathways and determine the impact of climate-related risk drivers on their overall risk profile. These analyses should consider physical and transition risks as drivers of credit, market, operational, and liquidity risks over a range of relevant time horizons._

Scenario analysis could consider any or all of the following goals:

- Determining the effects of climate change and a move to low-carbon usage on the bank’s business operations and operational resiliency

- Determining climate risk factors that apply most to the bank

- Quantifying exposures and expected losses from climate risks

- Determining any restrictions regarding data and analysis of climate risks

- Formulating methods of managing climate risk

Scenario analysis should focus only on the pertinent risks that apply to the bank’s operations. It should include a wide enough breadth of potential and realistic situations and account for pros and cons as well as document the underlying assumptions in each of the situations. The scope of scenario analyses of climate risks should be commensurate with the bank’s size and operational complexity.

Scenario analysis should cover short- and long-term periods. Shorter terms could cover specific climate risks that are more likely to materialize in the future and could be quantified with greater certainty. Longer terms could cover more general climate risks that are much less certain to quantify, but such analysis could still assess the bank’s ability to adapt to long-term economic changes or adapt to changing climate risks.

It is important to ensure that any scenario analysis does not remain static given the constant and frequent changes expected in climate science. Therefore, all scenarios must be revised for changes and updated to reflect current circumstances on an ongoing basis.

**LO 101.d: Describe the principles for the supervision of climate-related financial risks related to prudential regulatory and supervisory requirements for banks and responsibilities, powers, and functions of supervisors.**

#### **Prudential Regulatory and Supervisory Requirements for Banks**

There are three principles related to prudential regulatory and supervisory requirements for banks.

_Principle 13: Supervisors should determine that banks’ incorporation of material climate-related financial risks into their business strategies, corporate governance, and internal control frameworks is sound and comprehensive._

All roles and duties pertaining to climate risks should be transparent, sufficient, thoroughly documented, and delegated to appropriate individuals.

In being able to properly oversee the bank’s climate risks, the board and senior management must be able to receive relevant and reliable internal information on significant climate risks.

Supervisors must maintain regular and adequate lines of communication with the board and senior management to ensure management has a plan to deal with climate risks in a proactive manner with an eye to the future.

Supervisors should ensure that banks look at the effects of climate risks in their business models and strategies, including resiliency in both the short term and long term. In the context of climate risk, supervisors need to be in a position to question the underlying assumptions used in establishing business strategies.

Additionally, climate risks must be included in corporate governance and internal controls. This would include having relevant regulations in place in all three lines of defense as well as having periodic audits of the bank’s general internal control process.

_Principle 14: Supervisors should determine that banks can adequately identify, monitor, and manage all material climate-related financial risks as part of their assessments of banks’ risk appetite and risk management frameworks._

Supervisors should check the scope and frequency of the bank’s determination of the materiality of climate risks. In addition, they should check for methods proposed to

##### minimize such risks.

Supervisors should examine the bank’s risk management process to ensure that they include the significant climate risk exposures. Also, banks need to determine whether their data processing and reporting abilities are sufficient to allow for the effective determination of current and future risks and prompt execution of various risk mitigation measures.

_Principle 15: Supervisors should determine the extent to which banks regularly identify and assess the impact of climate-related risk drivers on their risk profile and ensure that material climate-related financial risks are adequately considered in their management of credit, market, liquidity, operational, and other types of risk. Supervisors should determine that, where appropriate, banks apply climate scenario analysis._

Banks should consider multiple methods of climate risk mitigation. Any such risks deemed to be significant should be included when computing internal capital and liquidity sufficiency for a given time period.

As stated earlier, a process of scenario analysis should exist that is commensurate to the size and operational complexity of the bank. Supervisors should be in a position to question any underlying assumptions and processes behind the scenario analyses performed.

#### **Responsibilities, Power, and Functions of Supervisors**

There are three principles related to responsibilities, powers, and functions of supervisors.

_Principle 16: In conducting supervisory assessments of banks’ management of climaterelated financial risks, supervisors should utilize an appropriate range of techniques and tools and adopt adequate follow-up measures in case of material misalignment with supervisory expectations._

Requirements should be established, accounting for the scope and operational complexity of the bank. International cooperation between banking groups is strongly encouraged in the form of exchanging information on climate risk and making use of existing information from other groups to create efficiencies and to avoid duplication of work.

_Principle 17: Supervisors should ensure that they have adequate resources and capacity to effectively assess banks’ management of climate-related financial risks._

Supervisors must always be cognizant of their current abilities and what will be required in the future based on industry trends. This will involve setting aside time regularly to enhance their knowledge and skills. For tasks delegated to external parties, supervisors must still possess sufficient knowledge to interpret the work performed by the external parties.

A wide and varied group of stakeholders possessing complementary knowledge and experience should be assembled to measure and analyze climate risks.

Supervisors could use publicly available information from past years to determine the significance of climate risks. Should there be any deficiencies in the data, supervisors could then turn to banks and request their internal information in an attempt to obtain complete information.

_Principle 18: Supervisors should consider using climate-related risk scenario analysis to identify relevant risk factors, size portfolio exposures, identify data gaps, and inform the adequacy of risk management approaches. Supervisors may also consider the use of climate-related stress testing to evaluate a firm’s financial position under severe but plausible scenarios. Where appropriate, supervisors should consider disclosing the findings of these exercises._

Note that scenario analysis and stress testing, specifically, are targeted toward larger and international banks, for example—and these tools will not necessarily be used for smaller local banks.

Supervisors should state their goals for climate scenario analysis, some of which may include the following:

- Determining the effects of climate change and a move to low-carbon usage on the bank’s business operations and operational resiliency

- Determining climate risk factors that apply most to the bank or overall financial system

- Promoting information exchange between banking entities; determining any existing deficiencies regarding data and analysis of climate risks

- Determining the sufficiency of risk management systems in banks as well as their abilities to manage climate risk

Stress testing might be employed to examine the sufficiency of the balance sheet in an extreme, but possible, situation.

As stated earlier, scenario analysis should cover short- and long-term periods. Shorter terms could cover specific climate risks that are more likely to materialize in the future and could be quantified with greater certainty. Longer terms could cover more general climate risks that are much less certain to quantify, but such analysis could still assess the bank’s ability to adapt to long-term economic changes or adapt to changing climate risks.

Supervisors possess enough knowledge and skill to supervise the scenario analyses. As mentioned before, a wide and varied group of stakeholders possessing complementary knowledge and experience should be assembled to create scenarios that allow for a complete range of analysis of climate risks. In addition, supervisors must be up to date in their knowledge of current issues in scenario analysis.

Because scenario analysis is constantly changing, supervisors must always be aware that such analysis may not always be relevant in all circumstances or may have become obsolete with time. This will have a significant impact on interpretation and reporting of the analysis. Bank supervisors are encouraged to interact with other supervisors and

other banks to stay abreast of and acquire more in-depth knowledge of climate risks affecting banks—and how to develop methods to manage such risks.

In reporting the results of scenario analysis, care must be taken in view of the lack of certainty that is the nature of scenario analysis. For example, the reporting might occur at a higher level with less specific detail on underlying assumptions and methods of analysis given the lack of certainty.

As stated earlier, international cooperation between banking groups is strongly encouraged in the form of exchanging information on climate risk and making use of existing information from other groups to create efficiencies and avoid duplication of work. Exchanging best practices as well as the results of scenario analyses and using consistent scenarios are highly encouraged.

###### **MODULE QUIZ 101.2**

1. Which of the following actions by a bank is least likely to mitigate material climaterelated credit risk?

   - A. Targeting specific clients.

   - B. Engaging in long-term lending.

   - C. Using discounted asset valuations.

   - D. Lowering the debt-equity ratio covenant.

2. Which of the following statements regarding the role of a supervisor in climate-related risk management is most accurate?

   - A. Supervisors should ensure that climate-related risk assessments are done on an internal basis.

   - B. Supervisors should focus on engaging quantitative specialists to design robust and accurate scenario analyses.

   - C. Supervisors should focus more on stress testing and scenario analysis if they are responsible for a larger bank versus a smaller bank.

   - D. Supervisors should use sensitivity analysis to specifically assess the sufficiency of a bank’s financial position under a severe but possible situation.

##### **KEY CONCEPTS**

###### **LO 101.a**

Banks should develop and implement a sound process for understanding and assessing the potential impacts of climate-related risk drivers on their businesses and on the environments in which they operate. Banks should consider material climate-related financial risks that could materialize over various time horizons and incorporate these risks into their overall business strategies and risk management frameworks.

The board and senior management should clearly assign climate-related responsibilities to members and/or committees and exercise effective oversight of climate-related financial risks. Further, the board and senior management should identify responsibilities for climate-related risk management throughout the organizational structure.

Banks should adopt appropriate policies, procedures, and controls that are implemented across the entire organization to ensure effective management of climate-

##### related financial risks.

Banks should incorporate climate-related financial risks into their internal control frameworks across the three lines of defense to ensure sound, comprehensive, and effective identification, measurement, and mitigation of material climate-related financial risks.

###### **LO 101.b**

Banks should identify and quantify climate-related financial risks and incorporate those assessed as material over relevant time horizons into their internal capital and liquidity adequacy assessment processes, including their stress testing programs where appropriate.

Banks should identify, monitor, and manage all climate-related financial risks that could materially impact their financial condition, including their capital resources and liquidity positions. Banks should ensure that their risk appetite and risk management frameworks consider all material climate-related financial risks to which they are exposed and establish a reliable approach to identifying, measuring, monitoring, and managing those risks.

###### **LO 101.c**

Risk data aggregation capabilities and internal risk reporting practices should account for climate-related financial risks. Banks should seek to ensure that their internal reporting systems are capable of monitoring material climate-related financial risks and producing timely information to ensure effective board and senior management decision-making.

Banks should understand the impact of climate-related risk drivers on their credit risk profiles and ensure that credit risk management systems and processes consider material climate-related financial risks.

Banks should understand the impact of climate-related risk drivers on their market risk positions and ensure that market risk management systems and processes consider material climate-related financial risks.

Banks should understand the impact of climate-related risk drivers on their liquidity risk profiles and ensure that liquidity risk management systems and processes consider material climate-related financial risks.

Banks should understand the impact of climate-related risk drivers on their operational risk and ensure that risk management systems and processes consider material climate-related risks. Banks should also understand the impact of climate-related risk drivers on other risks and put in place adequate measures to account for these risks where material. This includes climate-related risk drivers that might lead to increasing strategic, reputational, and regulatory compliance risk, as well as liability costs associated with climate-sensitive investments and businesses.

Where appropriate, banks should make use of scenario analysis to assess the resilience of their business models and strategies to a range of plausible climate-related pathways and determine the impact of climate-related risk drivers on their overall risk profile.

These analyses should consider physical and transition risks as drivers of credit, market, operational, and liquidity risks over a range of relevant time horizons.

###### **LO 101.d**

Supervisors should determine that banks’ incorporation of material climate-related financial risks into their business strategies, corporate governance, and internal control frameworks is sound and comprehensive.

Supervisors should determine that banks can adequately identify, monitor, and manage all material climate-related financial risks as part of their assessments of banks’ risk appetite and risk management frameworks.

Supervisors should determine the extent to which banks regularly identify and assess the impact of climate-related risk drivers on their risk profile and ensure that material climate-related financial risks are adequately considered in their management of credit, market, liquidity, operational, and other types of risk. Supervisors should determine that, where appropriate, banks apply climate scenario analysis.

In conducting supervisory assessments of banks’ management of climate-related financial risks, supervisors should utilize an appropriate range of techniques and tools and adopt adequate follow-up measures in case of material misalignment with supervisory expectations.

Supervisors should ensure that they have adequate resources and capacity to effectively assess banks’ management of climate-related financial risks.

Supervisors should consider using climate-related risk scenario analysis to identify relevant risk factors, size portfolio exposures, identify data gaps, and inform the adequacy of risk management approaches. Supervisors may also consider the use of climate-related stress testing to evaluate a firm’s financial position under severe but plausible scenarios. Where appropriate, supervisors should consider disclosing the findings of these exercises.

##### **ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES**

##### **Module Quiz 101.1**

1. **D** The third line of defense involves the bank’s internal audit department, and it takes a high-level view of the internal control process, together with a review of the first and second lines (risk assessments). Additionally, the third line considers data quality. (LO 101.a)

2. **A** Concentration risk is particularly specific to climate-related financial risk. With concentrations, the risk could be related to industry, economic sectors, or geographic regions. The other risks, although relevant to climate effects, are not as strongly associated with climate-related financial risk. (LO 101.b)

##### **Module Quiz 101.2**

1. **B** Banks often resort to shorter-term loans to reduce their credit risk. Long-term loans would increase credit risk, all things being equal.

Targeting clients with higher credit ratings, for example, is a means of reducing credit risk. In valuing collateral using a steeper discount rate, the lower asset valuations (likely comfortably below market or net realizable value) for a given loan principal amount provide some additional protection to the bank as the market or realizable value will likely exceed the outstanding loan balance by a comfortable margin. This would decrease the bank’s credit risk. A debt-equity covenant represents a maximum amount of debt permitted by the bank. Lowering the ratio is, therefore, a tightening of credit standards and would decrease the bank’s credit risk. (LO 101.c)

2. **C** Stress testing and scenario analysis are more targeted toward larger banks but some use of those tools may be useful even for smaller banks.

Some aspects of climate-related risk assessments may be specialist in nature; therefore, they would need to be outsourced externally. Supervisors should collaborate with a broad and diverse set of stakeholders, including climate scientists. Supervisors should use stress testing (not sensitivity analysis) to assess the sufficiency of a bank’s financial position under a severe, but possible, situation. (LO 101.d)
