The following is a review of the Current Issues in Financial Markets principles designed to address the learning objectives set forth by GARP<sup>®</sup> . Cross-reference to GARP assigned reading—Basel Committee on Banking Supervision.

### **READING 102**

## **THE CRYPTO ECOSYSTEM: KEY ELEMENTS AND RISKS**

###### **Study Session 15**

#### **EXAM FOCUS**

The crypto ecosystem is a complex and ever-evolving network of digital technologies and applications. Key elements include cryptocurrencies, stablecoins, smart contracts, and decentralized finance (DeFi). For the exam, be familiar with these main crypto market characteristics. Also, know the structural flaws inherent in the crypto ecosystem, including congestion and fragmentation, the need for a stablecoin anchor, and the false claims of decentralization. Finally, be able to identify the risks that crypto poses to different parties, such as investors, governments, regulators, and financial institutions.

#### **MODULE 102.1: KEY ELEMENTS OF THE CRYPTO ECOSYSTEM**

##### **LO 102.a: Describe the key elements of the crypto ecosystem, including unbacked crypto, stablecoins, smart contracts, and DeFi services.**

The crypto ecosystem is built on a foundation of blockchain technology, which is a methodology that provides a secure and decentralized way to record transactions and store data. A **blockchain** is essentially a digital ledger that keeps track of transactions in an open yet unalterable manner. An interconnected network of computers stores the information, and (unlike traditional databases, which are centralized) every participant has a copy of the same ledger. When any new transaction is transmitted to the network, numerous validators (called “miners”) compete using complex algorithms to verify the transaction. A successful miner is compensated with a small amount of cryptocurrency.

By broadcasting all information publicly, the crypto ecosystem ensures that every transaction is in agreement with the history of transactions on the blockchain; for example, that a piece of digital currency actually belongs to the seller, and that the cryptocurrency has not been spent previously. Once a transaction has been confirmed,

it is placed in the ledger as a “block,” and cannot be changed without the network’s approval. The actual names of the transacting parties (i.e., the crypto wallet owners) are kept private. However, the history of every transaction is publicly viewable; thus, blockchain transactions are referred to as pseudo-anonymous.

Key elements of the crypto ecosystem include (1) unbacked crypto, (2) stablecoins, (3) smart contracts, and (4) decentralized finance (DeFi).

**Unbacked crypto** refers to the original variety of cryptocurrency, first created in 2009 with Bitcoin. Unbacked crypto is not supported by any other assets, such as precious metals or fiat currency. Rather, the value of unbacked crypto is based on supply and demand. Well-known examples of unbacked cryptocurrency include Bitcoin and Ethereum.

Unbacked cryptocurrencies offer numerous advantages, such as decentralization, security, and transparency. However, unbacked crypto is sometimes criticized for its lack of intrinsic value as well as its volatility. These cryptocurrencies are subject to extreme price volatility (i.e., market risk), which makes them inappropriate as a medium of exchange. As a result, stablecoins have been developed as a means of allowing cryptocurrencies to potentially serve as payment instruments.

**Stablecoins** are a relatively new kind of cryptocurrency that are pegged to an independent asset, such as a fiat currency, (commonly the U.S. dollar [USD]), or occasionally to an asset such as gold, or even to other cryptoassets. This pegging feature is intended to make stablecoins more liquid and less volatile than unbacked cryptocurrencies. The underlying assumption is that a user can exchange a stablecoin relatively quickly for USD (or other asset) at par value. For this reason, stablecoins have become an essential medium of exchange in the crypto ecosystem. _Asset-backed stablecoins_ are supported by bonds or bank deposits and are managed by a centralized intermediary that invests the underlying collateral and manages coin redemptions. _Algorithmic stablecoins_ , on the other hand, are not backed by real-world assets, but use arbitrage with a volatile token to maintain their value. (The failed TerraUSD is an example of an algorithmic stablecoin.)

**Smart contracts** are pieces of self-executing code that are stored on a blockchain (used by Ethereum and other newer blockchains). Smart contracts trigger some predetermined task if specific conditions are met. These computer programs can be used to automate a range of financial agreements and will perform transactions directly with another party without the use of an intermediary.

In traditional finance, a collateralized loan would require the parties to agree on terms, transfer the collateral to a third party, and process the loan through multiple steps involving intermediaries. Smart contracts eliminate the need for intermediaries, by embedding the loan agreement into a program stored on the blockchain. The blockchain automatically executes the contract between the two parties. _Composability_ in smart contracts refers to the possibility of combining several smart contracts together to produce a more-sophisticated transaction.

**Decentralized finance (DeFi)** refers to financial services that are built on blockchain technology. DeFi services are intended to replicate conventional methods of managing

money, allowing users to borrow, lend, trade, and invest assets without the need for a central intermediary. DeFi services have the potential to be more open, accessible, transparent, and efficient than traditional financial services.

However, DeFi is still in its early stages of development, and faces some potential downsides. DeFi protocols are complex and can be vulnerable to hacks and other security vulnerabilities. Additionally, the DeFi market is very volatile given that the value of crypto assets can fluctuate wildly. Furthermore, there is no central authority to offer users recourse in the event of fraud or theft.

Though the crypto ecosystem is still in its initial stages of development, it is rapidly evolving and growing. Crypto is seen as having the potential to revolutionize the way we exchange value and interact with the financial system.

###### **MODULE QUIZ 102.1**

1. Which of the following statements about the key elements of the crypto ecosystem is least accurate?

   - A. Algorithmic stablecoins are backed by real-world assets.

   - B. The code underlying smart contracts is publicly available.

   - C. Smart contracts cannot directly access “off-chain” information.

   - D. Most DeFi transactions do not finance activity in the real economy.

2. Regarding the key elements of the crypto ecosystem, which of the following statements is most accurate?

   - A. Smart contracts can fetch real-world data for themselves.

   - B. Unbacked crypto is managed by a centralized intermediary.

   - C. Stablecoin values are susceptible to volatility and speculation.

   - D. DeFi services operate independently from any centralized authority.

#### **MODULE 102.2: STRUCTURAL FLAWS IN THE CRYPTO ECOSYSTEM**

**LO 102.b: Describe the structural flaws inherent in various elements of the crypto ecosystem.**

Because the crypto ecosystem is in its early stages of development, there are numerous inadequacies that still need to be addressed. These flaws include (1) congestion and fragmentation, (2) the need for a nominal anchor for stablecoins, and (3) the false claims of decentralization.

#### **Congestion**

Crypto **congestion** occurs when a cryptocurrency network is not processing transactions quickly enough to meet demand. During periods of congestion, fees for validators rise as a glut of users seek to have their transactions processed. This results in higher transaction fees, as users are forced to pay more to use congested blockchains.

The blockchain capacity problem is one result of the **scalability trilemma** , whereby blockchains can only achieve two of the following three properties:

1. _Security._ The ability to protect the network from attack.

2. _Decentralization._ The distribution of power not only in the hands of a few, but across the network.

3. _Scalability._ The ability to process many transactions per second.

#### **Fragmentation**

One result of a lack of scalability is that additional blockchains with greater capacity (such as the Terra blockchain) have been introduced. Essentially, with **fragmentation** , the cryptocurrency market divides into many different and often incompatible blockchain networks. These newer blockchains often feature a higher ability to process transactions, but weaker security and increased centralization. This has led to a proliferation of different blockchains and applications, each with its own unique set of rules and features.

Crypto fragmentation can lead to several problems, including the following:

- _Lack of interoperability._ Assets or data cannot be easily exchanged between blockchains.

- _Risk of hacks and theft._ Hackers may be able to exploit vulnerabilities in smaller and less secure blockchains.

- _Decreased network effects._ Users are less likely to use a blockchain if it is incompatible with other popular blockchains.

#### **Stablecoins Anchors**

As mentioned previously, stablecoins are cryptocurrencies that are pegged in value to some other asset, sometimes called a **nominal anchor** . This property is intended to make stablecoins less volatile than unbacked cryptocurrencies; and for this reason, stablecoins are considered in the crypto ecosystem to be a superior medium for transactions. However, the search for an appropriate nominal anchor can be challenging. Stablecoin issuers are incentivized to invest in risky assets in order to generate higher returns, which would increase demand for the stablecoins, and diversify their reserves. However, in order for the stabilization effects of a nominal anchor to be effective, the reserve assets must be transparent and consist of highquality assets.

#### **False Decentralization Claims**

Another structural flaw inherent to the crypto ecosystem is that decision-making power tends to be more concentrated than proponents of cryptocurrencies would have you believe. Advocates of cryptocurrencies and DeFi applications promote these items as decentralized. However, in practice, the nature of operational costs results in power being shared by just a small number of validators.

In a **proof-of-work (PoW)** consensus mechanism, potential validators compete with one another to solve cryptographic puzzles in order to be allowed to add transactions to the distributed ledger. The congestion resulting from PoW systems, which are decentralized platforms, leads to a concentration of power.

In newer **proof-of-stake (PoS)** blockchains, validators are required to post their coins as collateral in order to increase their chances of being chosen to validate the next block. This consensus mechanism reduces congestion; however, it too can lead to a concentration of power and coins among a small number of validators because operational costs are largely fixed.

Another factor that contributes to centralization is the issue of “contract incompleteness,” which refers to the impossibility of creating agreements that anticipate all possible future scenarios. Centralization can help to overcome the issue of contract incompleteness. An equivalent idea in the DeFi space is “algorithm incompleteness,” referring to the inability to write code that will account for all potential situations and outcomes. For this reason, DeFi platforms require some degree of centralized governance to address any gaps in their code. This aspect of centralization is typically represented by **governance tokens** , which are tokens issued as an incentive to platform developers and other insiders, who are then part-owners and can vote on proposals. A downside of validators becoming more centralized is that the risk of attacks and hacks increases.

Centralization is also exhibited in cryptocurrency trading, where investors primarily use **centralized exchanges (CEXs)** over **decentralized exchanges (DEXs)** . DEXs employ automated market-maker protocols to match transaction counterparties. Meanwhile, CEXs maintain off-chain records of outstanding orders placed by traders (analogous to limit order books in the world of traditional finance). One reason that CEXs attract more trading activity than DEXs is because of the lower costs for CEXs.

Crypto CEXs lack the regulatory oversight of traditional financial exchanges, despite replicating some of their functions. Not surprisingly, these exchanges have repeatedly demonstrated inadequate risk management controls and governance standards, sometimes resulting in the loss of users’ investments.

*[image omitted]*

###### **PROFESSOR’S NOTE**

Notable examples of failures of centralized exchanges include the November 2022 FTX bankruptcy, which led to billions in customer losses, as well as the 2014 Mt. Gox bitcoin exchange failure, which resulted in over $400 million in bitcoin losses.

False decentralization claims can also have a negative impact on the security of cryptocurrencies and DeFi applications. DeFi platforms tend to require some amount of central governance in order to deal with unforeseen eventualities. However, if a cryptocurrency or DeFi application is not truly decentralized, then it is more vulnerable to attacks and hacks.

#### **The Oracle Problem**

Smart contracts rely on _oracles_ to provide them with real-world data (such as the outcome of an event, or the price of an asset), because smart contracts cannot otherwise access information that resides “off-chain.” The **oracle problem** in decentralized finance refers to the challenge of providing accurate data to smart contracts (that may be used for lending, borrowing, and trading).

When an oracle is compromised or malicious, it can provide false data to smart contracts. If the self-executing code is deceived into making an incorrect decision based on inaccurate data, it can result in market manipulation, and losses for market participants. Because oracles are typically decentralized, there is little recourse should an oracle provide wrong information. The cost of errors and illegal actions related to oracles can be very high.

Technological efforts are being made to address the oracle problem and make DeFi systems more secure by making it more difficult for users to manipulate the oracle network.

#### **Addressing Crypto Ecosystem Flaws**

There are numerous initiatives to address the structural flaws mentioned in this section. For example, there are cross-chain bridges in development that should allow users to trade DeFi assets and cryptocurrencies across different blockchains. Also in the works are new kinds of stablecoins that are intended to be less volatile and more secure.

###### **MODULE QUIZ 102.2**

1. The scalability trilemma refers to the problem that permissionless blockchains can achieve only two of which of the following three properties?

   - A. Security, decentralization, and scalability.

   - B. Localization, robustness, and immutability.

   - C. Openness, representation, and auditability.

   - D. Monetization, centralization, and reliability.

2. Which of the following statements about stablecoins is most accurate?

   - A. Stablecoins require a central bank currency as a nominal anchor.

   - B. True to their name, stablecoins typically exhibit low price volatility and high liquidity.

   - C. Asset-backed stablecoins automatically rebalance the coin’s supply to maintain the peg value.

   - D. Stablecoins that peg their value to the U.S. dollar (USD) must be directly convertible into the USD.

#### **MODULE 102.3: RISKS POSED BY THE CRYPTO ECOSYSTEM**

**LO 102.c: Describe the risks crypto poses to parties including crypto investors, governments, regulators, and traditional financial institutions; and identify potential policy actions that can be taken to mitigate these risks.**

#### **Risks to Crypto Investors**

Media coverage and rising cryptocurrency prices in recent years have drawn many investors (particularly small retail investors) into the crypto market. However, the majority of crypto investors have not profited. In fact, large investors have generally benefited at the expense of small investors.

The highly volatile nature of cryptocurrencies makes these assets a risky bet for investors. Furthermore, because the crypto industry is fairly new and mostly unregulated, it has become a target for scammers and fraudsters (e.g., investors can lose their cryptocurrency if their wallet is hacked). Furthermore, market participants face the risk of loss if the exchange they are using collapses or is compromised. The bankruptcy of a crypto firm often means that investors will not be able to recover their funds, or that it may take a long time to do so.

#### **Risks to Governments**

The advent of crypto can pose new risks to governments, such as the risks arising from money laundering and terrorist financing. Cryptocurrencies are a natural fit for such nefarious undertakings, because these assets can be transferred anonymously. Governments are exploring measures to address these risks, including anti-money laundering efforts and schemes to combat the financing of terrorism. The use of crypto presents particular challenges for law enforcement agencies, because of the difficulty of tracking and tracing crypto transactions.

Cryptocurrencies may also lead to a loss of tax revenue for governments if they are used to avoid paying taxes. Cryptocurrencies may additionally pose a risk to governments in terms financial stability if crypto becomes widely adopted as a means of payment. This is because cryptocurrencies are decentralized and not subject to government control.

Crypto’s growth may furthermore pose a danger to monetary sovereignty. **Cryptoization** occurs when crypto currencies are used in place of local currencies. This is especially likely to happen in emerging markets; in a period of economic instability, holders of a country’s currency may decide to move into crypto, putting even more downward pressure on the domestic currency. Thus, cryptoization could lead to a loss of monetary sovereignty and have a negative impact on the country’s financial stability.

#### **Risks to Regulators**

Regulators have struggled to keep up with the rapidly evolving cryptocurrency industry, which has made it difficult to develop and implement effective regulations. While cryptocurrencies are global in nature, laws vary from country to country. These jurisdictional challenges will make it difficult for regulators to enforce rules and coordinate with one another.

As noted earlier, the anonymity of crypto lends itself well to being used for illicit activities such as tax evasion, money laundering, and the financing of terrorism. Such misuse of crypto presents vital challenges for law enforcement, who typically find it difficult to trace crypto transactions.

#### **Risks to Traditional Financial Institutions**

Up to this point in time, there has been relatively limited interaction between the real economy and the crypto market, and between traditional finance and the crypto

market. In fact, crypto has so far mostly been used as a tool for speculation. However, it is expected that linkages between crypto and traditional finance will become stronger going forward.

Traditional banks increase their connections to crypto when they provide credit to customers with crypto exposures, or when they provide crypto-related services to their clients. This effect could be observed in the latter half of 2022, when a plunge in crypto prices spilled over into the traditional banking sector, leading to the demise of at least two banks with significant customers in the crypto world. That outcome demonstrated how any bank with significant exposure to crypto can be impacted by crypto failures.

Another way that the interconnections between traditional finance and crypto could grow is through the **tokenization** of claims on real-world assets, such as real estate or stocks. Tokenization is the process of adding a digital representation of an asset (i.e., a “token”) to a programmable ledger. These tokens can include information such as ownership details. Tokenization could result in the development of a complicated web of interconnections between crypto and traditional finance, leading to a substantial increase in the systemic importance of the crypto ecosystem.

The continuing evolution of the regulatory landscape in many jurisdictions means that it can be difficult for institutions to operate in the crypto space, which can also create uncertainty for investors. In the long run, cryptocurrencies may pose a competitive threat to traditional financial institutions, because cryptocurrencies can offer a faster and cheaper way to transfer money.

#### **Potential Policy Actions to Mitigate Crypto Risks**

Governments around the world are still struggling with how best to deal with the risks introduced by cryptocurrencies. Some countries have pursued containment strategies. Others have opted for regulation, while a few countries have even banned crypto altogether.

_Containment_ refers to a policy intended to limit the connection between crypto and the traditional financial system. The idea of containment is to protect consumers from harm, while reducing the risk of crypto shocks to the financial system. Implementation could take numerous forms, including prohibiting banks from offering crypto services. This kind of containment can be effective, but can also stifle innovation, and make operating difficult for legitimate crypto businesses.

_Regulation_ means establishing rules and oversight for the crypto industry. Regulation could be accomplished through measures such as licensing crypto exchanges or requiring crypto businesses to adopt procedures that hinder criminal activity. To effectively manage the risks associated with cryptocurrencies, comprehensive governance, anti-money laundering (AML), and combating the financing of terrorism (CFT) measures are essential. Robust governance frameworks can ensure that market participants operate in a transparent and accountable manner, which should help to preserve the integrity of the financial system. The Financial Action Task Force (FATF) has provided guidance on registration and licensing requirements for service providers

in the cryptocurrency ecosystem. A downside of regulation is that it can be expensive and complex to implement.

_Bans_ involve prohibiting crypto entirely. This can be done through a variety of mechanisms, such as making it illegal to buy, sell, or trade crypto assets. The ultimate goal of a cryptocurrency ban is to eliminate the risks posed by crypto entirely. An unintended consequence of bans is that such prohibitions are likely to drive crypto activity into the underground economy.

If properly implemented, policy actions related to the crypto ecosystem could support private sector innovation, reduce the cost of transactions, increase financial inclusion, and improve the overall integrity of the financial system. Policy actions that could be taken to mitigate the risks that crypto poses include the following:

- _Develop and implement clear and comprehensive regulations._ Regulators and governments should develop detailed rules and laws for the cryptocurrency industry. Initiatives in this area are being coordinated by the Financial Stability Board (FSB), and steps are being taken to develop a consensus on appropriate regulatory actions.

- _Increase cooperation and coordination._ Governments and regulatory authorities should collaborate to develop a comprehensive framework for mitigating the jurisdictional issues associated with cryptocurrencies. There is already a proposed framework for the international regulation of crypto activities that is intended to promote consistency and comprehensiveness of regulation, supervision, and oversight of crypto activities and markets.

- _Encourage crypto innovation._ By encouraging positive innovation within the traditional financial system, the cost of payments should decrease, even as the quality of transactions increases. The FedNow instant payment service in the United States, which allows money to be sent and received instantly, is an example of such financial services innovation.

- _Develop central bank digital currencies (CBDCs)._ CBDCs are digital currencies issued by central banks. They could potentially offer a safer and more efficient way to transfer money.

###### **MODULE QUIZ 102.3**

1. Which of the following actions is least likely to lead to interconnectedness between the crypto market and traditional financial institutions?

   - A. Crypto exchanges partnering with banks.

   - B. Banks offering cryptocurrency-related services.

   - C. Asset managers launching cryptocurrency funds.

   - D. Retail investors speculating on cryptocurrency values.

2. The most appropriate policy action for managing the risks that crypto poses would be to:

   - A. tax cryptocurrency transactions at a high rate.

   - B. encourage the use of central bank digital currencies.

   - C. outlaw initial coin offerings and decentralized exchanges.

D. impose strict regulations on crypto exchanges and wallets.

##### **KEY CONCEPTS**

###### **LO 102.a**

Unbacked cryptocurrencies, such as Bitcoin and Ethereum, are digital assets that are not backed by physical assets nor by fiat currencies. Ownership of unbacked crypto assets is verified by decentralized validators, with transactions recorded on a public ledger.

Stablecoins are a kind of digital currency whose value is pegged to an independent asset, often USD. Stablecoins can be advantageous in that they provide a robust medium of exchange and can be traded relatively quickly for currency at par value.

Smart contracts are self-executing computer programs stored on a blockchain that help automate functions and reduce reliance on intermediaries. Multiple instructions can be combined within a single contract, thereby facilitating complex transactions. Smart contracts increase transparency and reduce the risk that transactions can be manipulated.

DeFi refers to decentralized financial services (such as trading, investing, borrowing, and lending) that are built on blockchain technology. DeFi has allowed for the introduction of innovative financial products and services and has helped to make these new products and services accessible to everyone.

###### **LO 102.b**

Because the crypto ecosystem is in its early stages of development, there are numerous structural flaws that still need to be addressed.

Congestion occurs when a cryptocurrency network is not processing transactions quickly enough to meet demand. This results in higher transaction fees for users and may lead to new competing blockchains being introduced—a process called fragmentation. Fragmentation increases crypto ecosystem complexity and decreases overall security. Also, the lack of interoperability can make it more difficult to trade across different blockchains.

A stablecoin’s “nominal anchor” should make it less volatile than unbacked cryptocurrencies. However, choosing an appropriate stablecoin anchor can be a challenge. Some common choices include: a fiat currency, gold, or a basket of goods and services. Each kind of nominal anchor has its own advantages and disadvantages; for example, fiat currencies can be volatile, while precious metals are not particularly liquid.

Cryptocurrencies and DeFi applications are generally perceived to be decentralized. However, this can be the result of false decentralization claims—sometimes these entities are actually controlled by a small group of people. False decentralization can have a negative impact on the security of cryptocurrencies and DeFi applications, making them vulnerable to attacks, and exposing them to possible manipulation or fraud.

Smart contracts must rely on oracles to provide them with real-world data, because smart contracts cannot otherwise access information “off-chain.” “The oracle problem” in DeFi refers to the challenge of providing accurate data to smart contracts. A compromised or malicious oracle could provide false or inaccurate data to smart contracts, resulting in market manipulation, with high losses and little recourse for users.

###### **LO 102.c**

Risks crypto poses to investors include:

- Cryptocurrency prices are volatile and can fluctuate wildly, making them a risky investment.

- The crypto industry is relatively new and unregulated, which makes it a target for fraud and scams.

- Investors can lose their cryptocurrency to theft if their wallet is hacked or if the exchange is compromised.

Risks crypto poses to governments include:

- Cryptocurrencies can pose a risk to financial stability because they are decentralized and not subject to government control.

- Crypto assets are ideal for laundering money and financing terrorism because they are anonymous and difficult to track.

- Governments can lose revenues when cryptocurrencies are used to avoid paying taxes.

Risks crypto poses to regulators include:

- Regulators have struggled to develop and implement effective rules and laws that keep up with the rapidly evolving cryptocurrency industry.

- Cryptocurrencies are global in nature, so jurisdictional challenges make it difficult for countries to enforce regulations.

Risks crypto poses to traditional financial institutions include:

- Cryptocurrencies pose a competitive threat to traditional financial institutions because this technology offers a faster and cheaper way to move money.

- Financial institution instability is possible if banks take on significant exposure to volatile cryptocurrencies.

Potential policy actions to mitigate crypto risks include:

- Develop and implement clear and comprehensive regulations to protect investors and reduce the risks of fraud, scams, and hacking.

- Increase cooperation and coordination among governments and regulators to address cryptocurrency jurisdictional challenges.

- Educate investors about the risks of cryptocurrencies to help them to make informed decisions.

- Develop central bank digital currencies (CBDCs) to offer a safer and more efficient way to transfer money compared to current cryptocurrencies.

##### **ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES**

##### **Module Quiz 102.1**

1. **A** Algorithmic stablecoins (such as the failed TerraUSD stablecoin) maintain a peg using a paired volatile token; they are typically not backed by real-world assets. The other statements are accurate. Most DeFi activities involve only exchanging one crypto coin for another. (LO 102.a)

2. **C** Stablecoins are susceptible to the same market forces (such as volatility and speculation) that affect other cryptocurrencies. Asset-backed stablecoins (not unbacked crypto) are managed by a centralized intermediary that invests the underlying collateral and coordinates the coins’ redemption. Stablecoins are typically issued by centralized entities that are responsible for maintaining the peg of the stablecoin to the underlying asset. In order to import data into a blockchain that can be accessed by a smart contract, it is necessary to involve a third party (the oracle). (LO 102.a)

##### **Module Quiz 102.2**

1. **A** The scalability trilemma of a blockchain implies that permissionless blockchains can achieve some combination of scalability, security, and decentralization, but not all three together. Security is achieved through incentives and decentralization, but congestion and high fees, which are needed to provide incentives, limit scalability. (LO 102.b)

2. **B** Stablecoins aim to minimize volatility, and typically have relatively high levels of liquidity. Asset-backed stablecoins use an intermediary to manage and invest the collateral to maintain the peg value, while algorithmic stablecoins use a feedback loop to automatically rebalance supply to maintain the peg value. Stablecoins that peg their value to a currency like the USD can be convertible to another cryptoasset instead of the currency directly (e.g., the TerraUSD stablecoin was convertible to another token named Luna to maintain the 1:1 peg value with the USD). Stablecoins often peg their value to a conventional currency such as the USD, but they can also peg their value to other assets, including commodities or precious metals. (LO 102.b)

##### **Module Quiz 102.3**

1. **D** The interplay between the crypto market and traditional finance (as well as with the real economy) has remained limited so far, because for the most part, crypto has mainly been used for speculation. However, there are signs of growing interconnectedness between crypto and traditional financial institutions. For example, Coinbase has partnered with PayPal to allow users to buy and sell cryptocurrencies using their PayPal accounts, BlackRock launched a private

Bitcoin trust in 2021, and JPMorgan Chase now offers its clients access to Bitcoin and other cryptocurrencies. (LO 102.c)

2. **B** Encouraging the use of central bank digital currencies should reduce costs of payment, enhance financial inclusion, and bolster the integrity of the financial system. Outlawing certain types of crypto activity would be difficult to enforce, and it would stifle innovation in the crypto space. Imposing heavy regulations on crypto exchanges and wallets would make it difficult and expensive for people to buy and sell crypto and would discourage the adoption of crypto. Taxing crypto transactions at a high rate would make it less attractive as an investment and would discourage people from using it. (LO 102.c)

The following is a review of the Current Issues in Financial Markets principles designed to address the learning objectives set forth by GARP<sup>®</sup> . Cross-reference to GARP assigned reading—Martinez.

### **READING 103**

## **DIGITAL RESILIENCE AND FINANCIAL STABILITY: THE QUEST FOR POLICY TOOLS IN THE FINANCIAL SECTOR**

###### **Study Session 15**

#### **EXAM FOCUS**

Digital resilience is crucial for maintaining financial stability as financial institutions move toward an almost completely digital system. For the exam, understand the complexities involved with the extensive and concentrated use of cloud computing by financial institutions. There has been some progress with increasing cyber resilience regarding the mitigation of risks posed by third-party service providers. However, challenges in fundamental measurement principles and standardization still exist. Finally, understand the role of macroprudential measures such as circuit breakers and cooperation arrangements for dealing with cyber threats across the banking industry.

#### **MODULE 103.1: CYBER THREATS AND DIGITAL RESILIENCE**

**LO 103.a: Describe characteristics of cyber risks and information/communication technology (ICT) risks faced by financial institutions.**

#### **Cyber and ICT Risks**

**Cyber risk** refers to the loss of confidentiality, integrity, and availability (CIA) of data and data systems that result from a cyberattack. **Information and communications technology (ICT) risk** refers to disruptions pertaining to information and communication systems that may have similar effects as cyber risk—but, strictly speaking, do not stem from cyberattacks.

The overwhelming level of digitalization at financial institutions, combined with the essential nature of their services, places financial institutions in a vulnerable position regarding cyber and ICT risks. The financial sector is responsible for managing an immense amount of funds together with the expectation of providing uninterrupted transaction services and maintaining confidentiality. This suggests a greater risk of cyberattacks and ICT breakdowns.

Based on a general increase in serious cybersecurity incidents, some financial institutions have ranked cyber risk as more significant than market risk. To date, there have been major examples of confidentiality breaches of client information, theft of funds, and denial-of-service (DoS) attacks experienced by financial institutions worldwide. Reliable estimates of losses arising from cyber risks are generally not possible to compute due to a lack of data. As a result, the development of cyber insurance has been significantly hindered.

#### **Cloud Computing**

**Cloud computing** can be thought of as the provision of computer resources (e.g., storage, servers, software) through the internet (i.e., “the cloud”). The cloud operates on a shared system with multiple users, and its benefits include lower costs and greater operating flexibility. Cloud service providers are expected to have extremely robust controls in place. Financial institutions have strongly embraced cloud computing in recent years, which has resulted in a corresponding decrease in cyber risks; however, it has also led to an increase in ICT risks. The main issue is that the use of cloud computing moves some, but not all, of an organization’s cyber risks to cloud service providers because the organization’s providers and customers might not always have adequate controls.

The significant adoption of cloud computing by financial institutions has resulted in the integration of cyber and ICT risks to an extent that there could be systemic implications (discussed in the next section). Furthermore, cloud computing has needed to evolve (e.g., multiclouds) to deal with concentration risks, for example. The use of multiclouds limits the amount of damage that could occur from a cyberattack because the computing assets are spread out over multiple clouds, rather than just one. All major banks have adopted the use of multiclouds (compared to less than half for asset management companies and insurance companies). However, such evolution comes with unintended additional risks, such as weaker stewardship over the distributed computing processes.

ICT risks in the form of physical outages have become a key concern, with about 80% of cloud customers suffering at least one outage within the prior three-year period. During that same period, roughly 20% of those outages were considered significant enough to noticeably disrupt operations. Furthermore, climate change and rising energy costs may be causing longer and more frequent outages. In short, cloud computing use is high—despite its numerous and significant drawbacks.

**Interactions Between Cyber Risks and Financial Stability**

**LO 103.b: Assess the interactions between cyber and ICT risks and financial risks and explain how cyber and ICT risk events at financial institutions can lead to systemic financial risk.**

Cyber vulnerabilities that are triggered can cause negative financial impacts such as illiquidity, excessive leverage, and loss of customer trust. The most important consideration is how technology risks can lead to the realization of financial risks.

The modern-day design of cyber and ICT connections is highly integrated, which opens the potential for significant contagion effects. Financial contagion can occur extremely rapidly through the financial industry with the onset of just one key triggering event. The effects of such systemic risks on the **CIA triad** (i.e., confidentiality, integrity, and availability) are wide-ranging. For example, severity seems to be lower for data confidentiality, but higher for data integrity and data/systems availability for operational use. Although the quantification of systemic risks is important, it is also necessary to look at the qualitative effects of systemic risks in terms of their impact on the CIA triad.

Because contagion effects are nonlinear in nature, it is challenging to predict the probability of key events with reasonable accuracy. The solution is to use **agent-based models** , which are computer simulations of actions and interactions among agents. These models quantify the impacts of a cyber incident in terms of the CIA effects and how they affect the liquidity and asset values of the financial institutions in question. However, such models are still in their infancy stage, but their basic principles could be applied to developing cyber and ICT stress tests.

Agent-based models are useful for tracing the source of systemic risks, as what appears to happen in real life between technology and finance is not always completely accurate. For example, a cyber incident that causes a run on bank deposits may not materialize because the bank’s computer systems have been affected so as to not allow the actual withdrawals of funds to occur. In that regard, it would appear on the surface that technology was able to suppress the effects of the contagion—but this is clearly misleading because bank customers have other alternatives available, such as withdrawing funds they have on deposit with other banks.

Virtually all financial institutions are digitalized now, and they have become integrated and more reliant on each other due to cloud computing, networks, and shared software. Therefore, it is necessary for each participating institution to be reliable enough to quickly recover from a cyber incident to ensure the continuity of key functions in the general financial system. In that regard, given the importance of ICT functions in the financial system, each institution must be “tested” to ensure it is sufficiently reliable (which does not necessarily mean 100% reliability).

The ultimate result of cyber and ICT risks, combined with the vulnerabilities of financial institutions, is the overwhelming loss of confidence of customers. This

combination has been seen frequently in the past with runs on bank deposits stemming from customer panic or financial fundamentals. Building up liquidity buffers helps to mitigate the effects of bank runs caused by cyber shocks in theory, although history has shown that there has been a high level of liquidity in the financial system for a reasonable amount of time, thereby not requiring such buffers to be used. A more accurate assessment of the impact of a loss of confidence does require the use of agentbased models.

#### **Financial Stability Policies and Tools**

**LO 103.c: Describe potential macroprudential tools and policy measures that can be used to address cyber risks and ICT risks and explain challenges to the adoption of each one.**

**Digital resilience** covers both technological and financial aspects and involves microoriented tools and framework policies. **Micro-oriented tools** deal with general operational guidance, while **framework policies** deal with fundamental rules pertaining to network and system operations. Due to the significant threats to digital resilience in recent years, framework policies have seen major revisions to strengthen the defenses against new cyber and ICT risks.

#### **Policy Measures and Micro-Oriented Tools**

The **International Telecommunications Union (ITU)** covers a wide range of legal, technical, and other measures to promote cyber resilience. However, due to the complexity involved, it is necessary to have certification and standardization of solutions as a starting point for building cybersecurity defenses. With the many entities involved with cyber resilience, there needs to be a well-coordinated effort to maximize efficiency and minimize redundancy; therefore, the result has been the consolidation of numerous cybersecurity entities when dealing with cybersecurity issues on a national level.

Compared to past rules, recent rules pertaining to operational resilience are more forward-looking and principles-based approaches. This is necessary to evolve with the growing complexity of the cyber resilience world. However, a principles-based approach is more useful when working with the risk for a given entity as opposed to systemic risk, which involves multiple entities.

Policies have long been in place for incident reporting and information sharing in the European Union (EU)—but, in taking it a step further, the **European Systemic Risk Board (ESRB)** has suggested the formation of a European-wide structure for coordinating incident reporting.

Also in the EU, the **Digital Operational Resilience Act (DORA)** has provisions to respond to ICT third-party risks associated with cloud computing. In addition, it includes provisions dealing with penetration stress testing (i.e., cyberattack simulation), which is a useful exercise to keep entities conscious of security issues on an ongoing basis.

#### **Macroprudential Tools**

##### **_Circuit Breakers_**

**Circuit breakers** are essentially a temporary pause on normal rules and regulations, and they have been used more in the banking industry in recent years. In the context of major cyber incidents impacting the banking industry, it makes sense to have circuit breakers to minimize the negative impacts of customer loss of confidence and the resulting bank run on deposits. Therefore, circuit breakers supplement the controls already in place regarding bank deposits. Circuit breakers are analogous to a temporary stoppage to analyze the fallout from a major attack and to determine the upcoming steps for recovery.

The challenge, here, is for the bank to prove that a given cyberattack is sufficiently serious to be considered a _force majeure_ event that will provide some recovery time to the bank after the attack. It could be argued that proper cyber controls and other preventative measures could have been put in place to avoid the negative effects of a cyberattack; therefore, it is not a force majeure event. At the same time, a compelling argument for the use of circuit breakers is their ability to control and minimize the negative effects of a potential bank run after a significant cyberattack on a bank.

##### **_Cooperative Arrangements_**

Cooperation among members may seem like an obvious solution for small- and medium-sized organizations, but it has taken time to occur in the financial sector where most organizations are large. With the development of information and knowledge-sharing resources within the financial sector, there is evidence of growing cooperation among organizations to develop more robust defenses against cyberattacks.

Within the United States, there has been progress on unifying the various cybersecurity standards and in transitioning from tick-the-box-style compliance requirements to a judgment-based system. The challenge remains to bring the level of standardization and thorough reporting to an international level, although cooperation within the realm of incident reporting has been on the rise in Europe as well as internationally.

**ICT buffers** to combat systemic risk are a good example of cooperation and allow for a much quicker recovery time for those banks that have suffered a major cyberattack. ICT buffers have been used in the past in the United States with its tri-party repo system. In the United States, there is a collectively owned entity (Sheltered Harbor, LLC) whereby any co-owner bank that suffers a serious cyberattack could allow its customers to transact on their accounts at another co-owner bank. With consistent data formats and operating procedures for storage and restoration of data, the risk to a co-owner bank from a cyberattack is substantially reduced.

##### **_Structural Measures_**

The reach of macroprudential regulation has inevitably spread to systemically important technological institutions (SITIs), given the numerous externalities arising from SITIs as well as the significant dependence of the financial sector on SITIs.

Alternatives to macroprudential regulation would include more thorough evaluations of critical providers, strongly encouraging multicloud services, and using private clouds.

Additionally, regulators could engage in “forced diversification” of firms to incentivize them to use multiclouds or multiple types of software. Penalties for concentrated software use could be used as negative motivators; however, penalties also introduce the issue of enforceability.

###### **MODULE QUIZ 103.1**

1. Which of the following statements regarding cloud storage and cloud computing is most accurate?

   - A. All major banks have adopted the use of multiclouds.

   - B. The increased use of cloud computing in recent years by banks has resulted in decreases in both cyber and ICT risks.

   - C. The advance of cloud storage and computing has allowed ICT risks and cybersecurity risks to be treated independently for analytical purposes.

   - D. A cloud services audit firm has recently reported that about 50% of cloud customers have experienced a physical outage in the past three years.

2. Which of the following statements regarding the interaction between cyber risks and financial stability is most accurate?

   - A. Agent-based models are useful to trace the source of systemic risks.

   - B. The severity of shocks tends to be higher for data integrity compared to data confidentiality.

   - C. The most important consideration is how financial risks can lead to the realization of technology risks.

   - D. Shocks to the CIA triad can trigger financial sector vulnerabilities that are focused on a few main areas, including profitability.

3. The concept of a bank being able to recover very quickly from a serious cyberattack by allowing its customers to access their accounts from another bank is best described as which of the following macroprudential tools?

   - A. Circuit breaker.

   - B. Structural measures.

   - C. Forced diversification.

   - D. Cooperative arrangement.

4. Which of the following statements regarding the use of circuit breakers is most accurate? Circuit breakers:

   - A. have been used extensively in the banking industry in the past.

   - B. are used to deal with the effects of a loss of confidence by bank customers.

   - C. are an improvement; therefore, they are a valid substitute to the controls in place regarding bank deposits.

   - D. are a long-term pause on normal rules and regulations to allow a bank sufficient time to recover from a serious cyberattack.

**KEY CONCEPTS**

###### **LO 103.a**

Cyber risk refers to the loss of confidentiality, integrity, and availability (CIA) of data and data systems that result from a cyberattack. ICT risk refers to disruptions pertaining to information and communication systems that may have similar effects as cyber risk, but do not stem from cyberattacks.

Financial institutions have strongly embraced cloud computing in recent years, which has resulted in a corresponding decrease in cyber risks but also an increase in ICT risks. The main issue is that the use of cloud computing moves some, but not all, of an organization’s cyber risks to cloud service providers because the organization’s providers and customers might not have adequate controls.

The significant adoption of cloud computing by financial institutions has resulted in the integration of cyber and ICT risks to an extent that there could be systemic implications.

###### **LO 103.b**

Cyber vulnerabilities that are triggered can cause negative financial impacts such as illiquidity, excessive leverage, and loss of customer trust. The most important consideration is how technology risks can lead to the realization of financial risks.

The modern-day design of cyber and ICT connections is highly integrated, which opens the potential for significant contagion effects. Financial contagion can occur extremely fast through the financial industry with the onset of just one key triggering event.

Because contagion effects are nonlinear in nature, it is challenging to predict the probability of key events with reasonable accuracy. The solution is to consider agentbased models to quantify the impacts of a cyber incident in terms of the CIA effects and how they affect the liquidity and asset values of the financial institutions in question.

###### **LO 103.c**

Regarding policy measures and micro-oriented tools, it is necessary to have certification and standardization of solutions as a starting point for building cybersecurity defenses. Given the many entities involved with cyber resilience, the result has been the consolidation of numerous cybersecurity entities. Compared to past rules, recent rules pertaining to operational resilience are more forward-looking and principles-based approaches. Other policy measures deal with coordinated incident reporting and information sharing.

Regarding macroprudential tools, circuit breakers are essentially a temporary pause on normal rules and regulations. In the context of major cyber incidents impacting the banking industry, it makes sense to have circuit breakers to minimize the negative impacts of customer loss of confidence and the resulting bank run on deposits. Another macroprudential tool is cooperation, and one aspect may involve unifying the various cybersecurity standards and transitioning from tick-the-box-style compliance requirements to a judgment-based system. ICT buffers to combat systemic risk are a good example of cooperation and allow for a much quicker recovery time for those banks that have suffered a major cyberattack. Finally, the reach of macroprudential regulation has inevitably spread to systemically important technological institutions

(SITIs) given the numerous externalities arising from SITIs as well as the significant dependence of the financial sector on SITIs.

##### **ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES**

##### **Module Quiz 103.1**

1. **A** Within the financial sector, all major banks have adopted multicloud computing. With more banks embracing cloud computing in recent years, it has resulted in a corresponding decrease in cyber risks but also an _increase_ in ICT risks. The advance of cloud storage and computing has resulted in a _commingling_ of ICT and cybersecurity risks with potential systemic impact. A cloud services audit firm has recently reported that about _80%_ (not 50%) of cloud customers have experienced a physical outage in the past three years. (LO 103.a)

2. **A** Agent-based models are useful for tracing the source of systemic risks, as what appears to happen in real life between technology and finance is not always completely accurate. However, such models are still in their infancy stage, but their basic principles could be applied to developing cyber and ICT stress tests.

There tends to be a _lower_ severity of shocks to data confidentiality and a higher severity to data integrity and availability of data and systems. The most important consideration is how _technology risks_ can lead to the realization of _financial risks_ . Shocks to the CIA triad can trigger financial sector vulnerabilities that are focused on a few main areas such as _liquidity, leverage_ , and _trust_ . (LO 103.b)

3. **D** In the United States, there is a collectively owned entity (Sheltered Harbor, LLC) whereby any co-owner bank who suffers a serious cyberattack could allow its customers to transact on their accounts at another co-owner bank. With consistent data formats and operating procedures for storage and restoration of data, the risk to a co-owner bank from a cyberattack is substantially reduced. This is a good example of a cooperative arrangement. (LO 103.c)

4. **B** In the context of major cyber incidents impacting the banking industry, it makes sense to have circuit breakers to minimize the negative impacts of customer loss of confidence and the resulting bank run on deposits.

Circuit breakers have not been commonly used in the banking industry until recent years. Circuit breakers are a _complement_ to the controls in place regarding bank deposits. Circuit breakers are a _temporary_ (and, therefore, likely short-term) pause on normal rules and regulations to allow a bank sufficient time to recover from a serious cyberattack. (LO 103.c)

# **APPENDIX**

#### **USING THE CUMULATIVE** **_Z_ -TABLE**

#### **Probability Example**

Assume that the annual earnings per share (EPS) for a large sample of firms is normally distributed with a mean of $5.00 and a standard deviation of $1.50. What is the approximate probability of an observed EPS value falling between $3.00 and $7.25?

*[image omitted]*

_For_ z- _value of 1.50:_ Use the row headed 1.5 and the column headed 0 to find the value 0.9332. This represents the area under the curve to the left of the critical value 1.50.

_For_ z- _value of –1.33:_ Use the row headed 1.3 and the column headed 3 to find the value 0.9082. This represents the area under the curve to the left of the critical value +1.33. The area to the left of –1.33 is 1 − 0.9082 = 0.0918.

The area between these critical values is 0.9332 − 0.0918 = 0.8414, or 84.14%.

#### **Hypothesis Testing—One-Tailed Test Example**

A sample of a stock’s returns on 36 nonconsecutive days results in a mean return of 2.0%. Assume the population standard deviation is 20.0%. Can we say with 95% confidence that the mean return is greater than 0%?

*[image omitted]*

The significance level = 1.0 - 0.95 = 0.05, or 5%.

Since this is a one-tailed test with an alpha of 0.05, we need to find the value 0.95 in the cumulative _z_ -table. The closest value is 0.9505, with a corresponding critical _z_ -value of 1.65. Since the test statistic is less than the critical value, we fail to reject H0.

#### **Hypothesis Testing—Two-Tailed Test Example**

Using the previous assumptions, suppose that the analyst now wants to determine with 99% confidence that the stock’s return is not equal to 0.0%.

*[image omitted]*

Since this is a two-tailed test with an alpha of 0.01, there is a 0.005 rejection region in both tails. Thus, we need to find the value 0.995 (1.0 − 0.005) in the table. The closest value is 0.9951, which corresponds to a critical _z_ -value of 2.58. Since the test statistic is

less than the critical value, we fail to reject H0 and conclude that the stock’s return equals 0.0%.
